Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
10 min
Marriage Voided by Ownership of Spouse
Ruling on marriage voided by ownership of spouse

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why ownership of a spouse (milk al-yamin) voids the marriage contract and determine when a subsequent divorce takes effect.

Core Idea
Right-hand ownership supersedes marriage ownership, dissolving the marriage automatically.

This ruling addresses a unique conflict: when either spouse acquires ownership over the other, the marriage contract is immediately voided. The reasoning draws on the hierarchy of ownership types—milk al-yamin (right-hand ownership) is stronger than milk al-nikah (ownership of marriage). We will also examine an important intra-Hanafi dispute: whether a divorce pronounced after such ownership has any effect, and why the iddah remains obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ownership scenarios
Two cases: (1) the husband owns his wife (e.g., buys her); (2) the wife owns her husband (e.g., she inherits him). Also consider partial ownership via a share.
The text covers both full and partial ownership ('shiqs').
2
State the ruling: marriage contract becomes void
The text says 'batal al-aqd' (the contract becomes void) upon such ownership. This applies to both scenarios by consensus of the Shaykhayn (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf).
3
Understand the reasoning based on strength of ownership
Milk al-yamin is a stronger form of ownership. Since marriage itself is a form of ownership (milk al-nikah), the two cannot coexist when one is stronger. The weaker is superseded.
An exception is a mukatib (slave under contract) who has only a right to ownership, not full ownership, so marriage continues.
4
Analyze the effect of a subsequent divorce
If the husband buys his wife and then divorces her, the divorce does not occur because the marriage was already voided by ownership. Despite this, iddah is still obligatory by consensus, because separation occurred via ownership.
Iddah is required to confirm the womb is empty, even if divorce itself is invalid.
5
Note the dispute when wife owns husband
According to the two shaykhs, divorce does not occur. Muhammad al-Shaybani holds it does occur because the contract subsists in some respect. The preferred (mufta bihi) position is that of the two shaykhs.
The text provides a rebuttal to Muhammad's distinction.

(عيني، مستخلص، فتح) فلو اشتراها وطلقها لم يقع شيء دخل بها أو لم يدخل، وتجب العدة اتفاقاً؛ لأنه قد وقعت الفرقة بينهما بملك الرقبة، والطلاق يستدعي قيام ملك النكاح من وجه و لم يوجد، وإذا ملكت المرأة الزوج كله أو بعضاً منه ثم طلقها أنه فكذلك لا يقع الطلاق عند الشيخين، وعن محمد لأن العدة واجبة هنا اتفاقاً، وقيام العقد من وجه يكفي لوقوع الطلاق عليها، بخلاف ما إذا ملكها هو؛ لأنه لا عدة عليها هناك حتى حل وطؤها، قلنا: العدة واجبة هناك حتى لا يجوز له أن يزوجها من غيره حتى تنقضي عدتها، ولو أعتقها ظهرت العدة، وإنما لم تظهر بالنسبة إليه لحل وطئها له بملك اليمين، فتبين أن هذا الفرق غير صحيح (رمز الحقائق: ٢٤٠/١] (فتح) أنت طالق: أي لو قال لامرأته وهي أمة غيره: أنت طالق إلخ. (ط) فأعتق: أي المولى الأمة طلقت ثنتين. (ع) له الرجعة: [ أي للزوج الرجعة؛ لأنه طلق وهي حرة والحرة لا تحرم بالطلقتين حرمة غليظة. (ع)] أي إذا قال لامرأته وهي أمة لغيره: أنت طالق ثنتين مع عتق مولاك، فأعتقها مولاها ملك الزوج الرجعة؛ لأن معنى قوله: "مع عتق مولاك" مع إعتاق مولاك؛ إذ العتق قد يستعمل متعديا، ثم لما كان إعتاق المولى شرطا للتطليق فلا بد أن يكون مقدماً عليه؛ لأن الشرط يكون مقدما على المشروط، فالعتق يكون مقدماً على وقوع الطلاق، فيقع الطلاق حال كونها حرة، -أيضاً يقع؛ كتاب الطلاق ٤١٢ إضافة الطلاق إلى الزمان أنت ولو تعلّق عتقها وطلقتاها بمجيء الغد فجاء الغد لا، وعدتها ثلاث حيض، في الصورتين بالإجماع للاحتياط طالق هكذا، وأشار بثلاث أصابع فهي ثلاث، أنت طالق بائن أو البتة = الأمة تعلقت طلقتاها أيضاً أي إشارته هذه فيصير طلاقها ثلاثا، فيملك الرجعة، فإن قيل: إن كلمة "مع" للقران فينبغي وقوع الطلاق، والعتق معاً فكيف هذا؟ قلنا: قد جاء كلمة "مع" للتأخير كما في قوله تعالى: ﴿إِنَّ مَعَ الْعُسْرِ يُسْرا (الشرح:٦) أي بعد العسر يسراً؛ لأن العسر مع اليسر أمران متضادان لا يجتمعان فكذا هنا يصير للتأخير. (عيني، مستخلص) الغد بأن قال لها مولاها: إذا جاء غد فأنت حرة، أو قال لها زوجها : إذا جاء غد فأنت طالق ثنتين. (ع) فجاء الغد لا أي عتقت الأمة، وطلقت مغلظة و لم تحل للزوج حتى تنكح زوجا غيره، ولا يملك الزوج الرجعة عند الشيخين، وعند محمد له الرجعة؛ لأن العتق والطلاق لما تعلقا بشرط واحد وهو مجيء الغد، والعتق أسرع وقوعا؛ لكونه رجوعا إلى الحالة الأصلية، وهو أمر مستحسن مندوب إليه، شرعا، بخلاف الطلاق؛ فإنه أبغض المباحات، وجب أن تطلق زمن نزول الحرية فيصادفها الطلاق وهي حرة فلا تحرم بالتطليقتين حرمة غليظة كما في المسألة الأولى، ولهما: أنه علق طلاقها بما علق به المولى عتقها ثم العتق يصادفها وهي أمة فكذا الطلاق فتحرم بتطليقتين حرمة غليظة؛ لأن زمان ثبوت العتق هو زمان ثبوت الطلاق ضرورة تعلقهما بشرط واحد، بخلاف المسألة الأولى؛ لأن فيها علق التطليق بإعتاق المولى، فيقع بعد العتق. (مستخلص، فتح) وعدتها ثلاث حيض أي بالاتفاق احتياطاً، ولو كان الزوج مريضا لا ترث منه؛ لأنه حين تكلم بالطلاق لم تكن لها أهلية الإرث وقت التكلم، فلم يوجد شرط إرثها منه وهو قصد الفرار، أما إذا وجد تعليق الزوج الطلاق في مرضه بعد علمه بحصول تعليق المولى حريتها على مجيء الغد ترث منه عند محمد. (فتح) فهي ثلاث [ أي ثلاث طلقات ولو أشار بأصابعه ولم يقل هكذا فهي واحدة (ط)] لأن الإشارة بالأصابع تفيد العلم بالعدد عرفاً وشرعاً إذا اقترنت بالاسم المبهم، قال عليه : الشهر هكذا وهكذا وهكذا" أشار بأصابعه العشرة ثلاثين يوماً، ثم قال الشهر هكذا وهكذا وهكذا"، وخنس إبهامه في يعني الثالثة تسعة وعشرين يوماً، والإشارة يعني تقع بالمنشورة منها دون المضمومة؛ لأنه عليها لما خنس إبهامه المرة الثالثة فهم منه تسعة وعشرون يوما، ولو اعتبرت المضمومة لكان المفهوم أحدا وعشرين يوما، فالمعتبر المنشور مطلقا للعرف وللسنة. ولو أشار بالواحدة طلقت واحدة، ولو أشار بالثنتين طلقت ثنتين، ولو أشار بثلاث أصابع وقال: عنيت الإشارة بالمضمومتين صدق ديانة لا قضاء، وكذا لو نوى الإشارة بالكف ولو قال: أنت طالق" وأشار بأصابعه ولم يقل: "هكذا فهي واحدة؛ لأن الإشارة تفسير للمبهم من العدد، ولم يوجد فلغت الإشارة ويبقى، قوله: "أنت طالق" وهو لا يحتمل العدد. [تبيين الحقائق: ٦٨/٣] (عيني، فتح طالق بائن أو البتة انتصابها على المصدرية من بت أمره إذا قطع فيه وجزم. (ع)] شروع في بيان وقوع البائن بوصف الطلاق بما ينبئ عن الشدة والزيادة، والبت مصدر بت أمره إذا قطع به، ولو نوى بطالق واحدة وبنحو بائن أخرى يقع ثنتان بائنتان. (فتح)
Book of Divorce or 611 Addition of Divorce to Time: With my death or with your death is void. And if he owns her or a share of her, or she owns him or a share of him, the marriage contract is void. That is, the woman owned the husband. So if he buys her and divorces her, it does not occur. You are divorced twice upon the emancipation of your master, so if he emancipates, he has the right to take her back. That is, the husband bought his wife. And the meaning of master is the slave-owner. And if the occurrence were by description, the mention of three would be useless. So it is established that the actual occurrence is only the described entity that is omitted. The phrase 'you are divorced one' means one divorce. So if the actual occurrence is the entity described by the number, and doubt enters into the occurrence, it does not occur by doubt. The second: if he said to her: You are divorced upon my death. The third: he said to her: You are divorced upon your death. These two are nothing by agreement, because he added divorce to a state contrary to divorce; because his death negates the capacity for divorce, and her death negates the capability. And each of capacity and capability is necessary for the permissibility of the act. (Mukhtasar) If she owns her husband, and if he owns her: i.e., if the husband owns his wife, as if she was a slave and he bought her, inherited her, etc. (Ayni) Or a share of her: i.e., he owns a part of her, such as buying half or inheriting half or third. (Ayni) The contract becomes void. As for the husband owning her, because establishing ownership over a free woman was for the need of preserving progeny, and the ownership of marriage was for necessity, and it has been removed by the existence of right-hand ownership. And as for her owning him, because of the combination of being an owner and owned. It cannot be said that permissibility is not established by owning part, because we say: right-hand ownership is evidence of permissibility, so it stands in place of permissibility for facilitation. The conclusion: the combination of being an owner and owned prevents the initiation of marriage, and likewise its continuation. And if the husband owns his wife, because right-hand ownership is stronger, so the weaker, i.e., the ownership of marriage, does not compete with it; rather it is dissolved by its existence. If you say: a mukatib who buys his wife does not nullify the marriage, I say: he does not have ownership but rather a right to ownership, and that does not prevent the continuation of marriage. (Ayni, Mukhtasar, Fath) So if he buys her and divorces her, nothing occurs, whether he has consummated or not, and the waiting period is obligatory by agreement, because separation has occurred between them by ownership of the neck, and divorce requires the existence of marriage ownership in some respect, and it did not exist. And if the woman owns the husband entirely or partially, then she divorces him, similarly divorce does not occur according to the two shaykhs. And from Muhammad: because the waiting period is obligatory here by agreement, and the existence of the contract in some respect is sufficient for divorce to occur upon her, unlike if he owns her, because there is no waiting period on her until her intercourse is permissible. We say: the waiting period is obligatory there, so that he may not marry her to another until her waiting period ends. And if he emancipates her, the waiting period appears, but it did not appear with respect to him because of the permissibility of intercourse on his part by right-hand ownership. So it becomes clear that this distinction is not correct. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/240, Fath) 'You are divorced': i.e., if he says to his wife who is a slave of another: You are divorced, etc. (T) So if he emancipates: i.e., the master emancipates the slave, she is divorced twice. (Ayni) He has the right to take her back (Ayni) i.e., the husband has the right to take her back because he divorced her while she was free, and a free woman is not prohibited by two divorces with severe prohibition. (Ayni) That is, if he says to his wife who is a slave of another: 'You are divorced twice upon the emancipation of your master', and her master emancipates her, the husband retains the right to take her back. Because the meaning of his words 'upon the emancipation of your master' is 'with the emancipation of your master', since emancipation may be used transitively. Then, since the master's emancipation is a condition for the divorce, it must be prior to it, because the condition is prior to the conditioned. So the emancipation is prior to the occurrence of divorce, so the divorce occurs while she is free. - also it occurs. Book of Divorce 412 Addition of Divorce to Time: 'You are divorced' and if her emancipation and her two divorces are tied to the coming of tomorrow, then tomorrow comes, no, and her waiting period is three menstrual cycles in both cases, by consensus for precaution. 'You are divorced like this' and he pointed with three fingers, so it is three. 'You are divorced irrevocably or absolutely' = the slave woman, her two divorces are also tied. That is, his pointing; so her divorce becomes three, so he retains the right to take her back. If it is said: the word 'with' indicates conjunction, so divorce and emancipation should occur together, so how is this? We say: the word 'with' has come to indicate delay, as in the verse of the Most High: 'Indeed, with hardship comes ease' (94:6), meaning after hardship comes ease, because hardship and ease are two opposite things that cannot be together. So here too it indicates delay. (Ayni, Mukhtasar) 'Tomorrow' meaning: if her master says to her: 'When tomorrow comes, you are free', or her husband says to her: 'When tomorrow comes, you are divorced twice' (Ayni), then tomorrow comes: no. That is, the slave woman is emancipated and divorced with severe prohibition, and she is not permissible for the husband until she marries another husband, and the husband does not have the right to take her back according to the two shaykhs. According to Muhammad, he has the right to take her back, because when the emancipation and divorce are both tied to one condition, which is the coming of tomorrow, and emancipation occurs faster because it is a return to the original state and is a praised, recommended thing in sharia, unlike divorce which is the most hated of permissible things, it becomes necessary that she be divorced at the time of descending upon freedom, so divorce meets her while she is free, and thus she is not prohibited by two divorces with severe prohibition as in the first case. Their evidence: he tied her divorce to that which the master tied her emancipation, and then emancipation meets her while she is a slave, so likewise divorce, so she is prohibited by two divorces with severe prohibition, because the time of establishing emancipation is the time of establishing divorce necessarily due to their being tied to one condition, unlike the first case, because in that case the divorce was tied to the master's act of emancipation, so it occurs after emancipation. (Mukhtasar, Fath) And her waiting period is three menstrual cycles by agreement for precaution. And if the husband is sick, she does not inherit from him, because at the time he spoke the divorce, she did not have capacity for inheritance at the time of speaking, so the condition of her inheriting from him, which is the intention of flight, did not exist. As for if the husband's tying of divorce occurred during his illness after he knew of the master's tying of her freedom to the coming of tomorrow, she inherits from him according to Muhammad. (Fath) So it is three (i.e., three divorces, and if he pointed with his fingers and did not say 'like this', it is one (T)) because pointing with fingers informs of the number by custom and law when accompanied by an ambiguous noun. He (peace be upon him) said: 'The month is like this, like this, like this' and he pointed with all his ten fingers, thirty days. Then he said: 'The month is like this, like this, like this' and he bent his thumb in the third time, meaning twenty-nine days. And the pointing is interpreted with the outstretched fingers, not the closed ones, because when he bent his thumb the third time, twenty-nine days was understood. If the closed ones were considered, the understanding would be twenty-one days. So the outstretched ones are considered absolutely, due to custom and the sunnah. And if he pointed with one, she is divorced once; if he pointed with two, she is divorced twice; if he pointed with three fingers and said: 'I meant the pointing with the closed ones', he is believed in his intention (diyaanatan) but not in court. Similarly, if he intended pointing with the palm. And if he said: 'You are divorced' and pointed with his fingers and did not say 'like this', it is one, because pointing is an interpretation of the ambiguous number, and it did not occur, so the pointing is invalid, and his words 'You are divorced' remain, and that does not take number. (Tabyin al-Haqa'iq 3/68) (Ayni, Fath) 'Divorced irrevocably or absolutely' its grammatical accusative due to being a verbal noun from 'batta' meaning to cut decisively. (Ayni) This is a beginning of explaining the occurrence of irrevocable divorce by description of divorce that indicates severity and increase. 'Al-batt' is the verbal noun of 'batta amrahu' when he decides it. And if he intended by 'taliq' one and by something like 'bain' another, two irrevocable divorces occur. (Fath)
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary (sharh) expanding on rulings from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding divorce. It covers several distinct issues: (1) Adding divorce to a future time such as death is void by consensus because death negates either the husband's capacity (ahliyyah) or the wife's suitability (mahalliyyah) for divorce. (2) If either spouse acquires ownership of the other (wholly or partially), the marriage contract is automatically voided. The reasoning is that right-hand ownership (milk al-yamin) is stronger than marriage ownership (milk al-nikah), so the latter cannot coexist with it. An exception is a mukatib (slave for a contract of manumission) who does not actually own his wife but has a right to ownership. (3) If the husband buys his wife (who is a slave) and then divorces her, the divorce does not take effect, but iddah remains obligatory because separation occurred via ownership rather than divorce. (4) If the wife owns the husband, divorce also does not occur according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf; Muhammad argues that it does because the contract subsists in some respect, but the preferred position (mufta bihi) is the former. (5) A separate scenario: if a man says to his wife who is a slave of another: 'You are divorced twice upon your master's emancipation,' and the master emancipates her, then two divorces occur and the husband retains the right to take her back because the emancipation precedes the divorce (the word 'ma'a' is interpreted as meaning delay, like in the Quranic verse). (6) If both emancipation and divorce are tied to a single condition (e.g., coming of tomorrow), then when the condition occurs, the emancipation happens simultaneously with the divorce. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, this results in an irrevocable three-fold prohibition because she was still a slave at the moment of divorce; Muhammad holds that emancipation occurs first, so she is free at the time of divorce, hence only two divorces occur and husband retains the right to take back. (7) The iddah in such cases is three menstrual cycles by consensus. (8) If the husband was sick at the time of pronouncing the conditional divorce, she does not inherit from him unless he knew of the emancipation condition and intended to divest her of inheritance. (9) Pointing with fingers during talaq: if the husband says 'you are divorced' and points with three outstretched fingers, it counts as three; if he says 'like this' and points with one finger, it is one; if he points without saying 'haka' (like this), it is only one because the pointing is an interpretation of the ambiguous and is ignored. (10) The terms 'bain' or 'al-batta' indicate an irrevocable divorce (talaq bain); if the husband intends by 'taliq' one and by 'bain' another, two irrevocable divorces occur.

Key Terms
ملك اليمين (milk al-yamin): Right-hand ownership, i.e., ownership of a slave by purchase or inheritance.
ملك النكاح (milk al-nikah): The ownership of marriage, i.e., the marital bond that gives the husband rights over his wife.
شقص (shiqs): A share; partial ownership of something.
مكاتب (mukatib): A slave who has contracted with his master to gain freedom upon paying a sum.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man buys his wife (a slave) and then says 'You are divorced.' What is the ruling?
A) The divorce is valid, and she must observe iddah.
B) The marriage is voided by purchase, the divorce has no effect, but iddah is required.
C) The marriage continues because ownership does not supersede marriage.
D) The divorce is suspended until the wife is freed.

Key Takeaway
Ownership (milk al-yamin) supersedes marriage, voiding it automatically; any divorce after that is void, but iddah remains obligatory.

Talaq — Divorce
advanced
12 min
Conditional Divorce Tied to Emancipation of a Slave Wife
Two scenarios of conditional divorce for a slave wife, with dispute on simultaneity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze two conditional divorce scenarios involving a slave wife and distinguish the outcomes based on whether the condition uses 'ma'a' (with) or a shared future event.

Core Idea
When divorce is tied to emancipation, the word 'ma'a' can indicate delay, making the wife free before divorce; if tied to the same time, simultaneity leads to stricter consequences.

This lesson examines two intricate cases from the Hanafi texts on conditional divorce involving a wife who is a slave of a third party. In the first, the husband says 'You are divorced twice upon your master's emancipation'—the word 'ma'a' is interpreted as delay, so the wife becomes free before divorce, resulting in only two divorces and the right of return. In the second, both emancipation and divorce are tied to the same future event (e.g., 'when tomorrow comes'), leading to a dispute: do they occur simultaneously or sequentially? The Hanafi relied-upon view holds simultaneity, resulting in an irrevocable three-fold divorce.

Guided Path
1
Understand the first scenario: divorce 'upon emancipation' using 'ma'a'
The husband says to his wife (a slave of another): 'You are divorced twice with (ma'a) your master's emancipation.' The master then emancipates her.
2
Interpret 'ma'a' as indicating delay
The text explains that 'ma'a' here means 'after', similar to the Quranic verse 'inna ma'a al-usri yusra' (indeed, with hardship comes ease), meaning after hardship. Thus emancipation occurs first, then divorce.
This is based on a linguistic principle that 'ma'a' can denote delay when the two events cannot coexist.
3
Determine the outcome: two divorces, husband can take back
Since she is free at the time of divorce, the two divorces do not make her irrevocably prohibited (which requires three for a free woman). The husband retains the right to take her back during iddah.
4
Analyze the second scenario: both tied to same condition (e.g., 'when tomorrow comes')
The husband and the master both condition their acts on the same future event. The text presents the dispute between the Shaykhayn and Muhammad.
5
State the Shaykhayn's view: simultaneity leads to irrevocable divorce
According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, both occur at the same instant; she is still a slave at that moment, so two divorces make her irrevocably forbidden (like three for a free woman). No right of return.
6
State Muhammad's view: precedence of emancipation
Muhammad argues that emancipation is faster because it is a return to the original state (freedom) and is recommended, while divorce is hated. Thus emancipation occurs first, divorce second, so she is free at divorce—only two divorces.
7
Identify the relied-upon view
The two shaykhs' view is preferred as the mufta bihi. The text notes that iddah is three menstrual cycles by consensus in both cases.
Also, if the husband was sick at the time of pronouncing, she may or may not inherit depending on his knowledge of the emancipation condition.

Book of Divorce or 611 Addition of Divorce to Time: With my death or with your death is void. And if he owns her or a share of her, or she owns him or a share of him, the marriage contract is void. That is, the woman owned the husband. So if he buys her and divorces her, it does not occur. You are divorced twice upon the emancipation of your master, so if he emancipates, he has the right to take her back. That is, the husband bought his wife. And the meaning of master is the slave-owner. And if the occurrence were by description, the mention of three would be useless. So it is established that the actual occurrence is only the described entity that is omitted. The phrase 'you are divorced one' means one divorce. So if the actual occurrence is the entity described by the number, and doubt enters into the occurrence, it does not occur by doubt. The second: if he said to her: You are divorced upon my death. The third: he said to her: You are divorced upon your death. These two are nothing by agreement, because he added divorce to a state contrary to divorce; because his death negates the capacity for divorce, and her death negates the capability. And each of capacity and capability is necessary for the permissibility of the act. (Mukhtasar) If she owns her husband, and if he owns her: i.e., if the husband owns his wife, as if she was a slave and he bought her, inherited her, etc. (Ayni) Or a share of her: i.e., he owns a part of her, such as buying half or inheriting half or third. (Ayni) The contract becomes void. As for the husband owning her, because establishing ownership over a free woman was for the need of preserving progeny, and the ownership of marriage was for necessity, and it has been removed by the existence of right-hand ownership. And as for her owning him, because of the combination of being an owner and owned. It cannot be said that permissibility is not established by owning part, because we say: right-hand ownership is evidence of permissibility, so it stands in place of permissibility for facilitation. The conclusion: the combination of being an owner and owned prevents the initiation of marriage, and likewise its continuation. And if the husband owns his wife, because right-hand ownership is stronger, so the weaker, i.e., the ownership of marriage, does not compete with it; rather it is dissolved by its existence. If you say: a mukatib who buys his wife does not nullify the marriage, I say: he does not have ownership but rather a right to ownership, and that does not prevent the continuation of marriage. (Ayni, Mukhtasar, Fath) So if he buys her and divorces her, nothing occurs, whether he has consummated or not, and the waiting period is obligatory by agreement, because separation has occurred between them by ownership of the neck, and divorce requires the existence of marriage ownership in some respect, and it did not exist. And if the woman owns the husband entirely or partially, then she divorces him, similarly divorce does not occur according to the two shaykhs. And from Muhammad: because the waiting period is obligatory here by agreement, and the existence of the contract in some respect is sufficient for divorce to occur upon her, unlike if he owns her, because there is no waiting period on her until her intercourse is permissible. We say: the waiting period is obligatory there, so that he may not marry her to another until her waiting period ends. And if he emancipates her, the waiting period appears, but it did not appear with respect to him because of the permissibility of intercourse on his part by right-hand ownership. So it becomes clear that this distinction is not correct. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/240, Fath) 'You are divorced': i.e., if he says to his wife who is a slave of another: You are divorced, etc. (T) So if he emancipates: i.e., the master emancipates the slave, she is divorced twice. (Ayni) He has the right to take her back (Ayni) i.e., the husband has the right to take her back because he divorced her while she was free, and a free woman is not prohibited by two divorces with severe prohibition. (Ayni) That is, if he says to his wife who is a slave of another: 'You are divorced twice upon the emancipation of your master', and her master emancipates her, the husband retains the right to take her back. Because the meaning of his words 'upon the emancipation of your master' is 'with the emancipation of your master', since emancipation may be used transitively. Then, since the master's emancipation is a condition for the divorce, it must be prior to it, because the condition is prior to the conditioned. So the emancipation is prior to the occurrence of divorce, so the divorce occurs while she is free. - also it occurs. Book of Divorce 412 Addition of Divorce to Time: 'You are divorced' and if her emancipation and her two divorces are tied to the coming of tomorrow, then tomorrow comes, no, and her waiting period is three menstrual cycles in both cases, by consensus for precaution. 'You are divorced like this' and he pointed with three fingers, so it is three. 'You are divorced irrevocably or absolutely' = the slave woman, her two divorces are also tied. That is, his pointing; so her divorce becomes three, so he retains the right to take her back. If it is said: the word 'with' indicates conjunction, so divorce and emancipation should occur together, so how is this? We say: the word 'with' has come to indicate delay, as in the verse of the Most High: 'Indeed, with hardship comes ease' (94:6), meaning after hardship comes ease, because hardship and ease are two opposite things that cannot be together. So here too it indicates delay. (Ayni, Mukhtasar) 'Tomorrow' meaning: if her master says to her: 'When tomorrow comes, you are free', or her husband says to her: 'When tomorrow comes, you are divorced twice' (Ayni), then tomorrow comes: no. That is, the slave woman is emancipated and divorced with severe prohibition, and she is not permissible for the husband until she marries another husband, and the husband does not have the right to take her back according to the two shaykhs. According to Muhammad, he has the right to take her back, because when the emancipation and divorce are both tied to one condition, which is the coming of tomorrow, and emancipation occurs faster because it is a return to the original state and is a praised, recommended thing in sharia, unlike divorce which is the most hated of permissible things, it becomes necessary that she be divorced at the time of descending upon freedom, so divorce meets her while she is free, and thus she is not prohibited by two divorces with severe prohibition as in the first case. Their evidence: he tied her divorce to that which the master tied her emancipation, and then emancipation meets her while she is a slave, so likewise divorce, so she is prohibited by two divorces with severe prohibition, because the time of establishing emancipation is the time of establishing divorce necessarily due to their being tied to one condition, unlike the first case, because in that case the divorce was tied to the master's act of emancipation, so it occurs after emancipation. (Mukhtasar, Fath) And her waiting period is three menstrual cycles by agreement for precaution. And if the husband is sick, she does not inherit from him, because at the time he spoke the divorce, she did not have capacity for inheritance at the time of speaking, so the condition of her inheriting from him, which is the intention of flight, did not exist. As for if the husband's tying of divorce occurred during his illness after he knew of the master's tying of her freedom to the coming of tomorrow, she inherits from him according to Muhammad. (Fath) So it is three (i.e., three divorces, and if he pointed with his fingers and did not say 'like this', it is one (T)) because pointing with fingers informs of the number by custom and law when accompanied by an ambiguous noun. He (peace be upon him) said: 'The month is like this, like this, like this' and he pointed with all his ten fingers, thirty days. Then he said: 'The month is like this, like this, like this' and he bent his thumb in the third time, meaning twenty-nine days. And the pointing is interpreted with the outstretched fingers, not the closed ones, because when he bent his thumb the third time, twenty-nine days was understood. If the closed ones were considered, the understanding would be twenty-one days. So the outstretched ones are considered absolutely, due to custom and the sunnah. And if he pointed with one, she is divorced once; if he pointed with two, she is divorced twice; if he pointed with three fingers and said: 'I meant the pointing with the closed ones', he is believed in his intention (diyaanatan) but not in court. Similarly, if he intended pointing with the palm. And if he said: 'You are divorced' and pointed with his fingers and did not say 'like this', it is one, because pointing is an interpretation of the ambiguous number, and it did not occur, so the pointing is invalid, and his words 'You are divorced' remain, and that does not take number. (Tabyin al-Haqa'iq 3/68) (Ayni, Fath) 'Divorced irrevocably or absolutely' its grammatical accusative due to being a verbal noun from 'batta' meaning to cut decisively. (Ayni) This is a beginning of explaining the occurrence of irrevocable divorce by description of divorce that indicates severity and increase. 'Al-batt' is the verbal noun of 'batta amrahu' when he decides it. And if he intended by 'taliq' one and by something like 'bain' another, two irrevocable divorces occur. (Fath)
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary (sharh) expanding on rulings from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding divorce. It covers several distinct issues: (1) Adding divorce to a future time such as death is void by consensus because death negates either the husband's capacity (ahliyyah) or the wife's suitability (mahalliyyah) for divorce. (2) If either spouse acquires ownership of the other (wholly or partially), the marriage contract is automatically voided. The reasoning is that right-hand ownership (milk al-yamin) is stronger than marriage ownership (milk al-nikah), so the latter cannot coexist with it. An exception is a mukatib (slave for a contract of manumission) who does not actually own his wife but has a right to ownership. (3) If the husband buys his wife (who is a slave) and then divorces her, the divorce does not take effect, but iddah remains obligatory because separation occurred via ownership rather than divorce. (4) If the wife owns the husband, divorce also does not occur according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf; Muhammad argues that it does because the contract subsists in some respect, but the preferred position (mufta bihi) is the former. (5) A separate scenario: if a man says to his wife who is a slave of another: 'You are divorced twice upon your master's emancipation,' and the master emancipates her, then two divorces occur and the husband retains the right to take her back because the emancipation precedes the divorce (the word 'ma'a' is interpreted as meaning delay, like in the Quranic verse). (6) If both emancipation and divorce are tied to a single condition (e.g., coming of tomorrow), then when the condition occurs, the emancipation happens simultaneously with the divorce. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, this results in an irrevocable three-fold prohibition because she was still a slave at the moment of divorce; Muhammad holds that emancipation occurs first, so she is free at the time of divorce, hence only two divorces occur and husband retains the right to take back. (7) The iddah in such cases is three menstrual cycles by consensus. (8) If the husband was sick at the time of pronouncing the conditional divorce, she does not inherit from him unless he knew of the emancipation condition and intended to divest her of inheritance. (9) Pointing with fingers during talaq: if the husband says 'you are divorced' and points with three outstretched fingers, it counts as three; if he says 'like this' and points with one finger, it is one; if he points without saying 'haka' (like this), it is only one because the pointing is an interpretation of the ambiguous and is ignored. (10) The terms 'bain' or 'al-batta' indicate an irrevocable divorce (talaq bain); if the husband intends by 'taliq' one and by 'bain' another, two irrevocable divorces occur.

Key Terms
العتق (al-'itq): Emancipation; freeing of a slave.
الطلاق المعلق (al-talaq al-mu'allaq): Conditional divorce; divorce that is suspended upon the occurrence of a future condition.
مع (ma'a): With; a preposition that can mean simultaneous or delayed depending on context.
الرجعة (al-ruj'ah): The right of the husband to take back his wife during her iddah without a new marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A man says to his wife (a slave of another): 'You are divorced twice with the emancipation of your master.' After the master emancipates her, how many divorces occur?
A) Two divorces; husband can take her back.
B) Three divorces; husband cannot take her back.
C) One divorce; husband can take her back.
D) No divorce occurs because the condition was invalid.

Key Takeaway
The interpretation of 'ma'a' as delay in conditional divorce leads to a more lenient outcome (two divorces, ruju possible), while simultaneous conditions lead to irrevocability.

Pointing in Divorce and Irrevocable Terms
Rulings on using finger pointing and irrevocable terms in divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the number of divorces when a husband uses finger pointing or terms like 'bain' and 'al-batta'.

Core Idea
Pointing with outstretched fingers while saying 'hāka' (like this) sets the number; without 'hāka', it is one. 'Bain' or 'al-batta' renders the divorce irrevocable.

The passage concludes with practical rules on two related matters: (1) How many divorces occur when a husband says 'You are divorced' while making a hand gesture, and (2) The effect of adding words like 'bain' (irrevocable) or 'al-batta' (absolutely). These rules illustrate how the number and nature of divorce are determined by explicit or implicit indicators, and how the husband's intention is considered in private (diyanatan) but not in court (qadaan) when it contradicts the apparent meaning.

Guided Path
1
Understand the role of pointing with the word 'hāka'
If the husband says 'You are divorced like this (hāka)' and points with fingers, the number of fingers indicates the number of divorces. Outstretched fingers count, not closed ones, based on the Prophet's hadith on the number of days in a month.
The hadith: the Prophet pointed with all ten fingers for 30 days, and with nine fingers (bending thumb) for 29 days.
2
Apply to various finger counts
Pointing with one outstretched finger = one divorce; with two = two; with three = three. If he points with three but says he meant the closed ones, he is believed in private (diyanatan) but not in court.
3
Rule when 'hāka' is not said
If he says 'You are divorced' and points with fingers without saying 'like this', the pointing is ignored, and only one divorce occurs because the word 'taliq' does not imply number.
4
Understand the terms 'bain' and 'al-batta'
These terms mean 'irrevocable divorce'. 'Al-batt' is from 'batta amrahu' meaning to decide decisively. When added to 'taliq', it indicates irrevocability.
5
Case where multiple intents lead to multiple divorces
If the husband says 'You are divorced' and intends with 'taliq' one, and with 'bain' another, two irrevocable divorces occur.

That is, the woman owned the husband. So if he buys her and divorces her, it does not occur. You are divorced twice upon the emancipation of your master, so if he emancipates, he has the right to take her back. That is, the husband bought his wife. And the meaning of master is the slave-owner. And if the occurrence were by description, the mention of three would be useless. So it is established that the actual occurrence is only the described entity that is omitted. The phrase 'you are divorced one' means one divorce. So if the actual occurrence is the entity described by the number, and doubt enters into the occurrence, it does not occur by doubt. The second: if he said to her: You are divorced upon my death. The third: he said to her: You are divorced upon your death. These two are nothing by agreement, because he added divorce to a state contrary to divorce; because his death negates the capacity for divorce, and her death negates the capability. And each of capacity and capability is necessary for the permissibility of the act. (Mukhtasar) If she owns her husband, and if he owns her: i.e., if the husband owns his wife, as if she was a slave and he bought her, inherited her, etc. (Ayni) Or a share of her: i.e., he owns a part of her, such as buying half or inheriting half or third. (Ayni) The contract becomes void. As for the husband owning her, because establishing ownership over a free woman was for the need of preserving progeny, and the ownership of marriage was for necessity, and it has been removed by the existence of right-hand ownership. And as for her owning him, because of the combination of being an owner and owned. It cannot be said that permissibility is not established by owning part, because we say: right-hand ownership is evidence of permissibility, so it stands in place of permissibility for facilitation. The conclusion: the combination of being an owner and owned prevents the initiation of marriage, and likewise its continuation. And if the husband owns his wife, because right-hand ownership is stronger, so the weaker, i.e., the ownership of marriage, does not compete with it; rather it is dissolved by its existence. If you say: a mukatib who buys his wife does not nullify the marriage, I say: he does not have ownership but rather a right to ownership, and that does not prevent the continuation of marriage. (Ayni, Mukhtasar, Fath) So if he buys her and divorces her, nothing occurs, whether he has consummated or not, and the waiting period is obligatory by agreement, because separation has occurred between them by ownership of the neck, and divorce requires the existence of marriage ownership in some respect, and it did not exist. And if the woman owns the husband entirely or partially, then she divorces him, similarly divorce does not occur according to the two shaykhs. And from Muhammad: because the waiting period is obligatory here by agreement, and the existence of the contract in some respect is sufficient for divorce to occur upon her, unlike if he owns her, because there is no waiting period on her until her intercourse is permissible. We say: the waiting period is obligatory there, so that he may not marry her to another until her waiting period ends. And if he emancipates her, the waiting period appears, but it did not appear with respect to him because of the permissibility of intercourse on his part by right-hand ownership. So it becomes clear that this distinction is not correct. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq 1/240, Fath) 'You are divorced': i.e., if he says to his wife who is a slave of another: You are divorced, etc. (T) So if he emancipates: i.e., the master emancipates the slave, she is divorced twice. (Ayni) He has the right to take her back (Ayni) i.e., the husband has the right to take her back because he divorced her while she was free, and a free woman is not prohibited by two divorces with severe prohibition. (Ayni) That is, if he says to his wife who is a slave of another: 'You are divorced twice upon the emancipation of your master', and her master emancipates her, the husband retains the right to take her back. Because the meaning of his words 'upon the emancipation of your master' is 'with the emancipation of your master', since emancipation may be used transitively. Then, since the master's emancipation is a condition for the divorce, it must be prior to it, because the condition is prior to the conditioned. So the emancipation is prior to the occurrence of divorce, so the divorce occurs while she is free. - also it occurs. 'You are divorced like this' and he pointed with three fingers, so it is three. 'You are divorced irrevocably or absolutely' = the slave woman, her two divorces are also tied. That is, his pointing; so her divorce becomes three, so he retains the right to take her back. If it is said: the word 'with' indicates conjunction, so divorce and emancipation should occur together, so how is this? We say: the word 'with' has come to indicate delay, as in the verse of the Most High: 'Indeed, with hardship comes ease' (94:6), meaning after hardship comes ease, because hardship and ease are two opposite things that cannot be together. So here too it indicates delay. (Ayni, Mukhtasar) 'Tomorrow' meaning: if her master says to her: 'When tomorrow comes, you are free', or her husband says to her: 'When tomorrow comes, you are divorced twice' (Ayni), then tomorrow comes: no. That is, the slave woman is emancipated and divorced with severe prohibition, and she is not permissible for the husband until she marries another husband, and the husband does not have the right to take her back according to the two shaykhs. According to Muhammad, he has the right to take her back, because when the emancipation and divorce are both tied to one condition, which is the coming of tomorrow, and emancipation occurs faster because it is a return to the original state and is a praised, recommended thing in sharia, unlike divorce which is the most hated of permissible things, it becomes necessary that she be divorced at the time of descending upon freedom, so divorce meets her while she is free, and thus she is not prohibited by two divorces with severe prohibition as in the first case. Their evidence: he tied her divorce to that which the master tied her emancipation, and then emancipation meets her while she is a slave, so likewise divorce, so she is prohibited by two divorces with severe prohibition, because the time of establishing emancipation is the time of establishing divorce necessarily due to their being tied to one condition, unlike the first case, because in that case the divorce was tied to the master's act of emancipation, so it occurs after emancipation. (Mukhtasar, Fath) And her waiting period is three menstrual cycles by agreement for precaution. And if the husband is sick, she does not inherit from him, because at the time he spoke the divorce, she did not have capacity for inheritance at the time of speaking, so the condition of her inheriting from him, which is the intention of flight, did not exist. As for if the husband's tying of divorce occurred during his illness after he knew of the master's tying of her freedom to the coming of tomorrow, she inherits from him according to Muhammad. (Fath) So it is three (i.e., three divorces, and if he pointed with his fingers and did not say 'like this', it is one (T)) because pointing with fingers informs of the number by custom and law when accompanied by an ambiguous noun. He (peace be upon him) said: 'The month is like this, like this, like this' and he pointed with all his ten fingers, thirty days. Then he said: 'The month is like this, like this, like this' and he bent his thumb in the third time, meaning twenty-nine days. And the pointing is interpreted with the outstretched fingers, not the closed ones, because when he bent his thumb the third time, twenty-nine days was understood. If the closed ones were considered, the understanding would be twenty-one days. So the outstretched ones are considered absolutely, due to custom and the sunnah. And if he pointed with one, she is divorced once; if he pointed with two, she is divorced twice; if he pointed with three fingers and said: 'I meant the pointing with the closed ones', he is believed in his intention (diyaanatan) but not in court. Similarly, if he intended pointing with the palm. And if he said: 'You are divorced' and pointed with his fingers and did not say 'like this', it is one, because pointing is an interpretation of the ambiguous number, and it did not occur, so the pointing is invalid, and his words 'You are divorced' remain, and that does not take number. (Tabyin al-Haqa'iq 3/68) (Ayni, Fath) 'Divorced irrevocably or absolutely' its grammatical accusative due to being a verbal noun from 'batta' meaning to cut decisively. (Ayni) This is a beginning of explaining the occurrence of irrevocable divorce by description of divorce that indicates severity and increase. 'Al-batt' is the verbal noun of 'batta amrahu' when he decides it. And if he intended by 'taliq' one and by something like 'bain' another, two irrevocable divorces occur. (Fath)
Source pages p. 412

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary (sharh) expanding on rulings from Kanz al-Daqa'iq regarding divorce. It covers several distinct issues: (1) Adding divorce to a future time such as death is void by consensus because death negates either the husband's capacity (ahliyyah) or the wife's suitability (mahalliyyah) for divorce. (2) If either spouse acquires ownership of the other (wholly or partially), the marriage contract is automatically voided. The reasoning is that right-hand ownership (milk al-yamin) is stronger than marriage ownership (milk al-nikah), so the latter cannot coexist with it. An exception is a mukatib (slave for a contract of manumission) who does not actually own his wife but has a right to ownership. (3) If the husband buys his wife (who is a slave) and then divorces her, the divorce does not take effect, but iddah remains obligatory because separation occurred via ownership rather than divorce. (4) If the wife owns the husband, divorce also does not occur according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf; Muhammad argues that it does because the contract subsists in some respect, but the preferred position (mufta bihi) is the former. (5) A separate scenario: if a man says to his wife who is a slave of another: 'You are divorced twice upon your master's emancipation,' and the master emancipates her, then two divorces occur and the husband retains the right to take her back because the emancipation precedes the divorce (the word 'ma'a' is interpreted as meaning delay, like in the Quranic verse). (6) If both emancipation and divorce are tied to a single condition (e.g., coming of tomorrow), then when the condition occurs, the emancipation happens simultaneously with the divorce. According to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, this results in an irrevocable three-fold prohibition because she was still a slave at the moment of divorce; Muhammad holds that emancipation occurs first, so she is free at the time of divorce, hence only two divorces occur and husband retains the right to take back. (7) The iddah in such cases is three menstrual cycles by consensus. (8) If the husband was sick at the time of pronouncing the conditional divorce, she does not inherit from him unless he knew of the emancipation condition and intended to divest her of inheritance. (9) Pointing with fingers during talaq: if the husband says 'you are divorced' and points with three outstretched fingers, it counts as three; if he says 'like this' and points with one finger, it is one; if he points without saying 'haka' (like this), it is only one because the pointing is an interpretation of the ambiguous and is ignored. (10) The terms 'bain' or 'al-batta' indicate an irrevocable divorce (talaq bain); if the husband intends by 'taliq' one and by 'bain' another, two irrevocable divorces occur.

Key Terms
هكذا (hāka): 'Like this' – a word used to connect a gesture to a statement.
بائن (bā'in): Irrevocable divorce.
البتة (al-batta): Absolutely; a term indicating finality.
ديانة (diyānah): In private, according to one's intention; not enforceable in court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says 'You are divorced' and points with three outstretched fingers. He does not say 'like this'. How many divorces occur?
A) Three divorces.
B) Two divorces.
C) One divorce.
D) No divorce because the gesture is invalid.

Key Takeaway
The number of divorces is determined by explicit wording or by gesture only when accompanied by 'hāka'; otherwise, only one divorce occurs.

Talaq — Divorce

Simultaneous Conditional Divorce and Emancipation: The Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on simultaneous conditional divorce and emancipation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the sequence of emancipation and divorce when both are conditioned on the same event, and apply the practical ruling to a similar scenario.

Core Idea
When a slave woman's emancipation and divorce are both conditioned on the same event, the Hanafi school splits: do they occur together or does emancipation precede divorce?

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute (ikhtilaf) between the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf) and Muhammad al-Shaybani. The case involves a slave woman whose owner conditions her emancipation on the arrival of tomorrow, and her husband conditions two divorces on the same event. The legal question is whether the emancipation and divorce occur simultaneously or in sequence, leading to different marital outcomes. Understanding this dispute reveals how the Hanafi school contrasts the nature of emancipation (a recommended return to origin) with divorce (the most hateful of permitted things), and how these considerations affect legal timing.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the case and the condition
The passage involves a slave woman whose emancipation is suspended on the arrival of tomorrow by her master, and whose husband also suspends two divorces on the same event. Both are dependent on a single condition.
The key is that both acts are tied to the same future moment.
2
2. State the position of the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf)
They hold that emancipation and divorce occur simultaneously. Since she is still a slave at the moment of divorce, two divorces suffice to make her irrevocably divorced (ba'in baynunah kubra). The husband cannot take her back except through a hulla marriage.
Their reasoning: time of affirmation is identical for both because they are attached to one condition.
3
3. State the position of Muhammad al-Shaybani
He holds that emancipation occurs first because it is a return to the original state and is a recommended act. Thus, she becomes free before the divorce falls, so the divorce meets a free woman, making it only two divorces of a free woman, which are revocable (raj'i). The husband can take her back during the 'iddah.
His reasoning: emancipation is 'faster' due to its nature, so it precedes.
4
4. Identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Under the two shaykhs, the divorce is irrevocable (ba'in baynunah kubra) and she must marry another before remarrying her first husband. Under Muhammad, the divorce is revocable, so the husband can reconcile without a new marriage.
This directly affects the woman's ability to return to her husband.
5
5. Note additional rulings: 'iddah and inheritance
Both positions agree that her 'iddah is three menstrual cycles (as a precaution). If the husband was sick at the time of pronouncing divorce, she does not inherit from him because the condition for inheritance (intent to evade) is not met. However, if the husband later knew of her pending freedom, Muhammad holds she inherits.
These details show how the dispute has further implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو تعلّق عتقها وطلقتاها بمجيء الغد فجاء الغد لا أي عتقت الأمة، وطلقت مغلظة و لم تحل للزوج حتى تنكح زوجا غيره، ولا يملك الزوج الرجعة عند الشيخين، وعند محمد له الرجعة؛ لأن العتق والطلاق لما تعلقا بشرط واحد وهو مجيء الغد، والعتق أسرع وقوعا؛ لكونه رجوعا إلى الحالة الأصلية، وهو أمر مستحسن مندوب إليه ،شرعا، بخلاف الطلاق؛ فإنه أبغض المباحات، وجب أن تطلق زمن نزول الحرية فيصادفها الطلاق وهي حرة فلا تحرم بالتطليقتين حرمة غليظة كما في المسألة الأولى، ولهما: أنه علق طلاقها بما علق به المولى عتقها ثم العتق يصادفها وهي أمة فكذا الطلاق فتحرم بتطليقتين حرمة غليظة؛ لأن زمان ثبوت العتق هو زمان ثبوت الطلاق ضرورة تعلقهما بشرط واحد، بخلاف المسألة الأولى؛ لأن فيها علق التطليق بإعتاق المولى، فيقع بعد العتق. (فتح)
And if her emancipation and her two divorces are both attached to the arrival of tomorrow, then when tomorrow comes, no (rather), she becomes free, and she is divorced with a severe divorce, and she does not become permissible for her husband until she marries another husband, and the husband does not have the right to reconciliation according to the two shaykhs, and according to Muhammad he has the right to reconciliation; because the emancipation and divorce were both attached to one condition, which is the arrival of tomorrow, and emancipation occurs faster because it is a return to the original state, and it is a praiseworthy recommended matter in Shari'a, contrary to divorce, which is the most hateful of permitted things, so it is necessary that she be divorced at the time of descent of freedom, so the divorce meets her while she is free, so she is not prohibited by two divorces with a severe prohibition as in the first case. But for them (the two shaykhs): he attached her divorce to what the slave-owner attached her emancipation to, then emancipation meets her while she is a slave girl, and likewise the divorce, so she is prohibited by two divorces with a severe prohibition; because the time of affirmation of emancipation is the time of affirmation of divorce, necessarily due to their attachment to one condition, unlike the first case, because in it the divorce was attached to the owner's emancipating, so it occurs after emancipation. (Khalasa, Fath) And her 'iddah is three menstrual cycles, meaning by agreement, as a precaution. And if the husband was sick, she does not inherit from him; because when he spoke the divorce, she did not have the capacity to inherit at the time of speaking, so the condition for her inheritance from him was not found, which is the intent of evasion. However, if the husband's suspended divorce occurred during his illness after his knowledge that the owner's suspension of her freedom has occurred upon the arrival of tomorrow, she inherits from him according to Muhammad. (Fath)
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute about a case where a slave woman's owner conditions her emancipation on the arrival of tomorrow, and her husband conditions her divorce (two divorces) on the same event. The matn states the ruling, and the commentary expands with the reasoning. According to the two shaykhs (Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf), when tomorrow comes, the emancipation and divorce occur simultaneously. Since she is still a slave at the moment of divorce, two divorces suffice to make her irrevocably divorced (ba'in baynunah kubra), so she cannot return to her husband without a hulla marriage. According to Muhammad al-Shaybani, emancipation takes precedence because it is a return to the original state and is recommended, so she becomes free first; then the divorce falls on a free woman, making it only two divorces, which are revocable (the husband can take her back during the 'iddah). The passage also affirms that the 'iddah is three menstrual cycles by consensus, as a precaution. Additionally, there is a nuance regarding inheritance: if the husband divorces while sick with the intent to deprive her of inheritance, she does not inherit from him; but if he was unaware of her pending freedom, she inherits according to Muhammad.

Key Terms
عتق ('itq): The act of freeing a slave; emancipation.
رجعة (ruj'a): The right of a husband to take back his wife during the 'iddah after a revocable divorce.
عدة ('idda): The waiting period a woman must observe after divorce or death of husband before she can remarry.
بائن بينونة كبرى (ba'in baynunah kubra): An irrevocable divorce that ends the marriage completely; the woman is not lawful for the husband until she marries another man (hulla).
رجعي (raj'i): A revocable divorce where the husband can take back his wife during the 'iddah without a new marriage contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The marriage remains valid and the divorce does not occur
B) She is divorced with a revocable divorce (raj'i)
C) She is divorced with an irrevocable divorce (ba'in baynunah kubra)
D) She becomes free but the divorce is suspended until the 'iddah ends

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute on simultaneous conditional divorce and emancipation turns on whether the acts occur at the same time or sequentially, leading to fundamentally different marital outcomes.

Indicating Number of Divorces with Finger Gesture
Ruling on counting divorces when a man points with his fingers while saying 'like this'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a finger gesture specifies the number of divorces and apply the ruling to given scenarios.

Core Idea
A finger gesture specifies the number of divorces only when accompanied by 'hākaḏā' and performed with spread fingers.

In this passage, we examine a subtle but important detail in the law of divorce: how a husband's finger gesture can determine the number of divorces. The ruling hinges on the phrase 'hākaḏā' (like this) and the manner of gesturing. Without these conditions, the gesture is null and the divorce defaults to one.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal effect
The act is uttering a divorce with an ambiguous number, accompanied by a finger gesture. The legal effect is that the number of divorces is determined by the gesture if conditions are met.
2
Locate the conditions for the gesture to be effective
Two conditions: (1) the word 'hākaḏā' must be spoken to connect the gesture to the number; (2) the gesture must be with spread fingers (manšūra), not closed (maḍmūma).
Closed fingers are not legally recognized for counting.
3
Understand the proof from the Prophet's hadith
The Prophet (peace be upon him) indicated the number of days in a month by spreading his fingers: ten for thirty, and with thumb tucked for twenty-nine. This establishes spread fingers as the valid method.
The hadith is used as evidence (dalīl) for the rule.
4
Distinguish between spread and closed fingers
Spread fingers clearly show each digit, hence count. Closed fingers (e.g., two pressed together) are ambiguous and not considered for specifying number.
If closed fingers were used, the understood number would be different (e.g., 21 instead of 29).
5
Apply the ruling to scenarios
Test cases: with 'hākaḏā' and spread fingers → the number indicated counts. Without 'hākaḏā', even with gesture → only one divorce. If he gestures but later claims a different intention, he is believed in conscience but not in court.
The default is one divorce if no specification is valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهي ثلاث [ أي ثلاث طلقات ولو أشار بأصابعه ولم يقل هكذا فهي واحدة (ط)] لأن الإشارة بالأصابع تفيد العلم بالعدد عرفاً وشرعاً إذا اقترنت بالاسم المبهم، قال عليه : الشهر هكذا وهكذا وهكذا" أشار بأصابعه العشرة ثلاثين يوماً، ثم قال الشهر هكذا وهكذا وهكذا"، وخنس إبهامه في يعني الثالثة تسعة وعشرين يوماً، والإشارة يعني تقع بالمنشورة منها دون المضمومة؛ لأنه عليها لما خنس إبهامه المرة الثالثة فهم منه تسعة وعشرون يوما، ولو اعتبرت المضمومة لكان المفهوم أحدا وعشرين يوما، فالمعتبر المنشور مطلقا للعرف وللسنة. ولو أشار بالواحدة طلقت واحدة، ولو أشار بالثنتين طلقت ثنتين، ولو أشار بثلاث أصابع وقال: عنيت الإشارة بالمضمومتين صدق ديانة لا قضاء، وكذا لو نوى الإشارة بالكف ولو قال: أنت طالق" وأشار بأصابعه ولم يقل: "هكذا فهي واحدة؛ لأن الإشارة تفسير للمبهم من العدد، ولم يوجد فلغت الإشارة ويبقى، قوله: "أنت طالق" وهو لا يحتمل العدد.
So it is three [meaning three divorces. And if he gestured with his fingers and did not say 'like this', then it is one.] Because gesturing with fingers conveys knowledge of number by custom and law when it is connected to an ambiguous noun. He (peace be upon him) said: 'The month is like this, like this, like this'—he gestured with all ten fingers for thirty days—then he said: 'The month is like this, like this, like this' and he tucked his thumb in the third time for twenty-nine days. The gesture is considered with the spread fingers, not with the closed ones; because when he tucked his thumb the third time, they understood twenty-nine days. If the closed ones were considered, the understood number would be twenty-one days. So the spread fingers are considered in all cases, by custom and by the Sunnah. If he gestured with one [finger], she is divorced once; if with two, twice; if with three fingers and said: 'I meant the gesture with the two closed fingers', he is believed in his conscience but not in court. Similarly, if he intended the gesture with the palm; and if he said: 'You are divorced' and gestured with his fingers without saying 'like this', then it is one; because the gesture interprets the ambiguous number, and since it did not occur [the condition], the gesture is null and his statement 'You are divorced' remains, which does not carry a number.
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling when a man says to his wife 'you are divorced like this' and uses his fingers to indicate the number. The key is the word 'hākaḏā' (like this), which makes the gesture an explicit interpretation of the ambiguous number. The commentary uses a hadith in which the Prophet (peace be upon him) indicated the number of days in a month by spreading his fingers (ten for thirty) and tucking his thumb in the third gesture for twenty-nine. This establishes that spread fingers (manšūra) are understood to count the number of digits, not closed ones (maḍmūma). Thus, holding up three spread fingers means three divorces. If a man says 'you are divorced' without 'hākaḏā' and only gestures, the gesture is disregarded, and it counts as one divorce, because the word 'tāliq' does not imply a number. If he gestures without 'hākaḏā' but later claims he meant a different number (e.g., two closed fingers), he is believed only as a matter of personal conscience (diyāna), not in judicial proceedings (qaḍā'). The ruling is based on custom and the Sunnah, which recognize finger enumeration as a valid means of specifying number.

Key Terms
هكذا (hākaḏā): A demonstrative phrase meaning 'like this', used to connect a gesture to the number of divorces.
الإشارة المنشورة (al-ishāra al-manšūra): A gesture with fingers spread apart, indicating the number by counting the digits shown.
المضمومة (al-maḍmūma): Fingers closed together (e.g., two fingers pressed together), which does not clearly indicate a specific number.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) The marriage is invalid due to ambiguity
D) It depends on the husband's intention, so he is asked

Key Takeaway
A finger gesture only specifies the number of divorces when it is connected to an ambiguous number by the word 'hākaḏā' and done with spread fingers; otherwise, the divorce defaults to one.

Descriptions Indicating Irrevocable Divorce: Hanafi vs Shafi'i
Ruling on various descriptions that make a divorce irrevocable (ba'in)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that describing divorce as ba'in makes it irrevocable, and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument.

Core Idea
Divorce inherently severs the marriage; describing it as ba'in merely affirms its natural effect.

When a husband says 'You are divorced irrevocably' or uses similar emphatic language, does the divorce become immediately irreversible? The Hanafi and Shafi'i schools differ sharply on this question. Understanding their respective proofs reveals deeper principles about the nature of divorce and the role of intention in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the core Hanafi view: describing divorce as ba'in or with similar words indicating finality or severity results in an irrevocable divorce immediately.
This is based on the principle that divorce in origin causes separation; the description merely hastens what is inevitable.
2
Identify the Shafi'i objection
State the Shafi'i view: such descriptions are null because divorce is legislated to allow for reconciliation (ruju'), so the divorce remains revocable.
Shafi'i argues that describing divorce as ba'in is contrary to the legislated nature of talaq.
3
Analyse the Hanafi proof
Trace the Hanafi reasoning: divorce is legislated to remove the marriage bond; ruju' is a delay granted when no description of finality is added. Thus, adding a description of finality returns the divorce to its original effect.
The Hanafis cite that even before consummation or after the 'iddah, divorce is inherently ba'in.
4
Examine the Shafi'i proof and its rebuttal
Understand the Shafi'i proof: the right of ruju' is part of the legislation of divorce; negating it is invalid. The Hanafi response: ruju' is not an inherent part of divorce but a postponement legislated for the default case.
The Hanafis analogize to saying 'you are divorced on condition I have no ruju' – such a condition is void in their view, but describing as ba'in is not a condition but a description.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
Conclude the practical consequence: Hanafi – husband cannot take her back during 'iddah; Shafi'i – husband can take her back. This affects the permissibility of reconciliation and future marriage.
The difference matters for the validity of subsequent marriage and inheritance rules.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أنت طالق بائن أو البتة انتصابها على المصدرية من بت أمره إذا قطع فيه وجزم. (ع)] شروع في بيان وقوع البائن بوصف الطلاق بما ينبئ عن الشدة والزيادة، والبت مصدر بت أمره إذا قطع به، ولو نوى بطالق واحدة وبنحو بائن أخرى يقع ثنتان بائنتان. (فتح) أو أفحش الطلاق أو أفحش الطلاق: انتصابه على أنه صفة لمصدر محذوف (ع)] أراد به كل وصف على أفعل مراداً به أصله كأخبثه أو أسوأه أو أشره أو أخشنه أو أعظمه؛ لأن الطلاق إنما يوصف بهذا الوصف باعتبار أثره وهو البينونة في الحال، ولو قال: "أجمل "الطلاق" أو "أحسنه" وقعت رجعية إلا أن ينوي ثلاثاً. تبيين الحقائق: ٦٦/٣] فإن قيل: ينبغي أن يقع بهذه الألفاظ ثلاث من غير نية؛ لأنها أفعل التفضيل، وبقوله: "شديدة" ونحوه تقع واحدة فوجب أن يزيد على ذلك، أجيب بأن أفعل يراد به الوصف قال تعالى: ﴿وَبُعُولَتُهُنَّ أَحَقُّ بِرَدِّهِنَ﴾ (البقرة: ۲۲۸) أي حقيق، فلا يحمل على الثلاث بالاحتمال والاعتبار للظاهر. (فتح) أو البدعة: أي أو قال: أنت طالق طلاق البدعة. (ع) أو كالجبل إلخ : أي أو قال: أنت طالق كالجبل أو أشد الطلاق. (ط) أو كألف أي في القوة والحاصل أن الوصف بما ينبئ عن الزيادة يوجب البينونة، وكذا التشبيه أي شيء كان المشبه به، واشترط أبو يوسف ذكر العظم مطلقاً، واشترط زفر أن يكون عظيماً عند الناس. (فتح) أو طويلة : أي أو قال: أنت طالق تطليقة طويلة. (ع) أو عريضة أي أو قال: أنت طالق تطليقة عريضة. (ع) واحدة بائنة : وقال الشافعي: يقع واحدة رجعية إن دخل بها في الفصول كلها؛ لأن الطلاق شرع معقباً للرجعة، فكان وصفه بالبينونة خلاف المشروع، فيلغو، كما إذا قال: أنت طالق على أن لا رجعة لي عليك ولنا أنه وصف الطلاق بما يحتمله لفظه وهو البينونة، ألا ترى أن البينونة تثبت به للحال قبل الدخول وكذا عند ذكر المال وبعد إذا انقضت العدة، لأن الطلاق في الأصل هو الموجب للبينونة؛ لأنه شرع لرفع النكاح وقطعه ولا تأثير لمضي المدة فيها لكن الشرع ورد بالتأخير إلى انقضاء الطلاق إذا لم يكن موصوفا بالبينونة فبقي ما وراءه على أصل القياس، وعن محمد في طلاق الشيطان أو البدعة تقع واحدة رجعية؛ لأن هذا الوصف قد يتكلم في حالة الغيظ، فلا يثبت البينونة بالشك فيكون رجعيا، وعن أبي يوسف في كالجبل أو مثل الجبل رجعي؛ لأنه تشبيه له في توحده. وعن محمد في قوله: "كألف" يقع ثلاث عند عدم النية أيضاً فصار كقوله: كعدد ألف، وكذا في قوله: كمائة، وبه قال أحمد؛ لأن الألف والمائة موضوعان للعدد فيكون التشبيه به للكثرة ، وقد سبق أن التشبيه فيه في القوة، ولو قال: كالنجوم فواحدة، وكعدد النجوم فثلاث؛ لأن التشبيه في الأول في الضياء والنور وفي الثاني للكثرة، وعن أبي يوسف في قوله: طويلة أو عريضة تقع رجعية وكذا عند زفر ؛ لأن الطلاق لا يقبل هذا الوصف فيلغو، ولنا: أنه يراد به العظم فصار كقوله: عظيمة. رمز الحقائق: ٢٤١/١] وعن أبي يوسف ومحمد في طلاق البدعة: لا يكون بائنا إلا بالنية؛ لأن البدعة قد تكون من حيث الإيقاع في حالة الحيض فلا بد من النية. (مستخلص، فتح)
You are divorced irrevocably (ba'in) or permanently (al-battah) — [the word] is in the accusative as a verbal noun from 'batta' meaning to finally decide and settle. (Ayni) [Then] beginning to explain the occurrence of irrevocability by describing divorce with what indicates severity and excess. 'Al-batt' is the verbal noun of 'batta amrahu' when he decides and carries it out. And if he intended with 'tāliq' one and with 'ba'in' another, two irrevocable divorces occur. (Fath) Or the foulest of divorce (afḥash al-ṭalāq) — it is in the accusative as an adjective for an omitted verbal noun. (Ayni) He intends by it every description on the pattern 'afʻal' meaning the original, like the worst, the most evil, the most foul, the harshest, or the greatest; because divorce is described with this description considering its effect, which is immediate irrevocability. And if he said 'the most beautiful divorce' or 'the best', it falls as revocable (raj'i) unless he intends three. (Tabyin al-Haqa'iq) For if it is said: it should be three by these words without intention because they are superlative forms, and by his statement 'severe' and the like one falls, so it must increase beyond that. The answer is that 'afʻal' is used as a descriptive adjective; Allah says {And their husbands are more entitled to take them back} (al-Baqarah 2:228), meaning deserving, so it is not taken as three by probability, and consideration is for the apparent. (Fath) Or 'the divorce of innovation' (ṭalāq al-bidʻa): meaning he said 'you are divorced with a divorce of innovation'. (Ayni) Or 'like a mountain' etc.: meaning he said 'you are divorced like a mountain' or 'the most severe of divorce'. (Taftazani?) Or 'like a thousand' meaning in strength. The sum is that description indicating excess necessitates irrevocability. And likewise simile (tashbih) with whatever object is used, provided Abu Yusuf stipulates mentioning greatness (ʿuẓm) absolutely, and Zufar stipulates that it be considered great among people. (Fath) Or 'long': meaning he said 'you are divorced with a long divorce'. (Ayni) Or 'wide': meaning he said 'you are divorced with a wide divorce'. (Ayni) [It is] one irrevocable divorce. And al-Shafi'i said: one revocable divorce occurs if he had consummated the marriage in all these cases; because divorce is legislated to be followed by reconciliation, so describing it with irrevocability is contrary to what is legislated, thus it is null, like if he said 'you are divorced on condition that I have no right to take you back'. For us (Hanafis), he has described divorce with something its literal meaning can bear, which is irrevocability. Do you not see that irrevocability is established by it immediately before consummation, and similarly when mentioning money, and after the 'iddah ends? Because divorce in origin is what necessitates irrevocability; for it was legislated to remove marriage and sever it, and the passage of time has no effect on it. But legislation delayed it to the end of the 'iddah when it is not described as irrevocable, so whatever remains is upon the original principle (qiyās). And it is reported from Muhammad concerning 'divorce of Satan' or 'divorce of innovation' that one revocable divorce occurs; because this description may be uttered in anger, so irrevocability is not established due to doubt, so it is revocable. And it is reported from Abu Yusuf concerning 'like a mountain' or 'like a mountain' that it is revocable; because it is a simile for its uniqueness. And from Muhammad concerning his saying 'like a thousand' that three occur even without intention, so it is like his saying 'like the number of a thousand', and similarly in his saying 'like a hundred', and Ahmad said the same; because 'thousand' and 'hundred' are terms for numbers, so the simile with them denotes multitude, and it has preceded that simile in it is for strength. And if he said 'like stars', one [divorce occurs]; and 'like the number of stars', three; because the simile in the first is in brightness and light, and in the second for multitude. And it is reported from Abu Yusuf concerning his saying 'long' or 'wide' that it falls as revocable, and likewise with Zufar; because divorce does not accept this description, so it is null. For us: what is intended is greatness, so it is like his saying 'great'. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq). And it is reported from Abu Yusuf and Muhammad concerning 'divorce of innovation' that it is not irrevocable except with intention; because innovation may be in terms of timing during menstruation, so intention is necessary. (Mukhtasar al-Mustakhlas, Fath)
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage details various expressions that make a divorce irrevocable (ba'in) rather than revocable (raj'i). The Hanafi school holds that descriptions implying severity, excess, or finality (such as 'ba'in', 'al-battah', 'afḥash al-ṭalāq', 'ṭalāq al-bidʻa', 'al-shayṭān', 'like a mountain', 'like a thousand', 'long', 'wide') result in one ba'in divorce, unless the husband intends three. The reasoning is that divorce inherently leads to irrevocability; the legislated postponement (to allow reconciliation) applies only when no such description is added. The Shafi'i school opposes, arguing that describing divorce as ba'in is against the legislated nature of divorce, which includes the right of ruju', so the description should be null and the divorce remains raj'i. The Hanafis counter that divorce itself necessitates separation, and the description merely hastens what will inevitably occur. There are intra-Hanafi ikhtilāfāt: According to a narration from Muhammad, 'ṭalāq al-shayṭān' and 'ṭalāq al-bidʻa' result in raj'i due to anger; likewise Abu Yusuf held that similes like 'like a mountain' are raj'i. Muhammad also held that 'like a thousand' results in three divorces even without intention (like saying 'like the number of a thousand'), and 'like a hundred' similarly. For 'like stars', one divorce; 'like the number of stars', three. Abu Yusuf and Zufar held that 'long' and 'wide' are null descriptions, so the divorce is raj'i. The passage uses the Quranic verse (2:228) to show that 'afʻal' (comparative/superlative) can be used merely as a descriptive adjective (e.g., 'ahaqqu' meaning 'deserving'), not necessarily implying multiplicity.

Key Terms
بائن (bā'in): Irrevocable divorce; the marriage is completely severed, and the husband cannot take her back without a new marriage.
رجعة (ruj'a): Reconciliation or taking back the wife during the 'iddah after a revocable divorce.
البتة (al-battah): An expression meaning 'permanently' or 'final', derived from 'batta' (to cut decisively). It indicates an irrevocable divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a man says to his wife 'You are divorced like a mountain', what is the ruling?
A) One revocable divorce (raj'i)
B) One irrevocable divorce (ba'in)
C) Three divorces
D) The divorce is invalid due to ambiguity

Key Takeaway
When a husband describes divorce with words of finality or severity, the Hanafi school rules it as one irrevocable divorce (ba'in), differing from the Shafi'i view.
