When Describing Talaq Changes Its Legal Type: Ba'in vs Raj'i
Explaining the legal effect of adjectives on talaq classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between adjectives that make talaq ba'in and those that keep it raj'i, and explain the Hanafi proof using the Quranic verse.

Core Idea
Adjectives of intensity trigger immediate ba'in; adjectives of moderation leave talaq raj'i unless three is intended.

This lesson examines a fascinating point of Hanafi talaq law: the adjectives a husband attaches to the word 'talaq' can change the legal nature of the divorce. The Hanafi school holds that descriptions of intensity (like 'most foul') cause immediate irrevocability, while the Shafi'i school considers such descriptions void. Let’s explore the reasoning and the evidence from Quran and analogy.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi Position
Identify the rule that adjectives implying intensity (e.g., 'most foul', 'strongest', 'like a mountain') make the talaq ba'in (irrevocable). Adjectives implying moderation (e.g., 'most beautiful') leave it raj'i unless the husband intended three.
The description is taken as a modification of the divorce's effect.
2
Identify the Opposing School and Their View
Learn that Imam al-Shafi'i holds that such adjectives are void because talaq is by nature revocable; describing it as ba'in contradicts the Shar'i purpose.
Shafi'i considers them meaningless, so the talaq remains raj'i.
3
Follow the Opponent's Proof
Examine the Shafi'i argument: the Sharī'ah established talaq with the possibility of return (raj'ah), so any description that negates that is invalid, similar to saying 'divorced on condition that I have no right to take you back'.
This is based on the original design of talaq.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Response
Understand the Hanafi proof: talaq in its essence causes ba'in; the delay until 'iddah is a Shar'i concession. When the husband specifies ba'in via description, it reverts to the original rule. Also, the Quranic verse 2:228 uses 'أحق' (af'alu form) as a mere description, not a superlative, supporting that such forms can be descriptive.
Dalil: Quran and qiyas (analogy to the original essence of talaq).
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Difference)
Hanafi: wife becomes immediately haram until she marries another; husband cannot take her back. Shafi'i: husband can take her back during 'iddah (if consummated). Note also intra-Hanafi differences for specific phrases (e.g., 'like a thousand', 'divorce of innovation').
The difference in legal outcome is significant for the wife's status.

(مستخلص، فتح)

Commentary
This passage discusses the legal effect of various adjectives or descriptions attached to the word 'talaq'. The core ruling is that any adjective implying intensity (like 'most foul', 'strongest') makes the talaq ba'in (irrevocable) immediately. If the adjective implies moderation (like 'most beautiful', 'best'), the talaq remains raj'i (revocable) unless he intends three. The commentator defends the Hanafi position by citing the Quranic verse 'وَبُعُولَتُهُنَّ أَحَقُّ بِرَدِّهِنَ' (2:228) where 'أحق' means 'having more right', not 'most right' in a literal sense. Thus, 'أفعل' here is not for superiority but as a mere description. The Hanafi school holds that such adjectives make the divorce ba'in because the description itself indicates immediate separation. The Shafi'i school argues that these adjectives are void because the Sharī'ah designed talaq to be followed by the possibility of return, so describing it as ba'in contradicts the nature of Shar'i talaq. The Hanafi response is that talaq in its essence causes ba'in, but Sharī'ah delays it unless specified. Intra-Hanafi differences are noted: Imam Muhammad holds that 'divorce of Satan' or 'divorce of innovation' yields one raj'i talaq due to doubt; Abu Yusuf considers 'like a mountain' as raj'i; Muhammad rules that 'like a thousand' gives three talaq without intention, as it implies number. For 'long' or 'wide', Abu Yusuf and Zufar consider them raj'i, but the relied-upon position is that they mean 'great' so they cause ba'in. The passage ends with a note that for 'divorce of innovation', Abu Yusuf and Muhammad require intention for ba'in.

Key Terms
أفحش الطلاق (afḥash al-ṭalāq): The most foul or worst divorce; an adjective describing the divorce as extremely severe.
بائنة (bāʾinah): Irrevocable divorce; the wife becomes completely separated and cannot be taken back except through a new marriage.
رجعية (rajʿiyyah): Revocable divorce; the husband can take the wife back during the 'iddah without a new contract.

Key Takeaway
Adjectives of intensity make talaq ba'in in Hanafi law, based on the Quranic use of 'أحق' as a description, not a superlative.

Triple Talaq and Sequential Talaq Before Consummation
Explaining the effect of triple and sequential talaq before consummation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the Hanafi ruling on triple talaq in one utterance and sequential talaq before consummation, and explain the legal reasoning behind the difference.

Core Idea
Before consummation, the first divorce makes her irrevocably separate, so subsequent divorces are ineffective.

This lesson examines a critical distinction in Hanafi divorce law: the effect of pronouncing three divorces at once versus sequentially before the marriage is consummated. The ruling hinges on the unique status of the non-consummated wife (ghayr al-mawṭūʾah). By understanding the reasoning, you will appreciate how the Hanafi school applies the principle that once a woman becomes ba'in (irrevocably separated), further pronouncements of divorce have no legal effect.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
First, understand the difference between pronouncing three divorces in one utterance (e.g., 'You are divorced three times') and pronouncing them sequentially (e.g., 'You are divorced, you are divorced, you are divorced').
2
State the Hanafi ruling for each
For triple in one utterance: all three occur, and she becomes ba'in. For sequential: only the first occurs, making her ba'in, and the rest are void.
This is based on the practice of Ali and Ibn Mas'ud (may Allah be pleased with them).
3
Understand the reasoning
The first talaq makes her a complete stranger (ajnabiyya), so subsequent pronouncements find her as a non-wife and thus have no effect.
Each sequential utterance is an independent act; the first removes the subject.
4
Compare with opposing schools
Malik, Ahmad, and al-Shafi'i (in his old opinion) hold that if the utterances are joined by a conjunction (عطف), all three count because the conjunction links them into a single statement.
The Hanafi response: conjunction does not override the legal effect of the first talaq making her a stranger.
5
Note the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
Hanafi: wife is separated by one talaq only (ba'in). Opposing: wife is divorced three times, with stricter consequences.
This affects the possibility of remarriage and other legal effects.

Chapter on divorce before consummation: If a man divorces his non-consummated wife three times, they all occur together. If he separates (makes sequential divorces), she becomes separated by one. And if she dies after the utterance...
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage discusses talaq before consummation (ghayr al-mawtu'a). The core ruling: if a man pronounces three talaq in one phrase (e.g., 'You are divorced three times'), all three occur and the wife becomes irrevocably separated. However, if he pronounces them separately (e.g., 'You are divorced, you are divorced, you are divorced'), only the first takes effect because after the first, the wife becomes ba'in, and subsequent talaq find her as a stranger. This is the Hanafi position, supported by the practice of Ali and Ibn Mas'ud. The opposing view of Malik, Ahmad, and Shafi'i (old) is that with 'atf (conjunction) all three occur. The passage also covers the case of death after the utterance: if the husband says 'You are divorced' intending to add a number but the wife dies before he mentions the number, no talaq occurs and he inherits from her. If the husband dies instead, one talaq occurs because the utterance 'You are divorced' is complete and operative.

Key Terms
غير الموطوءة (ghayr al-mawṭūʾah): A wife who has not been sexually consummated, including both actual and virtual (such as valid seclusion).
بانت بواحدة (bānat bi-wāḥidah): She becomes separated by one divorce; i.e., the first divorce makes her ba'in (irrevocably separated).
فرق (farraqa): To separate the divorces, meaning to pronounce them separately (e.g., one after another).

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Three
B) Two
C) One
D) None

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, sequential talaq before consummation results in only one talaq because the first makes her irrevocably separated.

Three Divorces Before Consummation: Collective vs. Separate Utterance
Ruling on three divorces before consummation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on three divorces before consummation and explain why the number of utterances and their conjunction determines whether all three fall or only one.

Core Idea
Three divorces before consummation fall collectively; separate utterances yield only one.

Divorce before consummation has distinct rulings: half the mahr, no 'iddah, and—as this passage clarifies—the effect of uttering three divorces depends on how they are said. The Hanafi school draws a sharp line between saying 'you are divorced three' (all three fall) and saying 'you are divorced, you are divorced, you are divorced' (only the first falls). This lesson unpacks the reasoning, the reliance on the practice of Companions, and the key ikhtilāf with other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? The pronouncement of three divorces before consummation. The ruling: they all fall if uttered collectively; only one falls if uttered separately.
The ruling is about waqū‘ (occurrence) of divorce, not a level of obligation.
2
Locate the dalīl
What proof does the author cite? The Hanafi proof is that the number is part of the utterance: ‘you are divorced three’ is estimated as ‘three divorces’, so all three occur. The school relies on the practice of ‘Alī and Ibn Mas‘ūd.
The text says ‘our madhhab is the madhhab of ‘Alī and Ibn Mas‘ūd’.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalālah
How does the proof yield the ruling? The word ‘three’ qualifies the divorce; it is not a separate sentence. Hence the triple pronouncement is a single act of divorce with three effects. For separate utterances, each is an independent occurrence, but the second finds her already separated (bā’in), so it does not fall.
Contrast with the view of Hasan al-Basrī who says the number ‘three’ meets her after she is already divorced, so only one falls.
4
Note the ikhtilāf
Which schools disagree? Mālik, Ahmad, Ibn Abī Laylā, Rabī‘ah, and al-Shāfi‘ī (old view) hold that if the divorces are separated with a conjunction (e.g., ‘you are divorced, and you are divorced, and you are divorced’), all three fall. Hasan al-Basrī holds that even with ‘you are divorced three’ only one falls.
The ikhtilāf hinges on whether each utterance is independent or part of a single declaration.
5
Evaluate the tarjīḥ
Which position is muftā bihī and why? The Hanafi madhhab relies on the transmission of Imām Muhammad from ‘Alī and Ibn Mas‘ūd, and this is the operating position for fatwā. The later commentators (al-‘Aynī, Fatḥ al-Qadīr) confirm this.
The thamarah al-khilāf (practical consequence) is that under the Hanafi view the woman becomes irrevocably separated (baynūnah kubrā) after three collective divorces and must leave without waiting period; under Hasan al-Basrī’s view she would be separated by only one (baynūnah ṣughrā) and could remarry after a new contract.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

بانت بواحدة: [وهي الأولى ولم يقع بالثانية والثالثة شيء. Section on divorce before consummation. It means concerning its rulings. If a man divorces his wife who has not been consummated three times, they all fall; but if he separates (i.e., says them separately), she becomes separated by one. And if she dies after the utterance... Meaning three divorces collectively. Meaning the woman. That is, if a man says to his wife, 'You are divorced,' if he did not intend three, then only one falls; because if he intended three, three fall; because separation (baynuna) is of two types: severe and light, and whichever he intends, his intention is valid. But if he did not intend anything, the lesser is established due to certainty. However, three words are excepted: 'a severe divorce,' 'a long divorce,' 'a wide divorce' – in these three, the intention of three is not valid because the word does not allow it. The 'taa' of 'tatliqa' is placed for singularity. (al-'Ayni, Fath) 'Unconsummated': by 'unconsummated' he means that which includes actual and legal. (Fath) 'They all fall': i.e., the three, because it is the utterance of an omitted infinitive estimated as 'three divorces' – so they fall collectively. According to Hasan al-Basri, one falls. (al-'Ayni) The madhhab of Hasan is that she becomes separated by his saying 'You are divorced not in a waiting period,' and his saying 'three' meets her as a non-related woman. Our proof: whenever the number is mentioned, the fall is by the number. 'Separated': i.e., he separates the divorce by saying 'You are divorced, one, and one, and one' or 'You are divorced, divorced, divorced' etc. (Tabari, al-'Ayni) 'She becomes separated by one': meaning the first falls and nothing falls by the second and third. (Tabari) Because each word is a separate utterance; the first falls immediately and the second finds her already separated. According to Malik and Ahmad: three fall if with conjunction, and it is the view of Ibn Abi Layla, Rabi'ah, and al-Shafi'i in the old view. (Rumi al-Haqa'iq) And it is restricted to the unconsummated because with the consummated all fall even with separation. (Mustakhlas)
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage is a ruling and commentary on the effect of uttering three divorces before consummation. The ruling is: if a man says to his non-consummated wife 'you are divorced' three times collectively (e.g., 'you are divorced three'), all three fall and she becomes irrevocably separated. If he says them separately (e.g., 'you are divorced, you are divorced, you are divorced'), the first one falls and she becomes separated by that one, and the subsequent utterances have no effect because she is already a non-marriageable woman. The commentary expands on the nuances: the husband's intention matters – if he intends three, three fall; if not, only one falls based on certainty that the lower number is intended. It also notes exceptions for words like 'severe divorce' where the word does not allow the intention of three due to linguistic form. The passage then records an ikhtilaf: Imam Hasan al-Basri held only one falls even with the word 'three', but the Hanafi school follows the majority of Companions (Ali and Ibn Mas'ud) that all three fall as the number is part of the utterance. Another ikhtilaf with Malik and others: if the divorces are given separately with a conjunction, they uphold that all three fall, while Hanafis say only one falls because each utterance is an independent occurrence. The commentary also restricts this ruling to before consummation; after consummation, all three fall even if separated.

Key Terms
الطلاق (al-talaq): Divorce, the dissolution of marriage by the husband's pronouncement.
قبل الدخول (qabl al-dukhul): Before the couple have been alone together or had sexual intercourse (not consummated).
بينونة (baynuna): Separation, the legal effect of divorce making spouses non-marriageable.
نية (niyyah): Intention of the husband that qualifies the default meaning of his divorce utterance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a man says to his unconsummated wife, 'You are divorced three,' how many divorces occur according to the Hanafī madhhab?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
Three divorces before consummation fall collectively if the number is attached; separate utterances yield only one divorce because the first makes her irrevocably separated.

Death After Initiating Divorce Before Mentioning Number
Ruling on wife dying after uttering 'you are divorced' but before number

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on divorce when one spouse dies after the utterance of 'talaq' but before the number is mentioned, and explain the reasoning based on the role of the number.

Core Idea
The number determines the divorce; if the wife dies before it, no divorce occurs; if the husband dies first, one divorce falls.

This lesson examines a nuanced case in Hanafi divorce law where the initiation of divorce is interrupted by death. The ruling hinges on whether the number of divorces has been articulated. The passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq provides the ruling and the reasoning, including an analogy that illuminates the principle.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage examines two cases: (1) wife dies after 'iqa' before number, and (2) husband dies after 'iqa' before number. The ruling differs.
2
Grasp the core principle
The number is what actually causes the divorce. The utterance 'you are divorced' is not effective until the number is specified.
3
Apply to wife's death
She died before number, so the subject (her legal capacity to receive divorce) is gone. Thus nothing falls; the utterance is void (laghw).
The mahr is not halved and the husband inherits from her.
4
Contrast with husband's death
The word 'talaq' itself is operative. When husband dies, the number is not mentioned, but the word stands alone and causes one divorce to fall.
The analogy of someone covering the mouth supports this: the word 'talaq' is sufficient for one divorce when no number is added.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو ماتت بعد الإيقاع: أي لو أراد الزوج أن يقول لامرأته: أنت طالق واحدة أو ثنتين أو ثلاثاً، فماتت بعد قوله: "أنت طالق" قبل ذكر العدد أي الواحد أو ثنتين لم يقع شيء ولغا الإيقاع، فلا يتنصف المهر ويرث الزوج منها، ووجه كون الإيقاع لغواً أن الواقع هو العدد، فإذا ماتت قبل ذكره بطل المحل، وقيد بموتها؛ إذ بموت الزوج قبل ذكر العدد يقع واحدة؛ لأنه وصل الطلاق بذكر العدد في موت المرأة وذكر العدد حصل بعد موتها، وفي موت الزوج ذكر لفظ الطلاق ولم يتصل به ذكر العدد، فبقي قوله : أنت طالق"، وهو عامل بنفسه في وقوع الطلاق، ألا ترى أنه لو قال لامرأته: "أنت طالق" يريد أن يقول: "ثلاثاً" فأخذ رجل فاه فلم يقل شيئاً بعد ذكر الطلاق تقع واحدة؛ قبل العدد لغا.
And if she dies after the utterance: i.e., if the husband intended to say to his wife 'You are divorced once or twice or three times,' but she died after his saying 'You are divorced' before mentioning the number, then nothing falls and the utterance becomes void; so the mahr is not halved and the husband inherits from her. The reason the utterance is void is that what actually occurs is the number; when she dies before its mention, the subject (the wife) is gone. It is restricted to her death because if the husband dies before mentioning the number, one divorce falls; because in the wife's death, the utterance of divorce was connected to the number, but the number occurred after her death. In the husband's death, he uttered the word 'divorce' without connecting it to the number, so his saying 'you are divorced' remains operative in causing divorce. Do you not see that if he said to his wife 'you are divorced' intending to say 'three', and a man grabbed his mouth so he said nothing after mentioning the divorce, one divorce falls. Before the number is nullified.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage deals with the scenario where a husband begins to divorce his wife by saying 'you are divorced' but dies or the wife dies before the number is mentioned. The ruling: if the wife dies after hearing 'you are divorced' but before hearing the number, no divorce occurs; the utterance is void. The mahr is not halved, and the husband inherits from her. The reasoning is that the actual divorce is tied to the number; if the subject (wife) is no longer alive when the number is mentioned, the divorce cannot occur. However, if the husband dies first after saying 'you are divorced' but before the number, one divorce does occur because the word 'divorced' itself is effective and the number was not yet mentioned. The commentary gives an analogy: if a man says 'you are divorced' intending to say 'three' but someone covers his mouth so he stops, one divorce falls because the word itself is operative. This is contrasted with the wife's death where the number is mentioned after her death, so nothing falls.

Key Terms
إيقاع (iqa'): The act of pronouncing divorce, specifically the utterance that initiates the divorce process.
لغو (laghw): Void; the utterance has no legal effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Three divorces fall because he intended them.
B) One divorce falls because the word 'talaq' itself is effective.
C) No divorce occurs; the utterance is void.
D) The marriage continues until he completes the number.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the number of divorces is essential; if the wife dies before the number is stated, no divorce occurs, but if the husband dies first, one divorce falls.

Divorce with Temporal Words: One, Before, After, With
Ruling with proof on divorce temporal words

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for divorce utterances containing temporal words (before, after, with) and explain how the presence or absence of a pronoun determines the number of divorces that occur.

Core Idea
When divorce is coupled with a number, the occurrence is by the number—temporal words describe the first or last mention depending on the pronoun, dictating one or two divorces.

In this passage, the author of Kanz al-Daqa'iq presents a series of divorce utterances that include temporal words such as 'before', 'after', and 'with'. The ruling distinguishes between when these words are connected to a pronoun (e.g., 'before her one') and when they are not. The key principle: temporal words describe the first mentioned if no pronoun, and the last mentioned if a pronoun is present. This grammatical precision affects whether one or two divorces occur.

Guided Path
1
Identify the utterances and their categories
List the four scenarios: 'one and one' (واحدة وواحدة), 'one after one' (واحدة بعد واحدة), 'one before her one' (واحدة قبلها واحدة), 'one with one' / 'one with her one' (واحدة مع واحدة / واحدة معها واحدة). Note that all scenarios assume the wife is unconsummated (ghayr madkhul biha).
2
Apply the temporal word rule
Recall the Hanafi rule: a temporal word (zarf) like 'before' or 'after' that falls between two mentions describes the first mention if it has no attached pronoun; if it has a pronoun (e.g., 'qabla-ha'), it describes the last mention. For 'with' (ma'a), it always indicates conjunction regardless of the pronoun.
Arabic: قبل بدون ضمير يصف الأول، بضمير يصف الآخر.
3
Determine the number of divorces
For 'one and one': only one divorce falls because the first makes her ba'in, so the second finds no subject. For 'one after one' (no pronoun): 'after' describes the first, so the first divorce occurs now, the second occurs before it (in the past), but they occur together → two divorces. For 'one before her one' (pronoun): 'beforeness' describes the last mention, so the last divorce occurs in the past, the first now → both occur together → two divorces. For 'one with one' or 'one with her one': 'with' indicates simultaneity → both occur together → two divorces.
The key is that the second divorce is not dependent on the first's subject remaining.
4
Account for the ikhtilaf
Imam al-Shafi'i held that in 'one before her one' only one divorce falls (or nothing, in one narration). Imam Abu Yusuf held that in 'one with her one' only one divorce falls because the pronoun (ha) requires its referent to exist first. The Hanafi majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) and the relied-upon view are that two divorces fall in all four scenarios.
The dispute is based on the interpretation of the pronoun's effect.
5
State the mufta bihi
The operative ruling in the Hanafi madhhab is that two divorces occur in all four scenarios: 'one after one', 'one before her one', 'one with one', and 'one with her one'. This is the position of al-'Ayni, Fath, and the majority of Hanafi scholars.
This is the tarjih: two divorces in each case.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(فتح) لغا: أي كلامه ولم يقع به شيء؛ لأن الواقع هو العدد فإذا ماتت قبل ذكره بطل المحل فلا يقع شيء. (ع) أو بعدها أي أو قال: أنت طالق واحدة بعدها واحدة. (ع) تقع واحدة: اعلم أن مبنى هذه المسائل على أن الظرف إذا وقع بين شيئين و لم يتصل به ضمير كان صفة للمذكور أولا، وإن اتصل به ضمير كان صفة للمذكور آخراً وعلى أن الإقرار بطلاق سابق إيقاع في الحال، ففي الصورة الأولى أي أنت طالق واحدة وواحدة؛ فلأنها بانت بالواحدة فلا يلحقها الثانية؛ لعدم توقف صدر الكلام على آخره فصار كل واحدة إيقاعا على حدة، وأما في قوله أنت طالق واحدة قبل واحدة أو بعدها واحدة؛ فلأن الأصل أن القبلية والبعدية صفة للمذكور أولا إن لم يقرن بالضمير، وإن قرن بها فهو صفة للمذكور آخراً، والقبلية في قوله واحدة قبل واحدة صفة الأولى فوقعت قبل الثانية فلا تلحقها الثانية، والبعدية في قوله: بعدها واحدة صفة الأخيرة فوقعت الأولى قبلها فلا تلحقها الثانية. (فتح)
وفي بعد واحدة إلخ أي وفي قوله: أنت طالق واحدة بعد واحدة أو قال: أنت طالق واحدة قبلها واحدة، أو قال: أنت طالق واحدة معها. (ط، ع) أو مع واحدة أي أو قال: أنت طالق واحدة مع واحدة. ثنتان : [ أي فالواقع في هذه الصور الأربع طلقتان (ع)] أي تطليقتان في الصور الأربع، أما في قوله: واحدة بعد واحدة؛ فلأن البعدية صفة الأولى لخلو الظرف عن الضمير فاقتضى إيقاع الطلاق الواحد للحال وإيقاع الأخرى قبلها فيقترنان وقوعا، وأما في الثانية أي واحد قبلها واحدة؛ فلأن القبلية صفة الأخرى؛ لاقترانها بالضمير فاقتضى إيقاعها في الماضي، والأولى واقعة في الحال، والإيقاع في الماضي إيقاع في الحال فيقترنان وقوعا جملة، وأما في الثالثة أي واحدة مع واحدة، والرابعة أي واحدة معها واحدة؛ فلأن كلمة "مع" للمقارنة سواء اقترنت بالضمير أو لا فيقعان جملة، وضابطة "قبل" و"بعد" في هذا الشعر الفارسي: قبل بے با وبعد با با را
اندر احکام یک طلاق بدان
وعند الشافعي: يقع واحدة في الصورة الثانية، وعنه أنه لا يقع شيء، وعن أبي يوسف في الصورة الأخيرة يقع واحدة؛ لأن الضمير تقتضي سبق المرجع وجوداً. (عيني، فتح، مسكين)
That is, the husband to his wife. Or if he said: 'You are divorced one' – one falls. And in '[one] after one' or 'one before her one' or 'one with one' or 'one with her one' – two fall. Because occurrence is by the word, not by intention. The author mentioned this issue in this chapter even though it is not specific to the unconsummated wife; because it is similar in meaning to what preceded, i.e., loss of subject upon utterance. This issue establishes that when divorce is coupled with a number, the occurrence is by the number, and in it is a refutation of the madhhab of Hasan. (Fath) 'Void' – meaning his speech and nothing occurs; because what occurs is the number, so when she dies before its mention, the subject is lost, so nothing occurs. (al-'Ayni) 'Or after her' – i.e., he said 'You are divorced one after her one.' (al-'Ayni) 'One falls' – know that the foundation of these issues is that when a temporal word falls between two things and is not connected with a pronoun, it is a description of the first mentioned; if connected with a pronoun, it describes the last mentioned. Also, an acknowledgment of a prior divorce is an immediate occurrence. In the first scenario, 'you are divorced one and one' – because she becomes separated by the first, so the second does not affect her; since the first part of the speech does not depend on the last, each becomes an independent occurrence. As for his saying 'you are divorced one before one' or 'after her one' – the principle is that 'beforeness' and 'afterness' describe the first mentioned if not coupled with a pronoun; if coupled, they describe the last. In 'one before one', the 'beforeness' describes the first, so it falls before the second, thus the second does not affect her. In 'after her one', the 'afterness' describes the last, so the first falls before it, thus the second does not affect her. (Fath) And in 'one after one' etc. – that is, four scenarios: 'one after one', 'one before her one', 'one with her one', 'one with one' – two divorces occur. (al-'Ayni) The rationale: in 'one after one', 'afterness' describes the first because the temporal word lacks a pronoun, so it necessitates that one divorce occurs immediately and the other occurs before it – thus they occur together. In the second, 'one before her one', 'beforeness' describes the last due to the pronoun, so it necessitates its occurrence in the past, and the first occurs now – occurrence in the past is equivalent to present occurrence, so they occur together. In the third and fourth, 'one with one' and 'one with her one', the word 'with' indicates conjunction whether or not it has a pronoun, so both occur together. A mnemonic in Persian: 'Before without 'ba' and after with 'ba' in rulings of one divorce know.' According to al-Shafi'i: one falls in the second scenario; from him also that nothing falls. From Abu Yusuf: in the last scenario, one falls because the pronoun necessitates the precedent existence of its referent. (al-'Ayni, Fath, Miskin)
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage presents detailed rules for when a husband uses qualifying temporal words in divorce utterances before consummation. The key ruling: if the husband says 'you are divorced one and one', only one divorce falls because the first utterance effects separation and the second finds her already separated. In contrasts, if he says 'one after one', 'one before her one', 'one with one', or 'one with her one', two divorces fall. The reasoning is based on grammatical analysis: temporal words like 'before' and 'after' either describe the first or last mention depending on whether they are connected to a pronoun. 'With' always indicates conjunction, so both divorces occur simultaneously. The commentary also records a ikhtilaf with al-Shafi'i, who holds one divorce falls in the second scenario, and from him also that nothing falls. In the last scenario, Abu Yusuf holds one divorce falls because the pronoun (ha) requires its referent to exist first. The Hanafi mufta bihi is that two divorces fall in the four mentioned scenarios. A Persian mnemonic is provided for the rules of 'before' and 'after'.

Key Terms
قبل (qabla): A temporal word meaning 'before', used to specify the order of divorce utterances.
بعد (ba'da): A temporal word meaning 'after', similarly used to specify order.
مع (ma'a): Meaning 'with', indicating simultaneity.
ظرف (zarf): A temporal or spatial adverb; here specifically temporal words like 'qabla', 'ba'da', 'ma'a'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi madhhab, when a husband utters divorces with temporal words like 'before', 'after', or 'with', two divorces fall in all scenarios except 'one and one' (one divorce), based on the grammatical rule of pronoun attachment.

Talaq — Divorce

8 min
Conditional Talaq Before Consummation with Conjunctions
Rulings on conditional talaq with multiple statements and conjunctions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the number of divorces that fall in conditional talaq with conjunctions (waw, fa, thumma) for a non-consummated wife, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A conditional divorce is like an immediate divorce upon occurrence; for a non-consummated wife, only one divorce can fall regardless of conjunctions.

This lesson examines a nuanced area of Hanafi fiqh: when a man conditionally divorces his non-consummated wife with multiple statements linked by conjunctions like 'waw' (and). The ruling depends on whether the condition is stated before or after the divorce statements, and on the specific conjunction used. We will explore the principle that a conditional divorce operates like an immediate divorce at the moment of the condition's fulfillment, and how the Sahibayn differ from Abu Hanifa in certain cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and its variables
The passage deals with a non-consummated wife (ghayr madkhul biha) and multiple divorce statements linked by conjunctions (waw, fa, thumma). Note whether the condition is placed before or after the statements.
The wife's status (consummated vs. non-consummated) is key because the iddah period after one divorce allows a second divorce to take effect in consummated marriages.
2
Apply the Hanafi principle of conditional divorce
Recall that in the Hanafi school, a divorce suspended on a condition (al-mu'allaq bi al-shart) is treated as executed (munajjaz) at the moment of the condition's occurrence. This means the same rules apply as if the divorce were stated immediately at that moment.
For a non-consummated wife, an immediate divorce results in only one divorce falling, because after the first divorce, she becomes irrevocably divorced (ba'in), and subsequent divorces have no effect.
3
Analyze the effect of conjunctions
The passage considers four scenarios: (1) condition before statement with 'waw', (2) condition before statement with 'fa', (3) condition before statement with 'thumma', and (4) condition after statement (delayed condition). For each, determine how many divorces fall according to Abu Hanifa.
The Sahibayn differ in the first scenario, holding that two divorces fall because 'waw' implies conjunction or arrangement, causing doubt, so only the first is certain.
4
Identify the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Abu Hanifa holds that only one divorce falls in the 'waw' case, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that two fall. Examine the proofs: Abu Hanifa relies on the principle of conditional = executed; the Sahibayn argue that the letter of conjunction (waw) is like the word 'and' for joining, creating a doubt that prevents the second from occurring.
The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) is that under Abu Hanifa, the husband retains the ability to remarry and can still issue two more divorces; under the Sahibayn, only one more divorce remains possible.
5
Summarize the rulings for all scenarios
Compile the results: for non-consummated wife, condition before statements: with waw → 1 (Abu Hanifa) or 2 (Sahibayn); with fa → 1 by agreement; with thumma → first is conditional, second immediate, third invalid. When condition is delayed (after statements), two divorces fall by agreement. For a consummated wife, two fall in all cases.
The final phrase 'fashatan' indicates that when the condition is delayed, two divorces fall by consensus because the beginning of the speech depends on the end due to the modifier.

إن دخلت فأنت طالق واحدة وواحدةً فدخلت تقع واحدةٌ، وإن أخر الشرط فئتان.
الدار
أي المرأة الدار
إن دخلت [ أي ولو قالت لامرأته: إن دخلت الدار. (ع)] أراد لو قال لامرأته الغير المدخول بها وترك التصريح بذلك؛ لأن الفصل معقود لها؛ أو لأنه لو كانت مدخولا بها لوقع عليها الثنتان كما لو أخر الشرط. وقوله: "تقع واحدة"؛ لأن المعلق بالشرط كالمنجز عند وقوعه، وفي المنجز يقع واحدة، فلا يبقى للثاني والثالث محل، فكذا هنا، وقيد بحرف العطف؛ لأنه لو حذفه وقعت واحدة اتفاقاً عند وجود الشرط، ولغا الباقي لعدم ما يوجب تشريكه معه، وعطف بالواو؛ لأنه لو كان "فاء" وقعت واحدة أيضاً اتفاقاً في الأصح، ولو عطف بـ"ثم" تعلق الأول بالشرط وينجز الثاني ولغا الثالث. (فتح)
تقع واحدة: أي طلقة واحدة عند أبي حنيفة، وعندهما: تقع ثنتان؛ لأن الجمع بحرف الجمع كالجمع بلفظ الجمع؛ ولأن الواو للجمع أو للترتيب فوقع الشك في الوقوع فلا يقع بالشك إلا الأولى. (ع) وإن أخر الشرط بأن قال: أنت طالق واحدة وواحدة إن دخلت الدار. (ط) أي إن قال: أنت طالق واحدة وواحدة إن دخلت الدار، يقع تطليقتان؛ لأن الجزائين يتعلقان بالشرط دفعة فيقعان كذلك، وكذا لو عطف بالفاء، وفي "ثم" يقع واحدة للحال، ويلغو الباقي، ويقع في الموطوءة ثنتان في الكل؛ لوجود العدة. (فتح، طائي) فشتان : فالواقع طلقتان بالاتفاق؛ لأن صدر الكلام يتوقف على آخره لوجود المغير فيه فكان في حكم البيان. (ع)
Book of Divorce. Chapter: Divorce before consummation. If you enter, you are divorced one and one, so she enters, one falls. And if he delays the condition, then two. 'The house' meaning the woman's house. 'If you enter' meaning if he said to his wife who is not consummated: If you enter the house. He intended that he said to his wife who is not consummated and left explicit mention because the chapter is for her; or because if she were consummated, two would fall on her as if he delayed the condition. His statement: 'one falls' because what is suspended on a condition is like what is executed upon its occurrence, and in the executed, one falls, so there is no place for the second and third, so here too. And it is restricted to the conjunction because if he omitted it, one falls by agreement upon existence of the condition, and the rest are invalid due to lack of a cause for sharing. And he joined with 'waw' because if it were 'fa', one falls also by agreement according to the most sound. And if he joined with 'thumma', the first is attached to the condition, the second is executed, and the third is invalid. (Fath) 'One falls' meaning one divorce according to Abu Hanifa, and according to them (the two): two fall because joining with a letter of joining is like joining with a word of joining, and because 'waw' is for conjunction or arrangement, so doubt arises in occurrence, so only the first occurs due to doubt. (Ayn) And if he delays the condition, such as saying: You are divorced one and one if you enter the house, then two divorces fall because the two penalty clauses are attached to the condition simultaneously, so they fall likewise. Similarly if joined with 'fa', and with 'thumma', one falls immediately, and the rest are invalid. And in the case of the one who has been consummated, two fall in all cases due to the existence of the 'iddah. (Fath, Ta'i) 'Fashatan' means two divorces fall by agreement because the beginning of the speech depends on its end due to the presence of a modifier, so it is in the ruling of clarification. (Ayn)
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq with commentary from Fath al-Qadir and al-'Inayah discusses the rulings of conditional talaq before consummation, particularly when multiple divorce statements are made with conjunctions. The author begins by presenting the basic ruling: if a man says to his wife (non-consummated), 'If you enter, you are divorced one and one', and she enters, only one divorce falls. The commentary explains that the phrase 'one and one' is restricted to this form because the intended wife is non-consummated; if she were consummated, two would fall. The reasoning is that a conditional divorce upon occurrence of the condition is like an immediate divorce at that moment, and in an immediate divorce from a non-consummated wife, only one falls, so the second and third have no place. The conjunction 'waw' is specified; if omitted, one falls by agreement. If 'fa' is used, also one falls. If 'thumma' is used, the first is conditional, the second is executed immediately, and the third is invalid. Then the commentary notes a dispute: according to Abu Hanifa, only one falls, but according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn), two fall because the conjunction 'waw' is like the word 'and' for joining, and since 'waw' can imply either conjunction or arrangement, doubt arises, so only the first is certain. The passage then addresses delaying the condition (e.g., 'You are divorced one and one if you enter the house'), where two fall because both penalties are attached to the condition simultaneously. This applies similarly with 'fa', but with 'thumma' only one falls immediately. For a consummated wife, two fall in all cases due to the 'iddah. The final phrase explains that 'fashatan' (two divorces) is agreed upon because the beginning of the speech depends on its end due to a modifier (the word 'fashatan' meaning 'two'? Actually 'fashatan' is a misspelling? The text says 'فشتان' which might mean 'two are different' but the commentary interprets as 'two fall by agreement'). The passage ends with a note that the overall ruling is that when the condition is delayed, two divorces fall by consensus.

Key Terms
المعلق بالشرط (al-mu'allaq bi al-shart): A divorce that is suspended upon a future condition; upon occurrence of the condition, it becomes effective as if it were immediate.
الجزائين (al-jaza'ayn): The two penalty clauses or consequences (i.e., the two divorce statements) that are attached to a condition.
الواو (al-waw): The Arabic conjunction 'and', which can imply simultaneous conjunction or sequential arrangement. Its ambiguity leads to ikhtilaf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a non-consummated wife is told 'If you enter, you are divorced one and one' (with 'waw') and she enters, how many divorces occur according to the Sahibayn?
A) One
B) Two
C) Three
D) None – the condition is invalid

Key Takeaway
For a non-consummated wife, conditional statements with conjunctions usually result in only one divorce under Abu Hanifa, while the Sahibayn differ on the 'waw' case, holding that two fall.

Kinayah in Divorce: Classification and Contextual Rulings
Definition and rulings of kinayah in divorce

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify kinayah expressions in divorce according to the Hanafi school and determine the occurrence of divorce based on the type of expression and situational context.

Core Idea
Divorce by kinayah depends not only on intention but also on the type of word and the situational context (contentment, discussion of divorce, anger).

In this lesson, we explore the Hanafi rules for divorce by indirect expressions (kinayah). Unlike explicit words like 'talaq', kinayah words require either the husband's intention (niyyah) or a strong contextual indication (dilalah al-hal) to effect divorce. The passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, with its commentary from Fath al-Qadir, provides a detailed classification of kinayah words and the three states that influence the ruling. Understanding these categories is essential for correctly applying Hanafi family law.

Guided Path
1
Define kinayah in legal context
Understand that kinayah are words that can imply divorce but are not explicit (sarih). In the Hanafi school, they require either intention or contextual indication to effect divorce.
The definition from al-Fatawa al-Khaniyyah specifies that kinayah is what is possible for divorce and other meanings and is not mentioned as an explicit word.
2
Identify the two determinants: intention (niyyah) and contextual indication (dilalah al-hal)
Know that either can trigger divorce. The passage emphasizes that contextual indication can override the need for intention in specific situations, as it is apparent (zahir) while intention is hidden.
The word 'or' in the text allows for combination; if both are present, it is even more certain.
3
Classify the three types of kinayah based on their functions (response, reply, insult)
Distinguish between words suitable only as a response (jawaab), words suitable as response and insult (sabb), and words suitable as response and reply (radd). This classification determines which words trigger divorce by contextual indication.
Category 1: amruki bi yadik, ikhtari, i'taddi. Category 2: khaliyyah, barriyyah, battah, ba'in, haram. Category 3: ukhruji, ighribi, idhhabi, qumi, taqanna'i.
4
Apply the three situational states (contentment, discussing divorce, anger)
Determine which type of kinayah triggers divorce automatically in each state based on the word's suitability for the context. In contentment, only intention matters. In discussing divorce, words not suitable as reply (categories 1&2) cause divorce. In anger, words not suitable for insult and reply (category 1) cause divorce.
In contentment, the husband's word is accepted with oath regarding no intention.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute on contextual indication
Understand the Shafi'i objection that intention is always required because the husband is free in all states. The Hanafi response is that contextual indication is stronger because it is apparent; the apparent is relied upon unless evidence to the contrary.
This dispute has practical implications: in Hanafi courts, a husband's statement in a divorce discussion is adjudicated without requiring proof of intention.

باب الكنايات: [جمع كناية وهي ما لا يظهر المراد منه إلا بنية، والمراد ههنا ما يحتمل الطلاق ولا يكون مذكورا نصاً. (ط، ع] الكناية عند النحاة واللغويين: أن يعبر عن شيء معين لفظاً كان أو معنى بلفظ غير صريح إما للإبهام على السامع كقولك: "جاءني "فلان" وأنت تريد معيناً أو للشناعة كالهن للفرج أو للاختصار كالضمائر أو لنوع من الفصاحة كـ"فلان" كثير الرماد ، ثم الصريح والكناية من أقسام الحقيقة والمجاز، فالحقيقة التي لم تهجر صريح، والمهجورة التي غلب معناها المجازي كناية والمجاز الغالب الاستعمال صريح وغير الغالب كناية، وفي اصطلاح أهل الأصول لفظ استتر المراد منه في نفسه سواء كان المراد معنى حقيقياً أو مجازياً، وفي "الفتاوى الخانية": الكناية ما يحتمل الطلاق وغيره ولا يكون مذكوراً لفظاً. (فتح)
إلا بالنية أو دلالة الحال أي لا تطلق بالكنايات إلا بأحد هذين الأمرين؛ لأن ألفاظ الكنايات غير مختصة بالطلاق بل تحتمله وغيره فلابد من المرجح ، وإذا وقع الطلاق بالكناية إما بالنية أو دلالة الحال فبالأولى إذا وجد الأمران فعلم أن "أو " في قوله أو دلالة الحال" لمنع الخلو فيجوز الجمع والمفهوم من هذا أن الكنايات كلها يقع بها الطلاق بدلالة الحال وليس كذلك، وإنما يقع ببعضها دون بعض، وذلك أن الأحوال ثلاثة: حالة الرضا، وحالة مذاكرة الطلاق، وحالة الغضب، والكنايات أيضاً ثلاثة أقسام قسم منها يصلح جواباً لسؤال المرأة الطلاق ولا يصلح رداً أي رد كلام المرأة عند سؤالها الطلاق ولا سباً أي شتماً، وهي ثلاثة ألفاظ: أمرك بيدك واختاري واعتدي ومرادفها وقسم يصلح جواباً وشتماً ولا يصلح رداً، وهي خمسة ألفاظ: خلية وبرية وبتة وبائن وحرام ومرادفها، وقسم يصلح جواباً ورداً ولا يصلح وهي خمسة ألفاظ: اخرجي واغربي واذهبي وقومي وتقنعي ومرادفها، ففي حالة الرضا لا يقع الطلاق بشيء منها إلا بالنية. والقول قوله مع يمينه في عدم النية، وفي حالة مذاكرة الطلاق وهي أن تسأله المرأة طلاقها أو يسأله أجنبي يقع في القضاء بكل لفظ لا يصلح للرد، وهو القسم الأول والثاني، ولا يصدق في قوله في عدم النية، وفي حالة الغضب لا يقع بكل لفظ يصلح للسب والرد، وهو القسم الثاني والثالث، ويقع بكل لفظ لا يصلح لهما، بل يصلح للجواب فقط، وهو القسم الأول، وقال الشافعي: لا اعتبار لدلالة الحال، بل لابد من النية؛ لأنه مختار في جميع أحواله، ولا يبعد أن يضمر خلاف الظاهر ولنا أن دلالة الحال أقوى من النية؛ لأنها ظاهرة والنية باطنة. رمز الحقائق: ٢٤٣/١](فتح)

Chapter of Kinayah. Chapter of Kinayah. T D meaning in explaining its rulings. She is not divorced by them except with intention or contextual indication. Meaning the woman by kinayah, meaning the intention of the divorcer. Chapter of Kinayah: [plural of kinayah which is what does not show its intended meaning except by intention, and what is meant here is what is possible for divorce and is not mentioned explicitly. (T, A) Kinayah according to grammarians and linguists: it is to express a specific thing, whether a word or meaning, with a non-explicit word, either to obscure from the listener like saying 'someone came to me' while meaning a specific person, or for obscenity like 'al-han' for private parts, or for abbreviation like pronouns, or for a type of eloquence like 'so-and-so with much ash'. Then the explicit and kinayah are types of reality and metaphor. The reality that is not abandoned is explicit, the abandoned one whose metaphorical meaning is predominant is kinayah, the predominant metaphorical usage is explicit, and the non-predominant is kinayah. In the terminology of the scholars of usul, it is a word whose intended meaning is concealed in itself, whether the intended meaning is real or metaphorical. In al-Fatawa al-Khaniyyah: kinayah is what is possible for divorce and other and is not mentioned as a word. (Fath) 'Except with intention or contextual indication' meaning she is not divorced by kinayah except with one of these two things because the words of kinayah are not specific to divorce but are possible for it and other, so a determinant is needed. And if divorce occurs by kinayah either by intention or contextual indication, then more so if both are found, so it is understood that 'or' in his statement 'or contextual indication' is for preventing absence, so combination is allowed. And the understanding from this is that all kinayah cause divorce by contextual indication, but that is not so. Rather, it occurs with some not others. That is because the situations are three: the state of contentment, the state of discussing divorce, and the state of anger. And the kinayah are also three types: a type that is suitable as a response to the woman's asking for divorce and is not suitable as a reply (i.e., replying to the woman's speech when she asks for divorce) nor as an insult (i.e., abuse). They are three words: 'amruki bi yadik' (your affair is in your hand), 'ikhtari' (choose), and 'i'taddi' (observe your 'iddah) and their synonyms. A type that is suitable as a response and as an insult but not suitable as a reply. They are five words: khaliyyah (free), barriyyah (innocent), battah (definitive), ba'in (separated), and haram (forbidden) and their synonyms. A type that is suitable as a response and as a reply but not suitable as an insult. They are five words: ukhruji (leave), ighribi (go away), idhhabi (go), qumi (stand), taqanna'i (cover your face) and their synonyms. In the state of contentment, divorce does not occur with any of them except with intention, and his word is accepted with his oath regarding the absence of intention. In the state of discussing divorce, which is when the woman asks him for her divorce or a third party asks, it occurs in judicial ruling with every word not suitable as a reply, which is the first and second types, and he is not believed in his statement regarding the absence of intention. In the state of anger, it does not occur with every word suitable for insult and reply, which is the second and third types, and it occurs with every word not suitable for them, rather suitable only for response, which is the first type. And al-Shafi'i said: contextual indication is not considered; rather intention is necessary because he is free in all his states, and it is not unlikely that he conceals something contrary to appearance. And we respond that contextual indication is stronger than intention because it is apparent and intention is hidden. Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 243/1 (Fath) And insult
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition on kinayah (indirect expressions) in divorce from Kanz al-Daqa'iq with commentary from Fath al-Qadir and other sources. It begins by defining kinayah: in the context of divorce, kinayah refers to words that can imply divorce but are not explicit. The definition is given from linguistic, grammatical, and usul perspectives, noting that in al-Fatawa al-Khaniyyah, kinayah is what is possible for divorce and other things and is not mentioned explicitly as a word (i.e., not a direct sarih word like 'talaq'). The core ruling is stated: a divorce by kinayah does not occur except with intention (niyyah) or contextual indication (dilalah al-hal). The commentary explains that 'or' here allows for combination; if both are present, it is even more certain. However, not all kinayah expressions cause divorce through contextual indication alone; it depends on the type of kinayah and the situation. The passage then classifies kinayah into three categories based on their suitability as a response (jawaab) to the wife's request for divorce, as a reply (radd) to her words, and as an insult (sabb). The three categories are: (1) words suitable only as a response (amruki bi yadik, ikhtari, i'taddi and synonyms); (2) words suitable as response and insult but not as reply (khaliyyah, barriyyah, battah, ba'in, haram and synonyms); (3) words suitable as response and reply but not as insult (ukhruji, ighribi, idhhabi, qumi, taqanna'i and synonyms). The situations (ahwal) are also three: contentment (rida), discussing divorce (mudhakarat al-talaq), and anger (ghadab). In contentment, no divorce occurs with any kinayah except with intention, and the husband's word is taken with his oath that he did not intend divorce. In discussing divorce (when the wife or a third party asks for divorce), in judicial ruling, divorce occurs with every word not suitable as a reply (i.e., categories 1 and 2), and the husband's denial of intention is not accepted. In anger, divorce occurs with words not suitable for insult and reply (category 1), but not with words suitable for insult and reply (categories 2 and 3). The passage then records a dispute with al-Shafi'i, who argued that contextual indication is not considered; intention is always necessary because the husband is free in all his states and may conceal his real intent. The Hanafi response is that contextual indication is stronger than intention because it is apparent (zahir) while intention is hidden (batin). The passage ends with a reference to Ramz al-Haqa'iq.

Key Terms
الكناية (al-kinayah): An expression that is not explicit (sarih) for divorce but can imply it; divorce occurs only with intention or contextual indication.
دلالة الحال (dilalah al-hal): Contextual indication or circumstantial evidence that reveals the speaker's intent, based on the situation (e.g., discussing divorce, anger).
جواب (jawaab): Response to the wife's request for divorce.
رد (radd): Reply or rebuttal to the wife's speech about divorce.
سب (sabb): Insult or abuse.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the state of discussing divorce (mudhakarat al-talaq), which categories of kinayah cause divorce by contextual indication alone?
A) Category 1 only (words suitable only as response)
B) Categories 1 and 2 (words not suitable as reply)
C) Categories 2 and 3 (words suitable as insult or reply)
D) All three categories

Key Takeaway
Divorce by kinayah is determined by a combination of word type and situational context; intention is secondary to contextual indication in states of discussing divorce and anger.

Definition of Kinayah and the Basic Rule: Intention or Contextual Indication
Defines kinayah and states the fundamental rule for divorce by allusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of kinayah in divorce and explain why either intention or contextual indication is necessary for divorce to occur.

Core Idea
Kinayah words for divorce require either intention or contextual indication because they are not exclusively divorce terms.

Divorce can be effected with explicit words (sarih) or allusive words (kinayah). Unlike explicit words, kinayah words have multiple possible meanings. Therefore, to determine which meaning is intended, we need a preponderator (murajjih). This lesson introduces the Hanafi rule that the preponderator can be the husband's explicit intention or the contextual situation (dalalat al-hal).

Guided Path
1
Define kinayah in the context of divorce
Identify the definition from the text: a word that accepts divorce and other meanings and is not explicitly mentioned as a divorce word.
2
State the basic rule
The text says: 'لا تطلق بالكنايات إلا بالنية أو دلالة الحال', meaning divorce by kinayah requires either niyyah or dalalat al-hal.
3
Explain why a preponderator is needed
Because kinayah words are not specific to divorce; they have other possible meanings, so we need a factor to make divorce meaning preponderant.
4
Understand the role of 'or'
The 'or' in the rule is not exclusive; if both intention and contextual indication are present, it is even stronger. The rule prevents a situation where neither is present.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الكنايات: [جمع كناية وهي ما لا يظهر المراد منه إلا بنية، والمراد ههنا ما يحتمل الطلاق ولا يكون مذكورا نصاً. (ط، ع] الكناية عند النحاة واللغويين: أن يعبر عن شيء معين لفظاً كان أو معنى بلفظ غير صريح إما للإبهام على السامع كقولك: "جاءني "فلان" وأنت تريد معيناً أو للشناعة كالهن للفرج أو للاختصار كالضمائر أو لنوع من الفصاحة كـ"فلان" كثير الرماد ، ثم الصريح والكناية من أقسام الحقيقة والمجاز، فالحقيقة التي لم تهجر صريح، والمهجورة التي غلب معناها المجازي كناية والمجاز الغالب الاستعمال صريح وغير الغالب كناية، وفي اصطلاح أهل الأصول لفظ استتر المراد منه في نفسه سواء كان المراد معنى حقيقياً أو مجازياً، وفي "الفتاوى الخانية": الكناية ما يحتمل الطلاق وغيره ولا يكون مذكوراً لفظاً. (فتح)
إلا بالنية أو دلالة الحال أي لا تطلق بالكنايات إلا بأحد هذين الأمرين؛ لأن ألفاظ الكنايات غير مختصة بالطلاق بل تحتمله وغيره فلابد من المرجح ، وإذا وقع الطلاق بالكناية إما بالنية أو دلالة الحال فبالأولى إذا وجد الأمران فعلم أن "أو " في قوله أو دلالة الحال" لمنع الخلو فيجوز الجمع والمفهوم من هذا أن الكنايات كلها يقع بها الطلاق بدلالة الحال وليس كذلك، وإنما يقع ببعضها دون بعض، وذلك أن الأحوال ثلاثة: حالة الرضا، وحالة مذاكرة الطلاق، وحالة الغضب، والكنايات أيضاً ثلاثة أقسام قسم منها يصلح جواباً لسؤال المرأة الطلاق ولا يصلح رداً أي رد كلام المرأة عند سؤالها الطلاق ولا سباً أي شتماً، وهي ثلاثة ألفاظ: أمرك بيدك واختاري واعتدي ومرادفها وقسم يصلح جواباً وشتماً ولا يصلح رداً، وهي خمسة ألفاظ: خلية وبرية وبتة وبائن وحرام ومرادفها، وقسم يصلح جواباً ورداً ولا يصلح وهي خمسة ألفاظ: اخرجي واغربي واذهبي وقومي وتقنعي ومرادفها، ففي حالة الرضا لا يقع الطلاق بشيء منها إلا بالنية. والقول قوله مع يمينه في عدم النية، وفي حالة مذاكرة الطلاق وهي أن تسأله المرأة طلاقها أو يسأله أجنبي يقع في القضاء بكل لفظ لا يصلح للرد، وهو القسم الأول والثاني، ولا يصدق في قوله في عدم النية، وفي حالة الغضب لا يقع بكل لفظ يصلح للسب والرد، وهو القسم الثاني والثالث، ويقع بكل لفظ لا يصلح لهما، بل يصلح للجواب فقط، وهو القسم الأول، وقال الشافعي: لا اعتبار لدلالة الحال، بل لابد من النية؛ لأنه مختار في جميع أحواله، ولا يبعد أن يضمر خلاف الظاهر ولنا أن دلالة الحال أقوى من النية؛ لأنها ظاهرة والنية باطنة.
Chapter of Allusions: [Collection of allusion which is what its intended meaning is not apparent except by intention, and what is meant here is what accepts divorce and is not mentioned explicitly. (T, A) Allusion according to grammarians and linguists: to express a specific thing, whether word or meaning, with a non-explicit word, either to obscure from the listener like saying 'So-and-so came' while you intend a specific person, or due to ugliness like 'al-han' for the private part, or for brevity like pronouns, or for a type of eloquence like 'So-and-so is abundant in ashes'. Then explicit and allusion are categories of literal and metaphorical. The literal that is not abandoned is explicit; the abandoned one whose metaphorical meaning is predominant is allusion; the metaphor that is frequently used is explicit; and the non-frequent is allusion. In the terminology of usul scholars, it is a word whose intended meaning is concealed in itself, whether the intended meaning is literal or metaphorical. In al-Fatawa al-Khaniyyah: allusion is what accepts divorce and other than it and is not mentioned as a word. (Fath) Except by intention or contextual indication: i.e., divorce does not occur with allusions except by one of these two matters; because the words of allusions are not specific to divorce but accept it and other than it, so a preponderance is necessary. If divorce occurs by allusion either by intention or contextual indication, then more so if both matters are present. So it is understood that 'or' in his saying 'or contextual indication' is for preventing emptiness, so combination is permissible. The implication from this is that divorce occurs by all allusions through contextual indication, but this is not the case; rather it occurs by some and not others. That is because the states are three: the state of satisfaction, the state of discussing divorce, and the state of anger. And allusions are also three categories: a category that is suitable as a response to the woman's request for divorce and is not suitable as a rejection (i.e., responding to the woman's speech when she asks for divorce) nor as an insult, and these are three words: 'your command is in your hand', 'choose', and 'observe the waiting period' and their synonyms. A category suitable as a response and an insult but not as a rejection, and these are five words: 'khaliyyah', 'bariyyah', 'battah', 'ba'in', and 'haram' and their synonyms. A category suitable as a response and a rejection but not suitable [for insult], and these are five words: 'leave', 'be gone', 'go', 'stand', and 'cover yourself' and their synonyms. In the state of satisfaction, divorce does not occur by any of them except by intention, and his word with his oath is accepted regarding the absence of intention. In the state of discussing divorce, which is when the woman asks him for her divorce or a stranger asks, divorce occurs in the judiciary by every word that is not suitable for rejection, which is the first and second categories, and he is not believed in his statement of absence of intention. In the state of anger, divorce occurs by every word suitable for insult and rejection, which is the second and third categories, and it occurs by every word not suitable for them but rather suitable only for response, which is the first category. Al-Shafi'i said: contextual indication is not considered; rather intention is necessary because he is free in all his states and it is not unlikely that he intends contrary to the apparent. But for us, contextual indication is stronger than intention because it is apparent while intention is hidden.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq defines kinayah (allusion) and states the fundamental rule: divorce by allusive words requires either explicit intention (niyyah) or contextual indication (dalalat al-hal). The commentary explains the linguistic and technical definitions of kinayah, then clarifies that the word 'or' in the ruling allows for both to be present. It then divides situations into three states (rida, mudhakarat al-talaq, ghadab) and allusive words into three categories based on whether they are suitable as a response, insult, or rejection. The ruling for each combination is given: in a state of satisfaction, only intention counts; in discussing divorce, words not suitable for rejection cause divorce irrespective of denial; in anger, words suitable for insult or rejection cause divorce. The passage concludes with a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, who requires intention in all cases, arguing that a person is always free to intend otherwise. The Hanafi response is that contextual indication is stronger because it is apparent (zahir) while intention is hidden (batin). This is a key methodological difference: Hanafis give weight to external context, while Shafi'is prioritize the individual's internal state. The authority_weight is Zahir al-Riwayah as it is from the core text of Kanz al-Daqa'iq.

Key Terms
كناية (kināyah): A word that accepts divorce and other meanings and is not explicitly mentioned as a divorce word.
دلالة الحال (dalālat al-ḥāl): Contextual indication that makes a particular meaning preponderant in a given situation.
نية (niyyah): Intention to effect divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this lesson, why does divorce by kinayah require either intention or contextual indication?
A) Because kinayah words are not divorce terms at all.
B) Because kinayah words have multiple possible meanings, so a preponderator is needed.
C) Because the Prophet forbade easy divorce.
D) Because the wife must consent.

Key Takeaway
Divorce by kinayah is not automatic; it requires either explicit intention (niyyah) or a contextual situation (dalalat al-hal) that points to divorce.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
10 min
The Three States and Three Categories of Kinayah Words
Refines the general rule by classifying states and word categories

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three states (satisfaction, discussing divorce, anger) and three categories of kinayah words, and apply the ruling for each combination.

Core Idea
Divorce by kinayah depends on the husband's emotional state and the word's suitability for response, insult, or rejection.

The general rule that kinayah requires intention or contextual indication is refined by the Hanafi jurists. They observed that not all kinayah words behave the same in all situations. They classified the husband's state into three and the kinayah words into three categories. This lesson details these classifications and the specific rulings that arise from their interaction.

Guided Path
1
Understand the correction of a misconception
The text first corrects the idea that all kinayah words cause divorce by contextual indication. It clarifies that only some do, depending on the state.
2
Learn the three states
State of satisfaction, state of discussing divorce (when wife or third party asks about divorce), and state of anger.
3
Learn the three categories of kinayah words
Category 1: words suitable only as a response (e.g., 'your command is in your hand'). Category 2: suitable as response and insult (e.g., 'khaliyyah'). Category 3: suitable as response and rejection (e.g., 'go').
4
Memorize the rulings for each state-category combination
In satisfaction: only intention matters for all categories. In discussing divorce: words not suitable for rejection (categories 1 and 2) cause divorce without intention. In anger: words suitable for insult/rejection (categories 2 and 3) cause divorce without intention; category 1 also causes divorce.
Note: category 1 always causes divorce in anger because it is not suitable for insult/rejection but is suitable only as a response.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq defines kinayah (allusion) and states the fundamental rule: divorce by allusive words requires either explicit intention (niyyah) or contextual indication (dalalat al-hal). The commentary explains the linguistic and technical definitions of kinayah, then clarifies that the word 'or' in the ruling allows for both to be present. It then divides situations into three states (rida, mudhakarat al-talaq, ghadab) and allusive words into three categories based on whether they are suitable as a response, insult, or rejection. The ruling for each combination is given: in a state of satisfaction, only intention counts; in discussing divorce, words not suitable for rejection cause divorce irrespective of denial; in anger, words suitable for insult or rejection cause divorce. The passage concludes with a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, who requires intention in all cases, arguing that a person is always free to intend otherwise. The Hanafi response is that contextual indication is stronger because it is apparent (zahir) while intention is hidden (batin). This is a key methodological difference: Hanafis give weight to external context, while Shafi'is prioritize the individual's internal state. The authority_weight is Zahir al-Riwayah as it is from the core text of Kanz al-Daqa'iq.

Key Terms
حالة الرضا (ḥālat al-riḍā): State of satisfaction, calm state where no divorce discussion is happening.
حالة مذاكرة الطلاق (ḥālat mudhākarat al-ṭalāq): State of discussing divorce, when divorce is being asked or talked about.
حالة الغضب (ḥālat al-ghaḍab): State of anger.
يصلح جواباً (yaṣluḥu jawāban): Suitable as a response to a request for divorce.
يصلح رداً (yaṣluḥu raddan): Suitable as a rejection, i.e., rejecting the request for divorce.
يصلح سباً (yaṣluḥu sabban): Suitable as an insult.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) State of satisfaction
B) State of discussing divorce
C) State of anger
D) It never causes divorce without intention

Key Takeaway
The ruling for kinayah divorce is not uniform; it depends on the husband's emotional state and the word's semantic suitability.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate

The Hanafi-Shafi'i Dispute on Contextual Indication (Dalalat al-Hal)
Presents the inter-madhhab dispute on the validity of contextual indication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state both the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on dalalat al-hal in divorce kinayah and evaluate the Hanafi response.

Core Idea
Hanafis consider contextual indication stronger than intention; Shafi'is require intention in all cases.

One of the most significant methodological differences between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools is the weight given to external context (dalalat al-hal). In the chapter of divorce by kinayah, this difference has concrete consequences. This lesson presents the arguments of both schools as recorded in Kanz al-Daqa'iq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Shafi'i position
Al-Shafi'i argues that contextual indication is not valid; intention is always required because a person is free to act contrary to appearances.
2
Identify the Hanafi position
Hanafis argue that contextual indication is stronger than intention because it is apparent (zahir) while intention is hidden (batin).
3
Understand the reasoning
Hanafis base their view on the principle that the default is actions match the context; otherwise, harm could come to the wife if the husband denies intention. Shafi'is prioritize the individual's free choice.
4
Recognize the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
In a state of anger, Hanafis rule divorce by words like 'go' without intention; Shafi'is rule no divorce unless he admits intention or proof of intent.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(ط، ع] الكناية عند النحاة واللغويين: أن يعبر عن شيء معين لفظاً كان أو معنى بلفظ غير صريح إما للإبهام على السامع كقولك: "جاءني "فلان" وأنت تريد معيناً أو للشناعة كالهن للفرج أو للاختصار كالضمائر أو لنوع من الفصاحة كـ"فلان" كثير الرماد ، ثم الصريح والكناية من أقسام الحقيقة والمجاز، فالحقيقة التي لم تهجر صريح، والمهجورة التي غلب معناها المجازي كناية والمجاز الغالب الاستعمال صريح وغير الغالب كناية، وفي اصطلاح أهل الأصول لفظ استتر المراد منه في نفسه سواء كان المراد معنى حقيقياً أو مجازياً، وفي "الفتاوى الخانية": الكناية ما يحتمل الطلاق وغيره ولا يكون مذكوراً لفظاً. (فتح)
إلا بالنية أو دلالة الحال أي لا تطلق بالكنايات إلا بأحد هذين الأمرين؛ لأن ألفاظ الكنايات غير مختصة بالطلاق بل تحتمله وغيره فلابد من المرجح ، وإذا وقع الطلاق بالكناية إما بالنية أو دلالة الحال فبالأولى إذا وجد الأمران فعلم أن "أو " في قوله أو دلالة الحال" لمنع الخلو فيجوز الجمع والمفهوم من هذا أن الكنايات كلها يقع بها الطلاق بدلالة الحال وليس كذلك، وإنما يقع ببعضها دون بعض، وذلك أن الأحوال ثلاثة: حالة الرضا، وحالة مذاكرة الطلاق، وحالة الغضب، والكنايات أيضاً ثلاثة أقسام قسم منها يصلح جواباً لسؤال المرأة الطلاق ولا يصلح رداً أي رد كلام المرأة عند سؤالها الطلاق ولا سباً أي شتماً، وهي ثلاثة ألفاظ: أمرك بيدك واختاري واعتدي ومرادفها وقسم يصلح جواباً وشتماً ولا يصلح رداً، وهي خمسة ألفاظ: خلية وبرية وبتة وبائن وحرام ومرادفها، وقسم يصلح جواباً ورداً ولا يصلح وهي خمسة ألفاظ: اخرجي واغربي واذهبي وقومي وتقنعي ومرادفها، ففي حالة الرضا لا يقع الطلاق بشيء منها إلا بالنية. والقول قوله مع يمينه في عدم النية، وفي حالة مذاكرة الطلاق وهي أن تسأله المرأة طلاقها أو يسأله أجنبي يقع في القضاء بكل لفظ لا يصلح للرد، وهو القسم الأول والثاني، ولا يصدق في قوله في عدم النية، وفي حالة الغضب لا يقع بكل لفظ يصلح للسب والرد، وهو القسم الثاني والثالث، ويقع بكل لفظ لا يصلح لهما، بل يصلح للجواب فقط، وهو القسم الأول، وقال الشافعي: لا اعتبار لدلالة الحال، بل لابد من النية؛ لأنه مختار في جميع أحواله، ولا يبعد أن يضمر خلاف الظاهر ولنا أن دلالة الحال أقوى من النية؛ لأنها ظاهرة والنية باطنة.
Chapter of Allusions: [Collection of allusion which is what its intended meaning is not apparent except by intention, and what is meant here is what accepts divorce and is not mentioned explicitly. (T, A) Allusion according to grammarians and linguists: to express a specific thing, whether word or meaning, with a non-explicit word, either to obscure from the listener like saying 'So-and-so came' while you intend a specific person, or due to ugliness like 'al-han' for the private part, or for brevity like pronouns, or for a type of eloquence like 'So-and-so is abundant in ashes'. Then explicit and allusion are categories of literal and metaphorical. The literal that is not abandoned is explicit; the abandoned one whose metaphorical meaning is predominant is allusion; the metaphor that is frequently used is explicit; and the non-frequent is allusion. In the terminology of usul scholars, it is a word whose intended meaning is concealed in itself, whether the intended meaning is literal or metaphorical. In al-Fatawa al-Khaniyyah: allusion is what accepts divorce and other than it and is not mentioned as a word. (Fath) Except by intention or contextual indication: i.e., divorce does not occur with allusions except by one of these two matters; because the words of allusions are not specific to divorce but accept it and other than it, so a preponderance is necessary. If divorce occurs by allusion either by intention or contextual indication, then more so if both matters are present. So it is understood that 'or' in his saying 'or contextual indication' is for preventing emptiness, so combination is permissible. The implication from this is that divorce occurs by all allusions through contextual indication, but this is not the case; rather it occurs by some and not others. That is because the states are three: the state of satisfaction, the state of discussing divorce, and the state of anger. And allusions are also three categories: a category that is suitable as a response to the woman's request for divorce and is not suitable as a rejection (i.e., responding to the woman's speech when she asks for divorce) nor as an insult, and these are three words: 'your command is in your hand', 'choose', and 'observe the waiting period' and their synonyms. A category suitable as a response and an insult but not as a rejection, and these are five words: 'khaliyyah', 'bariyyah', 'battah', 'ba'in', and 'haram' and their synonyms. A category suitable as a response and a rejection but not suitable [for insult], and these are five words: 'leave', 'be gone', 'go', 'stand', and 'cover yourself' and their synonyms. In the state of satisfaction, divorce does not occur by any of them except by intention, and his word with his oath is accepted regarding the absence of intention. In the state of discussing divorce, which is when the woman asks him for her divorce or a stranger asks, divorce occurs in the judiciary by every word that is not suitable for rejection, which is the first and second categories, and he is not believed in his statement of absence of intention. In the state of anger, divorce occurs by every word suitable for insult and rejection, which is the second and third categories, and it occurs by every word not suitable for them but rather suitable only for response, which is the first category. Al-Shafi'i said: contextual indication is not considered; rather intention is necessary because he is free in all his states and it is not unlikely that he intends contrary to the apparent. But for us, contextual indication is stronger than intention because it is apparent while intention is hidden.
Source pages p. 418

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq defines kinayah (allusion) and states the fundamental rule: divorce by allusive words requires either explicit intention (niyyah) or contextual indication (dalalat al-hal). The commentary explains the linguistic and technical definitions of kinayah, then clarifies that the word 'or' in the ruling allows for both to be present. It then divides situations into three states (rida, mudhakarat al-talaq, ghadab) and allusive words into three categories based on whether they are suitable as a response, insult, or rejection. The ruling for each combination is given: in a state of satisfaction, only intention counts; in discussing divorce, words not suitable for rejection cause divorce irrespective of denial; in anger, words suitable for insult or rejection cause divorce. The passage concludes with a dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i, who requires intention in all cases, arguing that a person is always free to intend otherwise. The Hanafi response is that contextual indication is stronger because it is apparent (zahir) while intention is hidden (batin). This is a key methodological difference: Hanafis give weight to external context, while Shafi'is prioritize the individual's internal state. The authority_weight is Zahir al-Riwayah as it is from the core text of Kanz al-Daqa'iq.

Key Terms
مختار (mukhtār): Free agent, one who acts by choice.
ظاهر (zāhir): Apparent, external context.
باطن (bāṭin): Hidden, internal state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response in this lesson, why is contextual indication stronger than intention?
A) Because intention is harder to prove.
B) Because contextual indication is apparent (zahir) and intention is hidden (batin).
C) Because the Prophet said so.
D) Because the wife can testify.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts contextual indication (dalalat al-hal) as a valid preponderator in divorce kinayah, arguing it is stronger than hidden intention, unlike the Shafi'i school which requires explicit intention in all cases.

Talaq — Divorce
