Delegation of Divorce: 'Choose' Yields One Bain Divorce
Ruling on delegation (tafwid) where husband tells wife 'choose' intending divorce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a husband says 'Choose' intending divorce and the wife chooses herself, and identify the proof (ijma) and the qiyas/istihsan reasoning.

Core Idea
When the husband delegates divorce by saying 'Choose', one bain divorce occurs if she chooses herself, based on Companions' consensus overriding qiyas.

This lesson unpacks a key case in Hanafi divorce law: the delegation of divorce (tafwid) through the word 'Choose'. Understanding why the school rules one bain divorce, not three or revocable, requires seeing how the Companions' consensus (ijma) overrides the logical analogy (qiyas) that would otherwise prevent any divorce. We will walk through the conditions, the proof, and the cross-madhhab differences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central case
The case: Husband says to wife 'Choose' (ikhtārī) intending divorce; the wife chooses herself (e.g., 'I choose myself') in the same sitting. Identify the act (wife's choice) and the legal consequence (one bain divorce).
The Arabic phrase is 'قال لها اختاري ينوي به الطلاق فاختارت في مجلسها بانت بواحدة'.
2
Recognize the conditions and limitations
Notice that the wife must choose herself (not her husband), and the choice must be in the same sitting (the council where she learned of the delegation). If she uses 'or' or chooses her husband, no divorce occurs. If she uses 'and', only the first choice counts.
The condition 'in her sitting' (في مجلسها) is crucial; delay invalidates the delegation.
3
Understand the requirement of intention
Since 'Choose' is a kinayah (implicit expression), the husband must have intended divorce. However, the context of anger or discussing divorce serves as a qarina (indication) that substitutes for intention in court, but not in conscience.
Intention is needed because it is kinayah; the text says 'إنما قيد بالنية؛ لأنه من الكنايات'.
4
Examine the qiyas argument
Qiyas would suggest no divorce occurs because the husband does not own the pronouncement by this word (tamlik is a branch of ownership, and the husband does not own the word 'choose' as a means of divorce). The text states: 'والقياس أن لا يقع شيء وإن نوى الزوج الطلاق'.
This qiyas is based on the principle that one cannot transfer what one does not own.
5
Apply istihsan via ijma of the Companions
The Hanafi school overrides qiyas with istihsan based on the silent consensus (ijma suktuti) of the Companions, who ruled that one divorce occurs. This ijma is binding and establishes the ruling.
The ijma is on one divorce only; attempts to effect more are invalid because 'choice' is not varied like 'ibanah'.

باب تفويض الطلاق لما ذكر ما يوقعه بنفسه بنوعيه أي الصريح كقوله : "طلقي نفسك"، والكناية كقوله: "اختاري" ذكر ما يوقعه غيره بإذنه، وما يوقعه الغير سواء كانت زوجة أو غيرها على ثلاثة أنواع: تفويض وتوكيل ورسالة وألفاظ التفويض ثلاثة تخيير وأمر بيد ومشية، ولما كان الطلاق من الغير على خلاف الأصل ذكره بعد بيان ما هو الأصل. (فتح) ينوي أي حال كونه ناويا بقوله اختاري (ع) فاختارت أي المرأة نفسها، فلو اختارت زوجها لم يقع وخرج الأمر من يدها، ولو عطفت بـ"أو" فقالت: اخترت نفسي أو زوجي لا يقع أيضاً، ولو كان بالواو، فالاعتبار للمقدم ويلغو ما بعده، وقوله: "في مجلسها" أي مجلس علمها بالاختيار مشافهة أو إخباراً، وقوله: "بانت لأن اختيارها نفسها به يتحقق؛ لثبوت اختصاصه بنفسها في البائن دون الرجعي لتمكن الزوج من رجعتها بلا رضاها والقياس أن لا يقع شيء وإن نوى الزوج الطلاق؛ لأن التمليك فرع ملك المملك. والزوج لا يملك الإيقاع بهذه اللفظة حتى لو قال: اخترت نفسي منك، أو اخترتك من نفسي ناوياً لا يقع إلا أنا استحسنا الوقوع باختيارها بإجماع الصحابة ، وهذا الإجماع إجماع سكوتي من قول بعضهم وسكوت غيرهم، والإجماع منعقد على الواحدة، وبقي ما وراءه على الأصل، وإنما قيد بالنية؛ لأنه من الكنايات، ودلالة الحال قائمة مقامها قضاء لا ديانة والواقع به بائن؛ لأنه كناية وعند الشافعي وأحمد: رجعي، والقول قول الزوج مع اليمين في عدم النية والدلالة، وفي سائر الكنايات لا يصدق إلا مع اليمين. (عيني، فتح) بانت وإن لم يكن له نية لا يقع شيء. (ط) ولم تصح : [لأن الاختيار ينبئ عن الخلوص وهو غير متنوع بخلاف البينونة؛ لأنها متنوعة إلى غليظة وخفيفة فأيهما نوى صح.] لأنه ليس بتمليك وضعاً وإنما جعل تمليكاً على خلاف القياس لإجماع الصحابة ؛ ولأن الاختيار لا يتنوع، بخلاف الإبانة؛ لأنها تتنوع يقال: بانت بينونة صغرى وكبرى، وعند مالك: يقع ثلاث بلا نية، وعند الشافعي: يقع ثلاث إذا كان بالنية رمز الحقائق: ٢٤٧/١] (مستخلص)
Chapter of Tafwid al-Talaq (Delegation of Divorce), i.e., explaining its ruling, i.e., to the woman or to the agent. He said to her: 'Choose' intending divorce by it, and she chose in her sitting, she becomes bāʾin by one [divorce], and it is not valid [for more]. i.e., a man said to his wife intending three. i.e., the woman. i.e., the sitting of her knowledge of the choice. 'by one' ; Chapter of Tafwid al-Talaq: When he mentioned what he himself pronounces in two types, i.e., explicit like his saying: 'Divorce yourself', and kinayah like his saying: 'Choose', he mentioned what someone else pronounces with his permission, and what someone else pronounces, whether wife or other, in three types: Tafwid, Tawkil, and Risalah. And the expressions of Tafwid are three: Takhyir (choice), Amr bi-yad (command in your hand), and Mashiyyah (will). Since divorce by another is against the original principle, he mentioned it after explaining the original. (Fath) 'Choose': He began with choice because it is established by explicit reports, and choice is inclination towards good and what is better and more preferable. And the case is set in 'choose yourself' because if he said to her 'choose divorce' and she chose it, it is rajʿī. And the husband cannot revoke the delegation because it is a tamlik (transfer of ownership) not a tawkil (agency). And his statement: 'intending divorce by it' i.e., delegation of divorce, indicates this. He titled the chapter as 'delegation' and did not mention the indication (qarina), relying on what has passed, and the intended indication is the state of discussing divorce and the state of anger. (Fath) 'intending' i.e., being intending by his saying 'choose' (Ayni). 'So she chose' i.e., the woman chose herself. If she chose her husband, nothing occurs and the matter leaves her hand. If she used 'or' and said 'I choose myself or my husband', nothing occurs as well. If it was with 'and' (waw), then consideration is for the first and what follows is void. And his statement: 'in her sitting' i.e., the sitting of her knowledge of the choice, either orally or by information. And his statement: 'she becomes bāʾin' because her choice of herself is realized by it; due to the establishment of her specific connection with herself in bāʾin [divorce] as opposed to rajʿī, because the husband can take her back without her consent. The qiyas is that nothing should occur even if the husband intended divorce because tamlik is a branch of the ownership of the owner, and the husband does not own the pronouncement by this word. So much so that if he said: 'I chose myself from you' or 'I chose you from myself' intending [divorce], nothing occurs. But we apply istihsan for the occurrence by her choice due to the ijma of the Companions. And this ijma is a silent ijma (ijma suktuti) from the statement of some and the silence of others. And the ijma is established on one [divorce], and what is beyond that remains on the original principle. And it is restricted to intention because it is from kinayat. And the indication of the situation (qarinat al-hal) stands in its place in court (qada) but not in conscience (diyana). And what occurs is bāʾin because it is kinayah. According to al-Shafi'i and Ahmad: rajʿī. And the statement is that of the husband with an oath regarding lack of intention and indication. And in other kinayat, he is not believed except with an oath. (Ayni, Fath) 'She becomes bāʾin': and if he had no intention, nothing occurs. (T) 'And it is not correct' [because choice indicates exclusivity and is not varied, unlike bainuna (separation) because it is varied into severe and light, so whichever he intends is valid.] Because it is not a tamlik by nature, rather it is made a tamlik contrary to qiyas due to the ijma of the Companions. And because choice is not varied, unlike ibanah, because it varies: it is said 'she became bain with minor separation and major separation.' And according to Malik: three occur without intention, and according to al-Shafi'i: three occur if with intention. (Ramz al-Haqa'iq: 1/247) (Mukhtasar)
Source pages p. 422

Commentary
This passage from the chapter on Tafwid al-Talaq (delegation of divorce) addresses the case where a husband says to his wife 'Choose' (ikhtārī) intending divorce, and the wife chooses herself (i.e., indicates divorce) in the same sitting. The Hanafi ruling is that she becomes bāʾin (irrevocably divorced) by one divorce. The ruling does not apply if she chooses her husband or uses a disjunctive 'or', and it requires that the choice be made in the council where she learned of the delegation. The passage explains that this is a form of tamlik (transfer of ownership) not tawkil (agency), so the husband cannot revoke. It then addresses the issue of intention: since 'choose' is a kinayah (implicit expression), the husband must have intended divorce; the context of anger or discussing divorce serves as a qarina (indication) that substitutes for intention in court but not in conscience. The ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference) overriding qiyas, because qiyas would suggest that no divorce occurs since the husband does not own the pronouncement of divorce by this word. However, the Companions' consensus (ijma suktuti) establishes that one divorce occurs. The passage also notes that according to Shafi'i and Ahmad, the divorce is rajʿī (revocable), not bāʾin. Additionally, it mentions that according to Malik, three divorces occur without intention, and according to Shafi'i, three occur if intention is present. The Hanafi position restricts to one divorce because 'choice' is not varied, unlike 'ibanah' which can be minor or major. The wife's choice of herself is decisive, while choosing the husband has no effect. The ruling is limited to one bāʾin divorce unless the husband intended more, but since the word does not indicate multiplicity, only one is valid.

Key Terms
تفويض (tafwīḍ): Delegation of the power to pronounce divorce by the husband to the wife or another person.
اختيار (ikhtiyār / ikhtārī): The wife's act of choosing herself (i.e., indicating divorce) when the husband says 'Choose' (ikhtārī). It is a kinayah requiring intention.
تمليك (tamlīk): Transfer of ownership, specifically the husband transferring the right to pronounce divorce to the wife as an owner.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what happens if a husband says 'Choose' (ikhtārī) intending divorce, and the wife says 'I choose myself' in the same sitting?
A) Three bain divorces occur.
B) One bain divorce occurs.
C) One rajʿī divorce occurs.
D) No divorce occurs because the husband did not pronounce divorce himself.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a husband delegates divorce by saying 'Choose', the wife's choosing herself yields one bāʾin divorce, established by the Companions' consensus over qiyas.

The Hanafi Ruling on Delegation of Divorce with the Word 'Choose'
Ruling on delegation of divorce via 'choose' with intention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on delegation of divorce using the word 'choose', explain the proof of ijma al-sahabah, and compare the Hanafi position with that of al-Shafi'i and Malik.

Core Idea
When a husband says 'choose' with intention, the wife's choice triggers one irrevocable divorce by consensus of the Companions.

This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq addresses a specific form of divorce delegation (tafwid al-talaq): the husband says to his wife 'choose' (اختاري) intending divorce. The ruling is that if she chooses herself in the same session, one irrevocable divorce (bain) occurs. This is based on istihsan and the consensus of the Companions, overriding the qiyas that would require nothing to occur. We will explore the conditions, the proof, and the differences with other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Hanafi position
What is the ruling when a husband says 'choose' intending divorce and the wife chooses herself in the same session? Note that the divorce is bain (irrevocable).
2
Examine the proof: ijma al-sahabah
Why does the Hanafi school rule this way? The primary proof is the consensus of the Companions. Understand what ijma al-sahabah means and why it overrides qiyas.
The consensus is silent (sukuti), but it is binding.
3
Understand the conditions for divorce to occur
What conditions must be met? The husband must intend divorce, the wife must choose herself (not her husband) in the same session, and the word 'choose' must be used. Note that delegation is tamlik (grant), not agency.
If the wife chooses her husband or delays, no divorce occurs.
4
Contrast with Shafi'i and Ahmad
How do al-Shafi'i and Ahmad rule? They say the divorce is raj'i (revocable) because 'choose' is a kinayah. Understand the basis of their view and the Hanafi response.
5
Contrast with Malik and another Shafi'i view
Malik rules that three divorces occur without intention, and some Shafi'is say three with intention. The Hanafi response relies on the consensus of one divorce.
The word 'choice' does not vary, so only one is possible.

Chapter of Delegation of Divorce, i.e. explaining its ruling, i.e. to the wife or to the agent. He said to her: 'Choose' intending divorce thereby, and she chose in her session, she becomes separated by one [divorce], and it is not valid... i.e. a man said to his wife intending three. i.e. the woman: i.e. the session of her knowledge of the choice. 'By one'... Chapter of Delegation of Divorce: since he mentioned what he himself executes by his two types, i.e. explicit like his saying: 'Divorce yourself', and implicit like his saying: 'Choose', he mentioned what another executes by his permission, and what another executes, whether wife or other, in three types: delegation, agency, and message. And the words of delegation are three: choice, command, and wish. And since divorce by another is contrary to the original rule, he mentioned it after explaining what is the original. (Fath) He began with 'choose' because it is established by explicit reports, and choice is inclination towards good and what is better and preferable. And the issue is placed on 'choose yourself' because if he said to her 'choose divorce' and she chose it, she is revocably divorced. And the husband's revocation of the delegation is not valid because it is a grant of ownership, not agency. And his statement: 'intending divorce thereby' means delegation of divorce, this is indicated by the chapter heading with delegation, and he did not mention the indication, relying on what preceded. And what is meant by indication is the state of discussing divorce and the state of anger. (Fath) 'Intending' i.e. while he is intending by his saying 'choose'. (Ayni) 'And she chose' i.e. the woman herself. If she chose her husband, it does not occur and the matter leaves her hand. If she used 'or' and said: 'I choose myself or my husband', it also does not occur. And if it was with 'and', then consideration is for the first and what follows is void. And his statement: 'in her session' i.e. the session of her knowledge of the choice, verbally or by information. And his statement: 'she becomes separated' because her choice of herself is thereby realized, due to the establishment of its specificity to herself in irrevocable divorce, unlike revocable because the husband can take her back without her consent. And the analogy is that nothing occurs even if the husband intends divorce, because transfer of ownership is a branch of the owner's ownership. And the husband does not have the power to effect divorce with this word, even if he said: 'I chose myself from you' or 'I chose you from myself' intending, it does not occur. But we ruled by istihsan that it occurs by her choice, due to the consensus of the Companions. And this consensus is a silent consensus from the statement of some of them and the silence of others. And the consensus is established on one [divorce], and what is beyond that remains on the original rule. And it is restricted by intention because it is from the implicit words. And the contextual indication stands in its place in judicial matters not in religious matters. And what occurs by it is irrevocable because it is an implicit word. And according to al-Shafi'i and Ahmad: it is revocable. And the oath of the husband regarding lack of intention and indication is accepted. And in all other implicit words, he is not believed except with an oath. (Ayni, Fath) 'She becomes separated' and if he has no intention, nothing occurs. (T) 'And it is not valid' [because choice indicates exclusivity and it is not varied, unlike separation because it varies into severe and light, so whichever he intends is valid.] Because it is not a grant of ownership in origin, but rather it is made a grant of ownership contrary to analogy due to the consensus of the Companions. And because choice does not vary, unlike separation because it varies; it is said: she separated with minor or major separation. And according to Malik: three occur without intention. And according to al-Shafi'i: three occur if with intention. (Ramz al-Haqaiq: 1/247) (Mustakhlis)
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from Kanz al-Daqa'iq with commentary, dealing with delegation of divorce using the word 'choose' (اختاري). It begins by defining the chapter and explaining that delegation can be to the wife or an agent. The core ruling: if a husband says to his wife 'Choose' intending divorce, and she chooses herself in the same session, she becomes separated by one irrevocable divorce (bain). The intention is necessary because 'choose' is a kinayah (implicit word). The ruling is based on istihsan (juristic preference) supported by the consensus of the Companions (ijma' al-sahabah). The Hanafi position is that the divorce is bain (irrevocable), contrary to al-Shafi'i and Ahmad who hold it is raj'i (revocable). Additionally, Malik holds that three divorces occur without intention. The commentary emphasizes that delegation is a tamlik (grant of ownership) not a tawkil (agency), so the husband cannot revoke it. If the wife chooses her husband or uses ambiguous wording, no divorce occurs. The passage also explains that contextual indications (dilalat al-hal) are considered in judicial matters (qada') but not in religious matters (diyanah). The keys to understanding: the word 'choose' must be accompanied by intention, the wife must respond in the same session, and the divorce is bain because it is a tamlik on the basis of istihsan and ijma.

Key Terms
تفويض الطلاق (tafwid al-talaq): Delegation of divorce: the husband empowers his wife or another to issue a divorce on his behalf.
اختاري (ikhtari): The word 'choose' used by the husband to delegate the choice of divorce to the wife; it is a kinayah requiring intention.
إجماع الصحابة (ijma' al-sahabah): Consensus of the Companions of the Prophet Muhammad, used here as the primary proof for the occurrence of one divorce despite the contrary analogy.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When a husband says to his wife 'Choose' intending divorce, and she chooses herself in the same session, what is the Hanafi ruling?
A) No divorce occurs
B) One revocable divorce occurs
C) One irrevocable divorce occurs
D) Three divorces occur

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school rules that delegation of divorce using the word 'choose' results in one irrevocable divorce when intention and immediate acceptance are present, based on the consensus of the Companions.

Actions That Nullify the Wife's Option to Choose
Conditions of the session and actions that nullify the option to choose

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which actions nullify the wife's option to choose in delegated divorce and explain the principle of disinterest (i'rad) that governs them.

Core Idea
Standing or beginning a significant action nullifies delegation; trivial acts without disinterest do not.

When a husband delegates the power to divorce, the wife must exercise her option in the same session without showing disinterest. This lesson expands on what counts as disinterest—major actions like standing or starting another significant act void the option, while minor motions like drinking or changing posture do not. Understanding this distinction is key to applying the Hanafi ruling correctly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base ruling
The wife must choose in the same session (majlis) without indicating disinterest (i'rad). The session is considered one continuous unit.
This is the core condition for the delegation to remain valid.
2
Define 'action that indicates disinterest'
Actions that clearly show the wife is no longer considering the choice—like standing or starting a separate major action—nullify the option. Trivial acts that do not signal disinterest (e.g., drinking water) are excused.
The criterion is the appearance of cutting off engagement, not mere movement.
3
Apply the exception for time-bound delegation
If the delegation is for a specific time (muwaqqat), standing or other actions do not nullify; only the expiry of the fixed time ends the option.
Contrast with absolute delegation where any major action voids.
4
Analyze specific cases: prayer and postures
Completing an obligatory prayer or witr does not nullify, but completing a supererogatory prayer of two rak'ahs does—because the supererogatory prayer is a distinct action. Changing sitting postures (e.g., from kneeling to cross-legged) does not nullify.
The ruling differentiates based on the intention and duration of the action.
5
State the underlying principle
Delegation is a tamlik (grant of ownership); it is void by any act that indicates abandonment, similar to other grants of ownership.
This principle ties the specific cases to a broader rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قامت أو أخذت في عمل آخر بطل، وذكر النفس أو الاختيارة في أحد أي شرعت غير الاختيار أو التطليقة كلاميهما شرط، فإن قال لها اختاري فقالت أنا أختار نفسي أو اخترت نفسي أي لامرأته الزوجين بلفظ المضارع بلفظ الماضي فإن قامت [ أي المخيرة من المجلس قبل الاختيار (ط)] هذا تفريع على قيد المجلس في الاختيار ؛ وذلك لأن ساعات المجلس اعتبرت ساعة واحدة، إلا أن المجلس تستبدل بالقيام مرة ومرة بالاشتغال بفعل آخر، والقيام مبطل إذا كان التفويض مطلقاً، وأما إذا كان مؤقتا فلا يبطل بالقيام ونحوه، وإنما يبطل بمضي الوقت وإن لم تقم. (مستخلص، فتح) أو أخذت في عمل آخر المراد بالعمل ما يعلم به أنه قطع لما قبله لا مطلق العمل حتى لو شربت ماء لا يبطل خيارها؛ لأنها قد تشرب لتتمكن من الخصومة فإن رطوبة الفم تذهب بالمشاجرة، فلا تقدر على الكلام ما لم تشرب، فلا يكون دليل الإعراض، وكذلك إذا أكلت شيئاً يسيراً من غير طلب الطعام أو لبست ثياباً من غير أن تقوم من ذلك المجلس أو سبحت أو قرأت آية؛ لأن ذلك عمل قليل، وقوله: "بطل" أي التفويض؛ لأنه تمليك فيبطل بما يدل على الإعراض من قيام أو أخذ في عمل آخر كسائر التمليكات فلو كانت في صلاة مكتوبة أو وتر فأتمتها لا يبطل، وكذا في التطوع إن أتمت ركعتين ولو أتمت بطل، ولو كانت قائمة فقعدت فهي على خيارها، وكذلك إذا كانت قاعدة فاتكأت أو متكئة فقعدت، وكذا إذا تربعت بعد أن كانت محتبية. (فتح، عيني) بطل: أي التفويض حتى لا يجوز لها أن تطلق نفسها بذلك الأمر. (مسكين)
If she stands or begins another action, it becomes void. And mentioning 'self' or 'choice' in one of them... i.e. if she started other than choice, or the divorce, or their speech is a condition. For if he said to her 'Choose' and she said 'I choose myself' or 'I chose myself'... i.e. to his wife... with the imperfect tense, with the perfect tense. Then if she stands [i.e. the one given choice, from the session before choosing (T)] This is a branch on the condition of the session in the choice; and that is because the moments of the session are considered as one moment, except that the session is nullified by standing sometimes and by being busy with another action sometimes. And standing nullifies if the delegation is absolute; as for if it was time-bound, it is not nullified by standing and the like, but rather by the passing of time even if she does not stand. (Mustakhlis, Fath) 'Or begins another action' – what is meant by action is that by which it is known that she has cut off what was before, not absolute action, so even if she drinks water, her option is not nullified; because she might drink to be able to argue, for the moisture of the mouth goes away with disputation, so she cannot speak unless she drinks, so this is not an indication of turning away. Similarly, if she eats a little without asking for food, or puts on clothes without standing from that session, or glorifies God [sabbah], or recites a verse; because that is a small action. And his statement: 'it becomes void' i.e. the delegation; because it is a grant of ownership, so it is void by what indicates turning away, like standing or beginning another action, like all other grants of ownership. So if she was in an obligatory prayer or witr and completes it, it is not nullified. Similarly in supererogatory prayer, if she completes two rak'ahs, but if she completes [a supererogatory prayer], it is nullified. And if she was standing and sits down, she is upon her option. Similarly if she was sitting and leans, or reclining and sits, and similarly if she sits cross-legged after having been sitting with knees up. (Fath, Ayni) 'It becomes void' i.e. the delegation, so that she is not permitted to divorce herself by that command. (Miskin)
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage expands on the condition of the session (majlis) in delegated divorce. The wife must choose in the same session without standing or engaging in another action that shows disinterest (i'rad). The session is considered one continuous unit; standing or starting a significant action nullifies the option. However, trivial actions like drinking water to soothe the throat for argument, eating a little without intending a meal, dressing without standing, or reciting short litanies do not nullify because they do not indicate disinterest. The ruling distinguishes between absolute delegation (mutlaq) and time-bound delegation (muwaqqat). In absolute delegation, standing or starting another action voids the option; in time-bound, only expiry of time voids it. The commentary also notes that if the wife was in prayer, completing an obligatory prayer or witr does not nullify, but completing a supererogatory prayer of two rak'ahs does nullify, because the supererogatory prayer is a separate action. Sitting, leaning, or changing posture does not nullify. The passage concludes that once the option is void, the wife cannot divorce herself with that command. Underlying principle: delegation is a tamlik (grant of ownership) and is void by any act indicating abandonment, like other grants of ownership.

Key Terms
المجلس (al-majlis): The session where the husband delegates and the wife must choose; considered as one continuous time unit.
الإعراض (al-i'rad): Turning away or disinterest; actions that indicate the wife is no longer considering the choice nullify the delegation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The wife's option in delegated divorce is nullified by any action that indicates disinterest, such as standing or beginning a separate major action, but trivial acts done without i'rad do not affect it.

Requirement of Mentioning 'Self' or 'Choice' in Delegated Divorce
Condition that 'self' or 'choice' must be mentioned in the utterance of either party.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition of mentioning 'self' or 'choice' in delegated divorce and apply it to determine the validity of divorce utterances.

Core Idea
In delegated divorce, ambiguity is resolved by explicit mention of 'self' or 'choice' — without it, no divorce occurs.

Delegated divorce (tafwid) gives the wife the power to divorce herself. However, the utterance 'Choose' is ambiguous: it could mean choose yourself (divorce) or choose your husband (stay). To eliminate this ambiguity, the Hanafi school requires that either the husband or the wife explicitly mention 'self' (nafs) or 'choice' (ikhtiyarah) in their speech. This lesson examines this condition, its reasoning, and a point of intra-Hanafi difference.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core condition
The passage states that mentioning 'self' or 'choice' in the speech of one of them (either husband or wife) is a condition for the delegated divorce to take effect. This condition resolves ambiguity about the wife's choice.
The condition can be fulfilled by either party.
2
Distinguish between valid and invalid scenarios
From the examples: if husband says 'Choose yourself' and wife says 'I chose' – valid. If husband says 'Choose' and wife says 'I chose myself' – valid. If husband says 'Choose' and wife says 'I chose' – invalid. Identify which party fulfilled the condition in each case.
The condition is met if either party uses the word 'self' or 'choice' explicitly.
3
Analyze the role of ambiguity
The word 'choose' alone is ambiguous (could be herself or her husband). Ambiguity leads to doubt, and doubt prevents divorce in Hanafi fiqh. The mention of 'self' or 'choice' (ikhtiyarah, with ta indicating singularity) removes that ambiguity.
The term 'ikhtiyarah' itself clarifies a single choice due to the feminine singular ta.
4
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
The passage notes that according to three scholars (likely Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and Zufar), this specification is not required. Understand the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf): under the three's view, a simple 'Choose' + 'I choose' would effect a divorce, while under the relied-upon Hanafi view it does not.
The mufta bihi is the Hanafi position requiring the specification.
5
Apply to new scenarios
Given various utterances, determine if the condition is satisfied. For example, if husband says 'I give you a choice' with 'choice' (ikhtiyarah) as a verbal noun, it satisfies the condition; if he says 'I give you the power', it does not.
Other texts add that saying 'divorce' (al-taliqah) also suffices.

And because 'choose' is ambiguous, and her saying 'I chose' is also ambiguous, and the ambiguous cannot serve to explain the ambiguous. And 'choice' (al-ikhtiyarah) serves as an explanation because the 'ta' in 'al-ikhtiyarah' indicates singularity. The woman's choice of her divorce can imply singularity or multiplicity, unlike her choice of her husband because it is an expression of keeping the marriage, so singularity and multiplicity are not realized. So it is necessary to mention 'self' or 'choice' in the speech of one of them. According to the three [imams?], it is not necessary. In al-Muhit: it is necessary to mention 'self' or 'divorce' or 'choice'. In al-Shamil: he said to her 'Choose' then he divorced her, and she said 'I chose myself' – it occurs because the divorced woman is not divorced. And if he said to her 'Choose a choice' and she said 'I chose', one bain divorce also occurs; because 'choice' is explained from the husband's side by the 'ta' of singularity. (Fath)
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage discusses the condition that in delegated divorce, either the husband or wife must explicitly mention 'self' (nafs) or 'choice' (ikhtiyarah) in their utterance. This requirement ensures clarity: the word 'choose' alone is ambiguous, and responding with 'I chose' (اخترت) is also ambiguous because it could refer to choosing herself or her husband. Therefore, without specifying, no divorce occurs due to doubt. The Hanafi school, based on consensus, requires that either the husband says 'Choose yourself' or the wife says 'I chose myself', or the husband uses 'choice' (اختيارة) which, due to the feminine singular ta, indicates one choice. If the husband says 'Choose' and the wife says 'I chose myself', it is valid; similarly, if the husband says 'Choose yourself' and she says 'I chose', it is valid. The passage notes that three other scholars (likely reference to Abu Hanifa's companions? Or other madhahib?) do not require this specification. A reference from al-Muhit adds that 'divorce' (taliqah) also suffices. The ruling is that the divorce occurs as one bain. The passage also includes a sample from al-Shamil: if the husband says 'Choose' then divorces her, and she then says 'I chose myself', it occurs because the divorced woman is not divorced (i.e., she is already divorced, so her choice is irrelevant?). The key takeaway: the mention of 'self' or 'choice' in either party's speech is a condition for validity, ensuring clarity in the intention.

Key Terms
النفس (al-nafs): The wife's self; mentioning 'self' in the utterance (e.g., 'choose yourself' or 'I chose myself') removes ambiguity.
الاختيارة (al-ikhtiyarah): A verbal noun meaning 'a single choice' with the feminine ta' indicating singularity; used to clarify that one divorce is intended.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In delegated divorce, the mention of 'self' or 'choice' by either spouse is a condition for the divorce to take effect.

Delegation of Divorce with the Word 'Ikhtiyar': Rulings and Proofs
Rulings and proofs for delegation of divorce by the word 'ikhtiyar'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling for delegation of divorce using the word 'ikhtiyar' in both single and repeated scenarios, and explain the istihsan proof from the hadith of 'A'isha, and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
The word 'ikhtiyar' in delegation triggers divorce by istihsan, but the number of divorces depends on the wording and repetition.

This lesson examines the Hanafi law of tafwid (delegation) specifically when the husband uses the word 'ikhtari' (choose). The passage from Kanz al-Daqaiq and its commentaries addresses two key scenarios: a single delegation and a repeated one. You will learn how istihsan overrides qiyas based on the precedent of the Prophet ﷺ and the Companions, and how the Imams differed on whether three or one divorces fall when the wife specifies 'first, middle, or last'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main scenarios
The text covers two situations: (A) husband says 'ikhtari' once, wife responds with 'akhtaru nafsi' or 'akhtartu nafsi'. (B) husband repeats 'ikhtari' three times, wife responds with 'first, middle, last' or 'ikhtiyarah'.
Focus on the distinct rulings for each.
2
Understand the ruling for scenario A
One ba'in divorce falls if the husband intended divorce, by istihsan. Qiyas would not find a divorce because the response could be a promise.
Niyyah (intention) of the husband is crucial.
3
Examine the istihsan proof
The text cites ijma of the Companions and the hadith of 'A'isha (ra) when she responded to the Prophet's offer with 'akhtaru Allah wa rasulahu', which was accepted as a valid choice. This shows that 'akhtaru' indicates present action.
The proof overrides the qiyas objection.
4
Analyze the dispute for scenario B
Abu Hanifa rules three divorces without the husband's intention; Sahibayn (and Shafi'i) rule one divorce. Each side bases its view on interpretation of the wife's words: 'first' etc.
Note the thamarah al-khilaf.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The commentators (Fath, 'Ayni) prefer Abu Hanifa's view for the repeated scenario, so the relied-upon position is three divorces.
This is the operative fatwa in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطلاق
التفويض بلفظ النفس و الاختيار
تطلق، وإن قال لها اختاري اختاري اختاري فقالت اخترت الأولى أو الوسطى أو لامرأته
ثلاث مرات
المرأة
الأخيرة أو اختيارةً وقع الثلاث بلا نية، ولو قالت طلّقتُ نفسي أو اخترتُ نفسي أي أو قالت اخترت
تطلق طلقة بائنة إن نوى استحسانا والقياس أن لا تطلق؛ لاحتمال الوعد فلا يتجرد جواباً وهو قول الثلاثة. (ط)] أي لو قال لها: اختاري، فقالت أختار نفسي، أو اخترت نفسي تطلق طلقة واحدة إن نوى الزوج الطلاق استحساناً، والقياس أن لا تطلق في "أختار نفسي"؛ لأن قولها: "أنا اختار نفسي" يحتمل الوعد فلا يكون جواباً
مع الاحتمال.

وجه الاستحسان: إجماع الصحابة؛ ولأن الشرع جعل هذا إيجاباً وجواباً؛ لما روي أنه لما نزل قوله تعالى: ﴿يَا أَيُّهَا النَّبِيُّ قُلْ لِأَزْوَاجِكَ إِن كُنتُنَّ تُرِدْنَ الْحَيَاةَ الدُّنْيَا وزينتها الآية (الأحزاب:۲۸) بدأ علينا بعائشة له فقال: "إني أخبرك بشيء فعليك أن لا تجيبني حتى تستأمري أبويك"، ثم أخبرها بالآية فقالت: أفي هذا أستأمر أبوي يا رسول الله؟ لا، بل أختار الله ورسوله، وأرادت بذلك الاختيار للحال وعده عليه جواباً وإيجاباً؛ ولأن الصيغة غلب استعمالها في الحال كما في كلمة الشهادة وأداء الشاهد الشهادة يقال فلان يختار كذا يريد به تحقيقه، فيكون حكاية عن اختيارها في القلب، بخلاف أنا أطلق نفسي في جواب قوله: "طلقي نفسك"؛ لأنه تعذر حمله على الحال، فإنه ليس حكاية عن حالة قائمة وقولها "أختار نفسي" حكاية عن حالة قائمة؛ لأن الاختيار فعل القلب فلا يستحيل اجتماع فعل القلب واللسان. (فتح، مستخلص) وقع الثلاث بلا نية: أي لو قال لها اختاري اختاري اختاري بلا عطف أو معه واواً كان أو فاء أو ثم فقالت: اخترت الأولى أو الوسطى أو الأخيرة أو قالت اخترت اختيارة وقع الطلقات الثلاث بلا نية من الزوج عند أبي حنيفة في المسألة الأولى، وعندهما تطلق واحدة؛ لأنها ما اختارت إلا واحدة؛ لأن ذكر الأولى وما جرى مجراه إن كان لا يفيد من حيث الترتيب فيفيد من حيث الأفراد وبه قال الشافعي. وله: أن هذا الوصف لغو؛ لأن المجتمع في الملك لا ترتيب فيه والكلام للترتيب، والأفراد من ضروراته فإذا لغا في أصل الكلام الذي هو الترتيب لغا في حق التبع الذي هو الأفراد فبقي قولها اخترت وهو يصلح جوابا للكل، والمعنى أن الطلقات الثلاث اجتمعت في ملك المرأة كالاجتماع في مكان بلا وصف ترتيب فلا سبق ولا توسط ولا تأخر ولو قالت اخترت اختيارة أو الاختيارة أو مرة أو بمرة أو دفعة أو بدفعة واحدة أو اختيارة واحدة وهي المسألة الثانية فيقع ثلاث بالإجماع ولا حاجة ههنا إلى نية الزوج ولا ذكر النفس بالإجماع؛ لدلالة التكرار عليه، ولو قالت اخترت التطليقة الأولى تقع واحدة إجماعاً. (فتح، عيني) بلا نية أي الطلقات الثلاث بلا نية للثلاث من الزوج. (ط، ع) ولو قالت في جواب قوله: اختاري أي
6
المسألة المذكورة. (ط،ع)
Book of Divorce. Delegation with the words 'nafs' and 'ikhtiyar'. She is divorced, and if he said to her: 'Choose, choose, choose' and she said: 'I chose the first, or the middle, or the last, or (I chose) an election' - the three (divorces) fall without intention. And if she said: 'I divorced myself' or 'I chose myself' (meaning: or she said: 'I chose') she is divorced one ba'in divorce if he intended, by istihsan. And by qiyas she is not divorced due to the probability of a promise, so it is not a pure response, and this is the saying of the three. (Tahtawi) Meaning: if he said to her: 'Choose' and she said: 'I choose myself' or 'I chose myself' she is divorced one divorce if the husband intended divorce by istihsan. By qiyas she is not divorced in 'I choose myself' because her saying 'I choose myself' could be a promise so it is not a response with probability. The basis of istihsan: ijma of the Companions; and because the Law made this an offer and a response; based on what was narrated when the verse ﴾O Prophet, say to your wives: If you desire the life of this world and its adornment...﴿ (al-Ahzab:28) was revealed, he (the Prophet) began with 'A'isha (may Allah be pleased with her) and said: 'I inform you of something, and you must not answer me until you consult your parents.' Then he informed her of the verse, and she said: 'Do I consult my parents in this? No, rather I choose Allah and His Messenger.' And she intended by that choice for the present, and he (the Prophet) counted it as a response and an offer. And because the expression is predominantly used for the present, like the word of testimony and the performance of a witness's testimony; it is said: 'So-and-so chooses such-and-such' meaning he affirms it, so it is a report of her choice in the heart. Unlike 'I divorce myself' in response to his saying 'Divorce yourself' because it is impossible to carry it on the present, as it is not a report of an existing state. Her saying 'I choose myself' is a report of an existing state because choice is an act of the heart, so it is not impossible for the act of the heart and the tongue to coexist. (Fath, Mustakhlas) 'The three fall without intention' means: if he said to her 'choose, choose, choose' without a conjunction, or with 'waw', 'fa', or 'thumma', and she said 'I chose the first, or the middle, or the last', or she said 'I chose an election', the three divorces occur without intention from the husband according to Abu Hanifa in the first case. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), one falls because she only chose one; because mentioning 'the first' and similar, if it does not indicate order, it indicates individuality, and al-Shafi'i said the same. And for him (Abu Hanifa) this description is superfluous because what is possessed collectively has no order, and speech is for order; individuality is a necessary consequence. So if it is superfluous in the origin of the speech which is order, it is superfluous in the dependent aspect which is individuality; thus her saying 'I chose' remains, which is valid as a response to all. The meaning is that the three divorces are gathered in the woman's possession like gathering in a place without any description of order, so there is no precedence, intermediate, or delay. And if she said 'I chose an election' or 'the election' or 'once' or 'by once' or 'at once' or 'with one instance' or 'one election' - this is the second case - then three fall by consensus, and there is no need here for the husband's intention nor for mentioning 'nafs' by consensus, because the repetition indicates it. And if she said 'I chose the first divorce', one falls by consensus. (Fath, 'Ayni) 'Without intention' means the three divorces without intention for the three from the husband. (Tahtawi, 'Ayni) And if she said in response to his saying: 'Choose' (meaning) the mentioned case. (Tahtawi, 'Ayni)
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Book of Divorce in Kanz al-Daqaiq, expanded by its commentators. It deals with delegation (tafweed) using the words 'nafs' (self) and 'ikhtiyar' (choice). The central issue: when a husband says to his wife 'ikhtari' (choose), and she responds with a phrase like 'akhtartu nafsi' (I chose myself) or 'akhtaru nafsi' (I choose myself), what divorce occurs? The text first states the ruling: she is divorced one ba'in (irrevocable) divorce if the husband intended divorce, by istihsan (juristic preference). The qiyas (analogy) would be that she is not divorced because her response could be interpreted as a promise for the future, not a present choice. However, istihsan overrides qiyas based on two proofs. The first proof is ijma of the Companions; the second is the hadith of 'A'isha (may Allah be pleased with her) when the verse of takhyir (choice) was revealed. The Prophet (peace be upon him) gave her the option, and she immediately chose Allah and His Messenger, using the present tense 'akhtaru' (I choose), and the Prophet considered that a valid acceptance of the offer. This shows that the expression 'akhtaru' is used for the present state, not a future promise. The commentator explains that choice is an act of the heart, and speaking it simultaneously is possible, unlike saying 'I divorce myself' in response to 'divorce yourself', which cannot be a report of a present act. The text then addresses a scenario where the husband repeats 'ikhtari' three times, and the wife responds with 'I chose the first, middle, or last', or 'I chose an election'. According to Imam Abu Hanifa, three divorces fall without the husband's intention, because the wife's response is to all three offers. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that only one divorce falls, because she chose only one. The commentator sides with Abu Hanifa, arguing that the description of 'first' etc. is superfluous since the three divorces are not ordered in reality, so her response 'I chose' suffices for all. The text also notes that if she says 'I chose the first divorce', only one falls by consensus. The key point is that in cases of repetition, the wife's mention of 'nafs' is not needed by consensus if the repetition indicates it.

Key Terms
التفويض (al-tafwid): Delegation of the power to divorce from husband to wife, using specific words like 'ikhtari' or 'amruki bi yadik.'
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas due to stronger evidence (ijma or textual indication).
الاختِيار (al-ikhtiyar): The act of choosing; in this context, the wife's verbal expression of choice in response to delegation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a husband repeats 'ikhtari' three times and the wife responds 'I chose the first', which ruling is the mufta bihi in the Hanafi school?
A) One divorce (Sahibayn's view).
B) Three divorces (Abu Hanifa's view).
C) No divorce.
D) Two divorces.

Key Takeaway
The delegation of divorce using 'ikhtiyar' is governed by istihsan, and the number of divorces in repeated delegation is disputed, with the relied-upon Hanafi position being three divorces when the wife responds to all three offers.

Talaq — Divorce

Divorce by Command by Hand: The Hanafi Ruling
Rulings for delegation of divorce via 'amruki bi yadik'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on divorce by 'amr bi al-yad' and identify whether the resulting divorce is raj'i or ba'in according to the relied-upon position, and apply the ruling to scenarios with different intentions.

Core Idea
Divorce by 'your affair is in your hand' is ba'in (irrevocable) because the husband's delegation is kinaya (allusive).

In Hanafi fiqh, a husband may delegate divorce to his wife by saying 'amruki bi yadik' (your affair is in your hand). This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq clarifies the nature of the divorce that results, whether it is revocable (raj'i) or irrevocable (ba'in). It also covers cases where the husband intends a specific number of divorces and how the wife's response affects the outcome. Understanding this ruling is crucial for correctly applying delegation in divorce.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule for delegation without specification
The passage begins with the default ruling: when the husband says 'amruki bi yadik' or 'ikhtari' for one divorce, and the wife chooses herself, she gets one raj'i divorce. This is the starting point before considering intentions or specifications.
This initial statement is later refined by the commentators.
2
Understand the dispute: raj'i vs ba'in
The text presents two narrations within the Hanafi school. One (from al-Jami' al-Saghir) holds it is raj'i because the wife uses explicit divorce words. The other (from al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Mabsut, al-Ziyadat) holds it is ba'in because the husband's words are allusive, so she only has power to separate irrevocably. The latter is more correct.
The operative factor is the husband's delegation wording, not the wife's execution.
3
Apply the rule when the husband intends three divorces
If the husband says 'amruki bi yadik' intending three, and the wife responds with 'akhtartu nafsi' intending one, the result is one ba'in divorce. The husband's intention of three is not realized; the wife's choice prevails.
The wife's intention in her response matters for the count.
4
Examine conditions for the wife's response
The wife must mention 'nafsi' (myself) in her response. Alternative phrasings like 'akhtartu amri' or 'qabiltu nafsi' suffice. If she says 'anta haram' or 'anta minni ba'in', these also constitute divorce.
The response must clearly indicate acceptance of the delegated power.
5
Distinguish command by hand from takhyir
Command by hand (amr bi al-yad) is like takhyir (choice) in most respects, except in takhyir the husband cannot validly intend to delegate three divorces, whereas in command by hand he can. This is the key difference.
This distinction affects the validity of the husband's intention.

Book of Divorce Page 425 Ruling on the Command by Hand: With one divorce, she becomes ba'in (irrevocably) by one; 'Your affair is in your hand' for one divorce, or 'choose' one divorce and she chose herself, she is divorced one raj'i (revocable) divorce. Section. That is, or if he said to her... that she said 'I chose myself'; 'Your affair is in your hand' intending three, so she said 'I chose myself' by one, the woman in response has the meaning of raj'a (revocability) by one instance, she becomes ba'in by one. (Meaning by one divorce, because the operative factor is the husband's delegating choice, not her execution; for even if she executed explicitly, her execution is disregarded, rather it is due to the husband's delegation. (Tahtawi, 'Ayni)) Because the operative factor is the husband's delegating choice, not her execution. It is mentioned in al-Hidayah: it is one and he has the right of return (raj'a). The correct view is that he does not have the right of return because the woman acts as a representative by virtue of delegation, and delegation is an error? Actually it is a ba'in divorce because it is from kinayat (allusive expressions), so she only possesses the power to separate. And it is said there are two narrations: one that it is one raj'i divorce because her wording is explicit, mentioned by Sadr al-Islam in al-Jami' al-Saghir; the other that it is ba'in, and this is more correct, mentioned in al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Mabsut, and al-Ziyadat. Do you not see that if the husband ordered her to give a ba'in or raj'i divorce and she reversed, what the husband intended falls. (Fath, 'Ayni) 'Your affair is in your hand for one divorce' means if he said to his wife 'Your affair is in your hand' etc. (Tahtawi) 'al-amr' here means 'the state' and 'al-yad' means 'disposal'. He restricted it with 'for one divorce' because if he said 'Your affair is in your hand; if you do not receive my maintenance, divorce yourself whenever you wish' and she did not receive and divorced, it would be ba'in because the word 'talaq' was not in the actual command. (Fath) She is divorced one raj'i divorce because he gave her the choice, but it is for one divorce, which is followed by revocability. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 1:248) If it is said: his saying 'Your affair is in your hand' and his saying 'choose' indicate irrevocability (baynuna), so it is not permissible to divert it to something else. We say: when it is coupled with explicit wording, it is known to be raj'i, just as if one couples an explicit divorce with ba'in by saying 'You are divorced ba'in' then it becomes ba'in because he intended ba'in; so likewise here. Know that explicit divorce does not become ba'in by mere intention, just as ba'in does not become raj'i by mere intention. (Mustakhlas, Fath) Section: on explaining the ruling of command by hand. (Ramz al-Haqaiq 1:248) 'Your affair is in your hand' means if a man says to his wife: 'Your affair is in your hand' or 'in your palm' or 'in your right hand' or 'in your left hand' or 'in your mouth' or 'in your tongue'. (Tahtawi) 'Intending three' means while intending three by that. ('Ayni) It is restricted by the intention for three because if he intended one or two and she was free, or did not intend anything, one falls. And his saying 'so she said: I chose myself' – mentioning 'nafs' is a condition; if she does not mention it, it does not fall, just as in the response to 'ikhtiyar' it does not fall except with mentioning 'nafs'. And mentioning 'I chose my affair' or 'I accepted myself' stands in place of mentioning 'nafs'. Similarly, if she says in response to command by hand: 'You are haram to me' or 'You are ba'in from me' or 'I am ba'in from you', because these words indicate divorce. And his saying 'they fall' because 'choice' is valid as a response to command by hand since it is a form of delegation like 'ikhtiyar', and 'one' is the description of the choice, so it becomes as if she said 'I chose myself with one instance', and thereby three fall. The conclusion: command by hand is like 'ikhtiyar' in all matters except regarding the possibility of three, where intention is not valid in 'ikhtiyar' as previously mentioned. (Fath, 'Ayni)
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage is a ruling from the Book of Divorce on the topic of 'al-amr bi al-yad' (command by hand). It begins by stating a general rule: if the husband says to his wife 'amruki bi yadik' (your affair is in your hand) for one divorce, or says 'ikhtari' (choose) for one divorce, and she chooses herself, she is divorced one raj'i (revocable) divorce. The commentator clarifies that the operative factor in this delegation is the husband's choice, not the wife's execution; even if she uses explicit words, the effect is based on the husband's delegation. There is a discussion about whether the divorce is raj'i or ba'in. The text mentions two narrations: one from al-Jami' al-Saghir that it is raj'i because her wording is explicit, and another from al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Mabsut, and al-Ziyadat that it is ba'in, which is deemed more correct. The reasoning for ba'in is that the husband's words are kinayat (allusive), so she only has the power to separate irrevocably. The text then addresses a specific scenario: if the husband says 'amruki bi yadik' intending three divorces, and the wife responds with 'akhtartu nafsi' (I chose myself) with the intention of one, the divorce falls as one ba'in, because the husband's intention (three) is not realized; rather, the wife's response is effective. The commentator emphasizes that the wife's mention of 'nafs' (self) is a condition; without it, divorce does not fall. Various alternative phrasings like 'akhtartu amri' (I chose my affair) or 'kabiltu nafsi' (I accepted myself) suffice. Also, if she says 'anta alayya haram' or 'anta minni ba'in' or 'ana minki ba'in', these also constitute divorce. The section concludes that the command by hand is analogous to takhyir (giving choice) in most respects, except that in takhyir, intention for three is not valid (meaning the husband cannot intend to delegate three divorces through the word 'ikhtari' alone), whereas in command by hand, intention for three is valid and effective.

Key Terms
الأمر باليد (al-amr bi al-yad): A form of delegation where the husband says 'amruki bi yadik' (your affair is in your hand) giving the wife authority to divorce herself.
الرجعة (al-raj'ah): The revocability of a divorce during the iddah period; a raj'i divorce can be revoked by the husband without new marriage.
البائن (al-ba'in): An irrevocable divorce where the husband cannot take the wife back during iddah except with new marriage and consent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) Hanafi position regarding the nature of divorce when a husband says 'amruki bi yadik' without specifying a number?
A) The divorce is raj'i because the wife's response is explicit.
B) The divorce is ba'in because the husband's words are kinaya.
C) No divorce occurs until the wife explicitly says 'tallaqtu nafsi'.
D) The divorce is suspended until the husband's intention is clarified.

Key Takeaway
Divorce by 'amr bi al-yad' (command by hand) results in a ba'in (irrevocable) divorce according to the relied-upon Hanafi position, because the husband's delegation is allusive and only grants the power of irrevocable separation.

The Ruling of 'Amr bi Yadik: Ba'in or Raj'i?
Explaining the legal effect of 'amr bi yadik' and the dispute within the Hanafi school

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on whether 'amr bi yadik' results in a ba'in or raj'i divorce and identify the stronger narration.

Core Idea
The command by hand gives the wife power only to effect a ba'in divorce.

In this lesson, we examine the foundational ruling of 'amr bi yadik' (command by hand). The passage establishes that when a husband delegates the power of divorce to his wife using phrases like 'Your command is in your hand,' the resulting divorce is irrevocable (ba'in). However, there is a minority narration that holds it is revocable (raj'i). We will analyze the evidence and the tarjih.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phrase and its legal category
Recognize that 'amr bi yadik' is a kinayah (allusive) for divorce. It is a form of tamlik (delegation), not a direct pronouncement.
Kinayah requires intention and context to determine the ruling.
2
Understand the key principle
The husband's delegation controls, not the wife's execution. Even if the wife uses explicit wording, the nature of the divorce depends on the husband's delegation.
This is why the normal default of raj'i does not apply.
3
Analyze the two narrations
One narration (from al-Jami' al-Saghir) says it is raj'i; the stronger narration (al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Mabsut, al-Ziyadat) says it is ba'in. The text prefers the ba'in position.
The stronger narration carries more weight in fatwa.
4
Apply the rule when coupled with explicit terms
If the husband couples the delegation with an explicit term like 'one divorce,' the divorce becomes raj'i because the explicit term specifies revocability.
This is analogous to saying 'You are divorced baa'in' – the qualifying word changes the nature.

(مستخلص، فتح) فصل: أي في بيان حكم الأمر باليد رمز الحقائق ٢٤٨/١٢] أمرك بيدك : أي لو قال رجل لامرأته: أمرك بيدك أو في كفك أو يمينك أو شمالك أو فمك أو لسانك. (ط) ينوي ثلاثاً : [ أي حال كونه ناويا بذلك ثلاثاً. (ع)] قيد بنية الثلاث؛ لأنه لو نوى واحدة أو ثنتين وكانت حرة أو لم ينو شيئاً وقعت واحدة، وقوله: "فقالت: اخترت نفسي" ذكر النفس خرج مخرج الشرط حتى لو لم تذكرها لا يقع كما لا يقع في جواب التخيير إلا بذكر النفس، ويقوم مقام ذكر النفس اخترت أمري أو قبلت نفسي، وكذا لو قالت في جواب الأمر باليد أنت علي حرام أو أنت مني بائن أو أنا منك بائن؛ لأن هذه الألفاظ تفيد الطلاق، وقوله: "وقعن"؛ لأن الاختيار يصلح لجواب الأمر باليد؛ لكونه تمليكاً كالتخيير، والواحدة صفة الاختيارة فصارت كأنها قالت اخترت نفسي بعمرة واحدة وبه يقع الثلاث، والحاصل: إن جعل الأمر بيدها كالتخيير في المسائل كلها إلا في احتمال الثلاثة، فإنه لا يصح نية في التخيير كما مر. (فتح، عيني)

أي المرأة المخيرة في المسألة المذكورة
وقعن: أي الثلاث؛ لأنه نوى محتمل كلامه (ع) وفي طلقت أي في قولها في جواب الزوج لما قال لها: أمرك بيدك. (ع) بواحدة: صفة لمصدر محذوف أي طلقت نفسي بتطليقة واحدة(ع) بانت بواحدة: [لأن الاعتبار بتفويض الزوج لا إيقاعها. (ع)] أي في المسألتين، أما الأولى : يعني لو قال الزوج لها: أمرك بيدك ونوى به ثلاثاً فقالت في جوابه: طلقت نفسي بواحدة فتبين بواحدة؛ لأن الواحدة صفة لمصدر محذوف تقديره طلقت نفسي بطلقة واحدة، وفي الثانية: أي لو قال لها: أمرك بيدك ونوى به ثلاثاً فقالت في جوابه: اخترت نفسي بتطليقة أيضاً بواحدة؛ لأن في جوابها تصريحا بالتطليقة وهي لا تكون إلا واحدة وإنما تكون بائنة؛ لأن المعتبر تفويض الزوج لا إيقاعها فتكون الصفة المذكورة في التفويض مذكورة في الجواب ضرورة الموافقة. وإنما صحت نية الثلاث في قوله: "أمرك بيدك"؛ لأن الأمر باليد تمليك فيملك تمليك ما يملكه قياسا واستحساناً، بخلاف التخيير؛ لأن وقوع الطلاق به على خلاف القياس لإجماع الصحابة فكان ضروريا، وذكر النفس في قولها: طلقت نفسي" في جواب الأمر باليد شرط حتى لو قال لها : أمرك بيدك فقالت: طلقت ولم تقل: نفسي لم يقع شيء. (مستخلص، فتح) ولا يدخل الليل لأن كل واحد من اليومين ذكر مفرداً، واليوم المفرد لا يتناول الليل، بخلاف الأيام؛ لأنها ينتظم ما بإزائها من الليالي مستخلص) وبعد غد فلا يكون لها الخيار بالليل حتى لو اختارت نفسها في الليل لا يقع الطلاق. (مص)
من غير
بطل أمر ذلك اليوم لأنه صرح بذكر وقتين اليوم وبعد غد أن بينهما وقت وهو الغد لم يتناول الأمر فعلم أنه ليس المراد بذكر الوقت الثاني امتداد الأمر الأول فاقتضى ضرورة إيجاب أمر آخر، فلما كانا أمرين ثبت لها الخيار في كل من الوقتين فإن ردت في اليوم لا يرتد بعد الغد، وقال زفر يبطل خيارها بعد غد أيضاً؛ لأنهما أمر واحد؛ لأنه عطف أحد الوقتين على الآخر تكرار لفظ الأمر، فيكون أمراً واحداً كما في الصورة الآتية أي اليوم وغداً، وكقوله: أنت طالق اليوم وبعد غد، قلنا: الأمر باليد يحتمل التوقيت لا حاجة إلى إدخال ما لم يدخل في اللفظ مقصوداً ولا تبعاً، فكانا أمرين ضرورة الانفصال، بخلاف الطلاق؛ لأنه لا يحتمل التوقيت فجاز أن يوصف في اليوم وبعد غد بطلاق واحد، فلا حاجة إلى إيقاع طلاق آخر ؛ لبقاء الأول إلى الوقت الثاني. وبخلاف قوله: "اليوم وغداً" على ما يأتي من الفرق أن الأمر باليد يحتمل التوقيت، فيتوقف الأمر بالأول ويجعل الثاني أمراً ابتداء، وعن أبي يوسف في الأمالي: " أنه إذا قال لامرأته : أمرك بيدك اليوم، وأمرك بيدك غداً إنهما أمران حتى إذا اختارت زوجها اليوم، ثم جاء الغد صار الأمر بيدها، وهو صحيح؛ لاستقلال كل واحد من الكلامين، فلا حاجة إلى الارتباط بما قبله، وينبغي أن يكون هذا بلا خلاف بينهم، ويتفرع عليه عدم جواز اختيارها نفسها ليلاً. (مستخلص، فتح)
Book of Divorce. Ruling of the command by hand: With one divorce, she becomes separated by one [divorce]. 'Your command is in your hand' in one divorce, or 'Choose' one divorce, and she chose herself, she is divorced by one revocable divorce. Section: Or he said to her: 'Your command is in your hand' intending three, and she said 'I have chosen myself with one.' The woman in her answer [produces] the meaning of revocable? With one time? She becomes separated by one [divorce] because the effective factor is the husband's delegation, not her execution; even if she executed explicitly, but no consideration is given to her execution, only to the husband's delegation. And it is mentioned in al-Hidayah: it is one [divorce] which he possesses, but the correct view is that he does not possess the right of return because the woman acts only as a result of the delegation, and the delegation is a mistake? It is an irrevocable divorce because it is from the kinayah, so she owns only the power of separation. And it is said there are two narrations: one is the occurrence of one revocable divorce because her wording is explicit, mentioned by Sadr al-Islam in al-Jami' al-Saghir, and the other is the occurrence of the irrevocable divorce, and this is more correct, mentioned in al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Mabsut, and al-Ziyadat. Do you not see that if the husband ordered her with the irrevocable or the revocable and she reversed, what the husband intended occurs. (Fath, 'Ayni) 'Your command is in your hand' in one divorce: meaning even if he said to his wife 'your command' etc. The word 'command' here means 'state' and 'hand' means 'disposal'. And he restricted it by 'in' one divorce because if he said: 'Your command is in your hand; if you do not receive my maintenance, then divorce yourself whenever you wish' and she did not receive [maintenance] and she divorced, it would be irrevocable because the word 'divorce' was not in the actual [statement]. (Fath) She is divorced by one revocable divorce because he gave her the choice but with one divorce which is followed by the right of return. [Ramooz al-Haqa'iq: 1/248] If it is said that his statement 'Your command is in your hand' and his statement 'Choose' indicate irrevocability, so it is not permissible to divert it to other than that, we say: Since it is associated with the explicit [wording], it is known to be revocable, just as if he associated the explicit with the irrevocable by saying 'You are divorced irrevocably', then she is separated by it because he intended the irrevocable. So likewise this. And know that explicit divorce does not become irrevocable by mere intention, just as the irrevocable does not become revocable by mere intention. (Mustakhlas, Fath) Section: explaining the ruling of the command by hand. [Ramooz al-Haqa'iq: 1/12] 'Your command is in your hand': meaning if a man said to his wife: 'Your command is in your hand' or 'in your palm' or 'your right hand' or 'your left hand' or 'your mouth' or 'your tongue'. (T) Intending three: meaning while intending thereby three. ('Ayni) He restricted it by intention of three because if he intended one or two and she was free, or he did not intend anything, one occurs. And his statement: 'she said: I have chosen myself' - mentioning 'self' is a condition such that if she does not mention it, it does not occur, just as it does not occur in the response of choice except with mentioning 'self', and 'I have chosen my affair' or 'I have accepted myself' stand in place of mentioning 'self'. Likewise if she says in response to the command by hand: 'You are forbidden to me' or 'You are irrevocable from me' or 'I am irrevocable from you', because these wordings indicate divorce. And his statement: 'they occur' because choice is suitable as a response to the command by hand since it is a form of delegation (tamlik) like choice (takhyeer), and 'one' is a description of the choice, so it is as if she said 'I have chosen myself with one divorce' and thereby the three occur. The conclusion: the command by hand is treated like choice (takhyeer) in all matters except regarding the possibility of three, for intention is not valid in choice (takhyeer) as has passed. (Fath, 'Ayni). They occur, and in 'I divorced myself with one' or 'I chose myself with one divorce' she is separated by one. And the night does not enter [the period] in 'your command is in your hand today and the day after tomorrow.' And if she rejects the command on her day, the command of that day becomes null. She becomes separated. Meaning the woman who is given choice in the mentioned matter. 'They occur': meaning the three because he intended what his speech can bear. ('Ayni) And in 'I divorced' meaning in her statement in response to the husband when he said to her: 'Your command is in your hand.' ('Ayni) 'With one': description of an omitted verbal noun, i.e., 'I divorced myself with one divorce.' ('Ayni) 'She is separated by one' because the consideration is given to the husband's delegation, not her execution. ('Ayni) That is in both matters. As for the first: meaning if the husband said to her: 'Your command is in your hand' and intended by it three, and she said in response: 'I divorced myself with one', she is separated by one because 'one' is a description of an omitted verbal noun, its implied form being 'I divorced myself with one divorce.' And in the second: meaning if he said to her: 'Your command is in your hand' and intended three, and she said in response: 'I chose myself with one divorce' also one, because in her response there is an explicit mention of the divorce, and it cannot be more than one, and it becomes irrevocable because the criterion is the husband's delegation, not her execution, so the description mentioned in the delegation is mentioned in the response necessarily for agreement. And the intention of three is valid in his statement 'your command is in your hand' because the command by hand is a delegation (tamlik), so he possesses the right to delegate what he possesses, by analogy and juristic preference (istihsan), unlike choice (takhyeer) because the occurrence of divorce by it is contrary to analogy due to the consensus of the Companions, so it was considered necessary. And the mention of 'self' in her statement 'I divorced myself' in response to the command by hand is a condition such that if he said to her: 'Your command is in your hand' and she said: 'I divorced' and did not say 'myself', nothing occurs. (Mustakhlas, Fath) And the night does not enter because each of the two days is mentioned individually, and a single day does not include the night, unlike days (plural) because they include what corresponds to them of nights. (Mustakhlas) And the day after tomorrow, so she has no choice during the night such that if she chooses herself at night, divorce does not occur. (MS) The command of that day becomes null because he explicitly mentioned two times: today and the day after tomorrow, and between them there is a time (tomorrow) that was not included in the command, so it is known that the intent of mentioning the second time is not extension of the first command, thus necessarily requiring another command. Since they are two commands, she has a choice in each of the two times; if she rejects on the day, it does not become null on the day after tomorrow. Zufar said that her choice after tomorrow also becomes null because they are a single command since the conjunction of one time to the other repeats the wording of the command, so it is one command as in the coming case, i.e., today and tomorrow, and like his statement: 'You are divorced today and the day after tomorrow.' We say: the command by hand admits of temporal fixation, so there is no need to include what was not included in the wording intentionally or incidentally, so they are two commands by necessity of separation, unlike divorce because it does not admit of temporal fixation, so it is possible for it to be described as one divorce in both today and the day after tomorrow, so there is no need to effect another divorce because the first continues until the second time. And unlike his statement 'today and tomorrow' as the difference will come that the command by hand admits of temporal fixation, so the first command stops and the second is made as an initial command. And from Abu Yusuf in al-Amali: that if he said to his wife: 'Your command is in your hand today, and your command is in your hand tomorrow' they are two commands such that if she chooses her husband today, then tomorrow comes, the command becomes in her hand, and this is correct because each of the two statements is independent, so there is no need for connection to what precedes. And it should be that there is no disagreement among them. And it branches from it that it is not permissible for her to choose herself at night. (Mustakhlas, Fath)
Source pages p. 426

Commentary
This passage from Kanz al-Daqa'iq, a foundational Hanafi text, discusses the ruling of 'amr bi yadik' (command by hand) in divorce. The author begins by stating a basic ruling: if a husband says to his wife 'Your command is in your hand' or 'Choose' with reference to one divorce, and she chooses herself, she is divorced by one revocable (raj'i) divorce. However, the commentary immediately clarifies that this is a summary; the detailed ruling is that it is actually an irrevocable (ba'in) divorce because such phrases are kinayah (allusive) and the wife only has the power to effect a ba'in divorce. There is a reported disagreement within the Hanafi school: some early texts state it is raj'i, but the majority (including al-Jami' al-Kabir, al-Mabsut, al-Ziyadat) hold it is ba'in. The key principle is that the consideration is given to the husband's delegation, not the wife's execution. So even if the wife uses explicit wording, the nature of the divorce depends on the husband's delegation. The passage then addresses an objection: if 'amr bi yadik' indicates ba'in, how can it be interpreted as raj'i? The response is that when the husband couples it with an explicit term like 'with one divorce', it is understood as raj'i because the explicit term specifies revocability. This is analogous to saying 'You are divorced irrevocably'—the word 'ba'in' makes it irrevocable. However, intention alone cannot change the nature of explicit divorce (i.e., a clear 'You are divorced' remains raj'i unless accompanied by a qualifying word). The passage then moves to a separate section (fasl) on the ruling when the husband intends three divorces. If the husband says 'Your command is in your hand' intending three, and the wife responds 'I have chosen myself with one divorce', only one divorce occurs (ba'in). This is because the wife's response specifies one divorce, and the husband's intention of three is not effective beyond what the wife's words allow. However, if the wife says 'I have chosen myself' without specifying, and the husband intended three, three divorces occur. This is because 'amr bi yadik' is a delegation (tamlik), which can bear the intention of three, unlike takhyeer (choice), which is a concession and cannot. The passage also discusses the condition that the wife must mention 'self' (nafs) in her response; otherwise, no divorce occurs. It then addresses temporal issues: if the husband says 'Your command is in your hand today and the day after tomorrow', the night between them is not included, so choosing at night does not count. If the wife rejects the command on the day (e.g., by exercising her choice to stay married), the command for that day becomes null. There is a dispute with Zufar who considers the two times as one continuous command, but the Hanafi position, supported by Abu Yusuf, is that they are separate commands because 'amr bi yadik' admits of temporal specification. The practical result is that the wife has a separate choice period for each day, and night is not included.

Key Terms
أمرك بيدك (amruki bi yadiki): A phrase used by the husband to delegate to his wife the power to divorce herself. It is a form of tamlik (delegation) and considered a kinayah (allusive) for divorce.
تمليك (tamlik): In this context, giving the wife ownership of the divorce decision, i.e., delegating to her the authority to divorce herself. It is the basis for the validity of 'amr bi yadik' and distinguishes it from takhyeer.
تخيير (takhyeer): The husband giving his wife the choice to choose herself (divorce) or stay. It is a concession (rukhsah) based on consensus, and unlike tamlik, the husband's intention for more than one divorce is not effective.
بائنة (ba'in): An irrevocable divorce that does not allow the husband to take his wife back without a new marriage. It is the result of 'amr bi yadik' according to the stronger Hanafi narration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A husband says to his wife 'Your command is in your hand' intending one divorce, and she responds 'I divorce myself.' According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, what is the nature of this divorce?
A) Revocable (raj'i)
B) Irrevocable (ba'in)
C) No divorce occurs because she did not mention 'myself'
D) Three divorces if the husband intended three

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi relied-upon position is that 'amr bi yadik' (command by hand) results in a ba'in (irrevocable) divorce because it is a kinayah that only conveys the power to separate completely.

Talaq — Divorce
intermediate
8 min
Intention of Three in 'Amr bi Yadik and Distinction from Takhyeer
Explaining the validity of intention of three in 'amr bi yadik and the distinction from takhyeer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tamlik and takhyeer in Hanafi divorce law and explain why intention of three is valid in 'amr bi yadik but not in takhyeer.

Core Idea
In tamlik intention of three is valid; in takhyeer it is not.

This lesson focuses on a key nuance: when a husband says 'Your command is in your hand' intending three divorces, and the wife responds specifying one divorce, only one occurs. But if she responds generally, three occur. This is because 'amr bi yadik' is a delegation (tamlik) which follows qiyas, unlike takhyeer which is a special concession. We will explore the proof and the difference.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between tamlik and takhyeer
Tamlik is delegation of the power to divorce; takhyeer is giving the wife a choice to divorce or stay. Takhyeer is based on consensus of the Companions and operates contrary to qiyas.
Tamlik is the basis for 'amr bi yadik'; takhyeer uses the word 'choose'.
2
Recognize that tamlik allows the husband's intention of three to take effect
Because tamlik is an act of ownership; the husband owns the right to divorce and can delegate as many as he owns. By qiyas and istihsan, his intention of three transfers if the wife does not limit her response.
If the wife specifies a number, her specification governs.
3
Understand that takhyeer is a special concession
Takhyeer operates contrary to qiyas and is limited to its textual basis (consensus of the Sahaba). Therefore, it cannot be extended by analogy, and intention of three is not effective.
If the husband intends three in takhyeer, only one divorce occurs.
4
Apply to the scenario
If husband says 'Your command is in your hand' intending three, and wife says 'I choose myself' without number, three occur. If wife says 'with one', only one occurs.
The wife's response must include the word 'self' (nafs) to be valid.
